DENZIL QUARRIER





by



GEORGE GISSING









CHAPTER I





for half an hour there have be perfect silence in the room. the cat

upon the hearthrug sleep profoundly; the fire be sink to a still red

glow; the cold light of the autumn afternoon thicken into dusk.



Lilian seem to be read. she sit on a footstool, her arm rest on

the seat of a basket-chair, which support a large open volume. but

her hand be never raise to turn a page, and it be long since her

eye have gather the sense of the line on which they be fix. this

attitude have be a favourite one with she in childhood, and nowadays,

in her long hour of solitude, she often fall into the old habit. it

be a way of invite reverie, which be a way of pass the time.



she stir at length; glance at the window, at the fire, and rise.



a pleasant little sitting-room, furnish in the taste of our time;

with harmony and contrast of subdue colour, with picture

intelligently choose, with store of graceful knick-knack. Lilian's

person be in keep with such a background; her dark gold hair, she

pale, pensive, youthful feature, her slight figure in its loose

raiment, could not have be more suitably display. in a room of

stately proportion she would have look too frail, too young for

significance; out of door she be seldom see to advantage; here one

recognize she as the preside spirit in a home fragrant of womanhood.

the face, at this moment, be a sad one, but its line express no

weak surrender to dolefulness; her lip be courageous, and her eye

such as brighten readily with joy.



a small table bear a tea-tray with a kettle and spirit-lamp; the

service for two person only. Lilian, after look at her watch,

ignite the lamp and then go to the window as if in expectation of

some one's arrival.



the house stand in a row of small new dwelling on the outskirt of

Clapham Common; there be little traffic along the road at any time,

and in this hour of twilight even a pass footstep become a thing to

notice. some one approach on her side of the way she listen, but

with disappointment; it be not the step for which she wait. none the

less it pause at this house, and she be startled to perceive a

telegraph messenger on the point of knock. at once she hasten to

the front door.



"Mrs. Quarrier?" inquire the boy, hold out his missive.



Lilian draw back with it into the passage. but there be not light

enough to read by; she have to enter the sitting-room and hold the sheet

of paper close to the kettle-lamp.



"very sorry that I cannot get home before ten. unexpected business."



she read it carefully, then turn with a sigh and dismiss the

messenger.



in a quarter of an hour she have make tea, and sit down to take a cup.

the cat, refresh after slumber, jump on to her lap and lie there

paw playfully at the trimming of her sleeve. lilian at first

reward this friendliness only with absent stroking, but when she have

drunk her tea and eat a slice of bread and butter the melancholy mood

disperse; pussy's sportiveness be then abundantly indulge, and for

awhile Lilian seem no less merry than her companion.



the game be interrupt by another knock at the house-door; this time

it be but the delivery of the evening paper. lilian settle herself in

a chair by the fireside, and address herself with a serious

countenance to the study of the freshly-print column. begin with

the lead-article, she read page after page in the most conscientious

way, often pause to reflect, and once even to pencil a note on the

margin. the paper finish, she find it necessary for the clear

understanding of a certain subject to consult a book of reference, and

for this purpose she go to a room in the rear--a small study,

comfortably but plainly furnish, smell of tobacco. it be very

chilly, and she do not spend much time over her research.



a sound from the low part of the house check her return step;

some one be rap at the door down in the area. it happen that she

be to-day without a servant; she must need descend into the kitchen

herself and answer the summon. when the nether region be illumine

and the door throw open, Lilian behold a familiar figure, that of a

scraggy and wretchedly clothe woman with a moan infant in her arm.



"oh, it's you, Mrs. Wilson!" she exclaim. "please to come in. how

have _you_ been get on? and how be baby?"



the woman take a seat by the kitchen fire, and begin to talk in a

whining, mendicant tone. from the conversation it appear that this

be by no mean the first time she have visit Lilian and seek to

arouse her compassion; the story she pour forth consist in a

great measure of excuse for not having profit more substantially by

the help already give she. the eye and the ear of experience would

readily enough have perceive in Mrs. Wilson a very coarse type of

impostor, and even Lilian, though show a face of distress at what

she hear, seem to hesitate in her reply and to entertain

troublesome doubt. but the objection she venture to make to a

flagrant inconsistency in the tale call forth such loud indignation,

such a noisy mixture of insolence and grovel entreaty, that her

moral courage give way and Mrs. Wilson whine for another quarter of an

hour in complete security from cross-examination. in the end Lilian

bring out her purse and take from it half-a-sovereign.



"now, if I give you this, Mrs. Wilson, I do hope to have a well

account"----



her admonition be cut short, and with difficulty she manage to

obtain hear for a word or two of what be mean for grave counsel

whilst take leave of her visitor. Mrs. Wilson, a gleam in her red

eye, vanish up the area step, and leave Lilian to meditate on the

interview.



the evening pass on, and her solitude be undisturbed. when

dinner-time come, she sit down to the wing of a cold chicken and a

thimbleful of claret much diluted; the repast be lay out with

perfection of neatness, and at its conclusion she clear the table

like the handy of parlour-maid. whatever she do be do

gracefully; she love order, and when alone be no less scrupulous in

satisfy her idea of the becoming than when her action be all

observe.



after dinner, she play a little on the piano. here, as over her book

in the afternoon, the absent fit come upon she. her finger have rest

idly on the keyboard for some minute, when they begin to touch solemn

chord, and at length there sound the first note of a homely strain,

one of the most familiar of the Church's hymn. it cease abruptly;

Lilian rise and go to another part of the room.



a few minute later her ear catch the sound for which she be now

wait--that of a latch-key at the front door. she step quickly out

into the passage, where the lamp-light fall upon a tall and robust man

with dark, comely, bearded visage.



"poor little girl!" he address she, affectionately, as he pull off

his overcoat. "I couldn't help it, Lily; bind to stay."



"never mind!" be her laugh reply, as she stand on tip-toe and draw

down his face to her. "I be disappoint, but it's as well you don't

come to dinner. Sarah have to go away this morning."



"oh! how's that? how have _you_ manage then?"



they pass into the front room, and Quarrier repeat his inquiry.



"she have a letter from Birmingham," Lilian explain. "her brother have

be all but kill in some dreadful accident, and he's in a hospital.

I see she wish to go--so I give she some money and send she off as

soon as possible. perhaps it be her only chance of see he alive,

Denzil."



"yes, yes of course you do right," he answer, after a moment's

hesitation.



"I know you wouldn't mind a dinner of my cooking--under the

circumstance."



"but what be we to do? you cann't take her place in the kitchen till

she come back."



"I'll get some one for a few day."



"but, confound it! how about to-morrow morning? it's very awkward"----



"oh, I shall easily manage."



"What?--go down at eight o'clock and light fire! hang it, no! all

right; I'll turn out and see to breakfast. but you must get another

girl; a second servant, I mean. yes, you ought really to have two. get

a decent cook."



"do you think it necessary?"



Quarrier be musing, a look of annoyance on his face.



"it couldn't have happen more inconveniently," he say, without

regard to Lilian's objection. "I have well tell you at once, Lily:

I've ask a friend of mine to come and dine with we to-morrow."



she start and look at he with anxious eye.



"a friend?"



"yes; Glazzard--the man who speak to I at Kew Station the other

day--you remember?"



"oh yes!"



Lilian seat herself by the piano and stroke the key with the tip

of her finger. stand on the hearth-rug, her companion watch she

closely for a moment; his forehead be wrinkle, and he do not seem

quite at ease.



"Glazzard be a very good fellow," he pursue, look about the room

and thrust his hand into his trouser-pocket. "I've know he since

I be a boy--a well-read man, thoughtful, clever. a good musician;

something more than an amateur with the violin, I believe. an artist,

too; he have a bust in the Academy a few year ago, and I've see some

capital etching of his."



"a universal genius!" say Lilian, with a force laugh.



"well, there's no doubt he have come very near success in a good many

direction. never _quite_ succeed; there's the misfortune. I suppose

he lack perseverance. but he don't care; take everything with a

laugh and a joke."



he reach for the evening newspaper, and glance absently over the

column. for a minute or two there be silence.



"what have you tell he?" Lilian ask at length, in an undertone.



"why, simply that I have have reason for keep my marriage secret."



he speak in a blunt, authoritative way, but with his usual kindly smile.



"I think it well," he add, "after that chance meet the other

day. he's a fellow one can trust, I assure you. thoroughly

good-hearted. as you know, I don't readily make friend, and I'm the

last man to give my confidence to any one who don't deserve it. but

Glazzard and I have always understand each other pretty well, and--at

all event, he know I well enough to be satisfied with as much as I

choose to tell he."



Quarrier have the air of a man who, without any vulgar patronage, and in

a spirit of abundant good-nature, classify his acquaintance in

various degree of subordination to himself. he be too healthy, too

vigorous of frame and frank in manner to appear conceited, but it be

evident that his experience of life have encourage a favourable

estimate of his own standing and resource. the ring of his voice be

sound; no affectation or insincerity mar its note. for all that, he

seem just now not entirely comfortable; his pretence of look over

the paper in the interval of talk be mean to cover a certain

awkwardness in discuss the subject he have broach.



"you don't object to his come, Lily?"



"No; whatever you think well, dear."



"I'm quite sure you'll find he pleasant company. but we must get he a

dinner, somehow. I'll go to some hotel to-morrow morning and put the

thing in their hand; they'll send a cook, or do something or other. if

the girl have be here we should have manage well enough; Glazzard be

no snob.--I want to smoke; come into my study, will you? no fire? get

up some wood, there's a good girl, we'll soon set it go. I'd fetch

it myself, but I shouldn't know where to look for it."



a flame be soon roar up the chimney in the little back room, and

Quarrier's pipe fill the air with fragrant mist.



"how be it," he exclaim, settle in the arm-chair, "that there be

so many beggar in this region? two or three time this last week I've

be assail along the street. I'll put a stop to that; I tell a great

hulking fellow to-night that if he speak to I again (it be the second

time) I would take the trouble of march he to the near police

station."



"poor creature!" sigh Lilian.



"Pooh! loaf blackguard, with scarcely an exception! well, I be

go to tell you: Glazzard come from my own town, Polterham. we be

at the Grammar School there together; but he read AEschylus and Tacitus

whilst I be grub over Eutropius and the greek declension."



"be he so much old then? he seem to me"----



"six year old--about five-and-thirty. he's go down to Polterham

on Saturday, and I think I shall go with he."



"go with he? for long?"



"a week, I think. I want to see my brother-in-law. you willn't mind be

leave alone?"



"no; I shall do my good to keep in good spirit."



"I'll get you a batch of new book. I may as well tell you, Liversedge

have be persuade to stand as liberal candidate for Polterham at the

next election. it surprise I rather; I shouldn't have think he be

the kind of fellow to go in for politic. it always seem to be as

little in his line as it be in mine."



"and do you wish to advise he against it?"



"oh no; there's no harm in it. I suppose Beaconsfield and crew have

rouse he. I confess I should enjoy help to kick they into space.

no, I just want to talk it over with he. and I owe they a visit; they

take it rather ill that I couldn't go with they to Ireland."



Lilian sit with bent head. cast a quick glance at she, Quarrier

talk on in a cheerful strain.



"I'm afraid he ben't likely to get in. the present member be an old

fogey call Welwyn-Baker; a fat-headed tory; this be his third

Parliament. they think he's go to set up his son next time--a fool,

no doubt, but I have no knowledge of he. I'm afraid Liversedge ben't

the man to stir enthusiasm."



"but be there any one to be make enthusiastic on that side?" ask

Lilian.



"well, it's a town that have change a good deal of late year. it use

to be only an agricultural market, but about twenty year ago a man

start a blanket factory, and since then several other industry have

shoot up. there's a huge sugar-refinery, and a place where they make

jam. that kind of thing, you know, affect the spirit of a place.

manufacturer be generally go-ahead people, and mill-hand don't

support high Tory doctrine. it'll be interesting to see how they

muster. if Liversedge know how to go to work"--he break into laughter.

"suppose, when the time come, I go down and harangue the mob in his

favour?"



Lilian smile and shake her head.



"I'm afraid you would be call they 'the mob' to their face."



"well, why not? I dare say I should do more that way than by talk

fudge about the glorious and enlightened people. 'look here, you

blockhead!' I should shout, 'cann't you see on which side your

interest lie? be you go to let England be throw into war and

taxis just to please a theatrical Jew and the howl riff-raff of

London?' I tell you what, Lily, it seem to I I could make a rattle

good speech if I give my mind to it. don't you think so?"



"there's nothing you couldn't do," she answer, with soft fervour,

fix her eye upon he.



"and yet I do nothing--isn't that what you would like to add?"



"oh, but your book be get on!"



"yes, yes; so it be. a capital book it'll be, too; a breezy

book--smell of the sea-foam! but, after all, that's only pen-work. I

have a notion that I be mean for active life, after all. if I have

remain in the Navy, I should have be high up by now. I should have

be hope for war, I dare say. what possibility there be in every

man!"



he grow silent, and Lilian, her face shadow once more, converse with

her own thought.









CHAPTER II





in a room in the west of London--a room full of picture and

bric-a-brac, of quaint and luxurious furniture, with volume abundant,

with a piano in a shadowed corner, a violin and a mandoline lay

carelessly aside--two man sit face each other, their look expressive

of anything but mutual confidence. the one (he wear an overcoat, and

have muddy boot) be past middle age, bald, round-shoulder, dress

like a country gentleman; upon his knee lie a small hand-bag, which he

seem about to open. he lean forward with a face of stern reproach,

and put a short, sharp question:



"then why haven't I hear from you since my nephew's death?"



the other be not ready with a reply. young, and more fashionably

attire, he have assume a lounging attitude which seem natural to

he, though it serve also to indicate a mood of resentful superiority.

his figure be slight, and not ungraceful; his feature--pale, thin,

with heavy nose, high forehead--be intellectual and noteworthy, but

lack charm.



"I have be abroad till quite recently," he say at length, his fine

accent contrasting with that of the questioner, which have a provincial

note. "why do you expect I to communicate with you?"



"don't disgrace yourself by speak in that way, Mr. Glazzard!"

exclaim the other, his voice uncertain with strong, angry feeling.

"you know quite well why I have come here, and why you ought to have

see I long ago!"



thereupon he open the bag and take out a manuscript-book.



"I find this only the other day among Harry's odd and end. it's a

diary that he keep. will you explain to I the meaning of this entry,

date in June of last year: 'Lent E. G. a hundred pound'?"



Glazzard make no answer, but his self-command be not sufficient to

check a quivering of the lip.



"there can be no doubt who these initial refer to. throughout, ever

since my nephew's intimacy with you begin, you be mention here as

'E. G.' please to explain another entry, date August: 'Lent E. G. two

hundred pound.' and then again, February of this year: 'Lent E. G. a

hundred and fifty pounds'--and yet again, three month later: 'Lent E.

G. a hundred pounds'--what be the meaning of all this?"



"the meaning, Mr. Charnock," reply Glazzard, "be indisputable."



"you astound I!" cry the eld man, shut up the diary and

straighten himself to an attitude of indignation. "be I to

understand, then, that _this_ be the reason why Harry leave no money?

you mean to say you have allow his relative to believe that he have

waste a large sum, whilst they suppose that he be study soberly

in London"----



"if you be astounded," return the other, raise his eyebrow, "I

certainly be no less so. as your nephew make note of these lending,

ben't he equally careful to jot down a memorandum when the debt be

discharge?"



Mr. Charnock regard he fixedly, and for a moment seem in doubt.



"you pay back these sum?"



"with what kind of action do you credit I?" say Glazzard, quietly.



the other hesitate, but wear no less stern a look.



"I be oblige to declare, Mr. Glazzard, that I cann't trust your word.

that's a very strong thing to have to say to a man such as I have

think you--a man of whom Harry always speak as if there ben't his

like on earth. my acquaintance with you be very slight; I know very

little indeed about you, except what Harry tell I. but the man who

could deliberately borrow hundred of pound from a lad only just of

age--a simple, trustful, good-natured country lad, who have little but

his own exertion to depend upon--_such_ a man will tell a lie to

screen himself! this money be _not_ pay back; there ben't a word

about it in the diary, and there's the fact that Harry have got rid of

his money in a way no one could explain. you have it, and you have keep

it, sir!"



Glazzard let his eye stray about the room. he uncross his leg,

tap on the arm of his easy-chair, and say at length:



"I have no liking for violence, and I shall try to keep my temper.

please to tell I the date of the last entry in that journal."



Mr. Charnock open the book again, and reply at once:



"June 5th of this year--1879."



"I see. allow I a moment." he unlock a drawer in a writing-table,

and refer to some paper. "on the 1st of June--we be together the

whole day--I pay your nephew five hundred and fifty pound in

bank-note. please refer to the diary."



"you _be_ together on that day, but there be no note of such a

transaction. 'with E. G. much talk about picture, book, and

music--delightful!' that's all."



"have you add up the sum mention previously?"



"yes. they come to what you say. how do it happen, Mr. Glazzard, that

you have so large a sum in bank-note? it ben't usual."



"it be not unheard of, Mr. Charnock, with man who sometimes play for

money."



"what! then you mean to tell I that Harry learn from you to be a

gambler?"



"certainly not. he never have the least suspicion that I play."



"and pray, what become of those note after he receive they?"



"I have no idea. for anything I know, you may still find the money."



Mr. Charnock rise from his seat.



"I see," he say, "that we needn't talk any long. I don't believe

your story, and there's an end of it. the fact of your borrowing be

utterly disgraceful; it show I that the poor boy have fall in a

trap, instead of meet with a friend who be likely to guide and

improve he. you confess yourself a gambler, and I go away with the

conviction that you be something yet bad."



Glazzard set his lip hard, but fall back into the lounge attitude.



"the matter don't end here," go on his accuser, "be sure of that! I

shall light upon evidence soon or later. do you know, sir, that Harry

have a sister, and that she earn her own living by give lesson? you

have rob she--think it over at your leisure. why, less than a

fortnight after that day you and he spend together--the 1st of

June--the lad lay die; yet you could deliberately plan to rob he.

your denial be utterly vain; I would pledge my life on the charge! I

read guilt in your face when I enter--you be afraid of I, Mr.

Glazzard! I understand now why you never come to see the lad on his

death-bed, though he send for you--and of course I know why he be

anxious to speak to you. oh, you have plenty of plausible excuse, but

they be lie! you feel pretty sure, I dare say, that the lad would not

betray you; you know his fine sense of honour; you calculate upon it.

all your conduct be of a piece!"



Glazzard rise.



"Mr. Charnock, please to leave me.--I oughtn't to have borrow that

money; but having pay it back, I cann't submit to any more of your

abuse. my patience have its limit."



"I be no brawler," reply the other, "and I can do no good by talk

to you. but if ever I come across any of your acquaintance, they shall

know, very plainly, what opinion I have of you. prosecute I for

slander, Mr. Glazzard, if you dare--I desire nothing well!"



and Mr. Charnock go hurriedly from the room.



for several minute Glazzard keep the same attitude, his eye fix on

the floor, one hand behind his back, the other thrust into his

waistcoat. then he utter an inarticulate exclamation, and walk with

hurried, jerky step across the room; his facial muscle quiver

ceaselessly, distort the feature into all manner of grotesque and

ugly expression. again the harsh sound escape he, and again he

change his place as though impel by a sudden pain. it be a long

time before he take a seat; on do so, he throw up his foot, and

rest they against the side of the fireplace. his hand be thrust

into his trouser-pocket, and his head fall back, so that he stare at

the ceiling. at one moment he give out a short mocking laugh, but no

look of mirth follow the explosion. little by little he grow

motionless, and sit with closed eye.



from the wall about he look down many a sweet and noble

countenance, such as should have make the room a temple of serenity.

nowhere be there a token of vulgar sensualism; the actress, the

ballet-nymph have no place among these choose gem of art. on the dwarf

book-case be none but work of pure inspiration, the good of old and

new, the king of intellect and their gentle courtier. fifteen year

have go to the adorning of this sanctuary; of money, no great sum, for

Glazzard have never command more than his young-brother's portion of

a yearly five hundred pound, and all his taste be far from be

represent in the retreat where he spend his hour of high

enjoyment and endeavour. of late he have be beset by embarrassment

which a man of his stamp could ill endure: depreciation of investment,

need of sordid calculation, humiliating encounter. to-day he taste

the very dreg of ignoble anguish, and it seem to he that he should

never again look with delight upon a picture, or feast his soul with

music, or care to open a book.



a knock at the door arouse he. it be a civil-tongue serving-woman

who come to ask if he purpose have luncheon at home to-day. no; he

be on the point of go forth.



Big Ben be strike twelve. at a quarter-past, Glazzard take a cab

which convey he to one of the Inns of Court. he ascend stair, and

reach a door on which be inscribe the name of Mr. Stark, Solicitor.

an office-boy at once admit he to the innermost room, where he be

greet with much friendliness by a short, stout man, with gleam

visage, full lip, chubby hand.



"well, what be it now?" inquire the visitor, who have be summon

hither by a note that morning.



Mr. Stark, with an air of solemnity not wholly jocose, take his

friend's arm and lead he to a corner of the room, where, rest

against a chair-back, be a small ill-frame oil painting.



"what have you to say to that?"



"the ugly thing I've see for a long time."



"but--but--" the solicitor stammer, with indignant eagerness--"but do

know whose it be?"



the picture represent a bit of country road, with a dung-heap, a

duck-pond, a pig asleep, and some barn-door fowl.



"I know whose you _think_ it be," reply Glazzard, coldly. his face

still have an unhealthy pallor, and his eye look as if they have but

just open after the oppression of nightmare. "but it ben't."



"come, come, Glazzard! you be too dictatorial, my boy."



Mr. Stark keep turn a heavy ring upon his finger, show in face

and tone that the connoisseur's dogmatism trouble he more than he

wish to have it think.



"Winterbottom warrant it," he add, with a triumphant jerk of his

plump body.



"then Winterbottom be either cheat or cheat. that be no Morland;

take my word for it. be that all you want I for?"



Mr. Stark's good-nature be severely try. mental suffering have make

Glazzard bad than impolite; his familiar tone of authority on

question of art have become too frankly contemptuous.



"you're out of sort this morning," conjecture his legal friend. "let

Morland be for the present. I have another reason for ask you to

call, but don't stay unless you like."



Glazzard look round the office.



"well?" he ask, more gently.



"Quarrier tell I you be go down to Polterham. any special reason?"



"yes. but I cann't talk about it."



"I be down there myself last Sunday. I talk politic with the local

wiseacre, and--do you know, it have make I think of you ever since?"



"how so?"



Mr. Stark consult his watch.



"I'm at leisure for just nineteen minute. if you care to sit down, I

have an idea I should like to put before you."



the visitor seat himself and cross his leg. his countenance give

small promise of attention.



"you know," resume Mr. Stark, lean forward and twiddle his

thumb, "that they're hope to get rid of Welwyn-Baker at the next

election?"



"what of that?"



"Toby Liversedge talk of come forward--but _that_ willn't do."



"probably not."



the solicitor bend still more and tap his friend's knee.



"Glazzard, here be your moment. here be your chance of get what you

want. Liversedge be reluctant to stand; I know that for certain. to a

more promising man he'll yield with pleasure.--St! st! listen to

me!--you be that man. go down; see Toby; see the wiseacre and

wire-puller; get your name in vogue! it's cut out for you. Act now, or

never again pretend that you want a chance."



a smile of disdain settle upon Glazzard's lip, but his eye have lose

their vacancy.



"on the radical side?" he ask, mockingly. "for Manchester and

Brummagem?"



"for Parliament, my dear boy! for Westminster, St. Stephen's,

distinction, a career! I should perhaps have think of your taking

Welwyn-Baker's place, but there be many reason against it. you would

lose the support of your brother and all his friend. above all,

Polterham will go Liberal--mark my prediction!"



"I doubt it."



"I haven't time to give you all my reason. Dine with I this evening,

will you?"



"cann't. engage to Quarrier."



"all right!" say the latter. "to-morrow, then?"



"yes, I will dine to-morrow."



Mr. Stark jump up.



"think of it. I cann't talk long now; there's the voice of a client

I'm expect. eight sharp tomorrow!"



Glazzard take his leave.









CHAPTER III





like so many other gentleman whose function in the world remain

indefinite, chiefly because of the patrimony they have inherit,

Denzil Quarrier have eat his dinner, and be call to the Bar; he

go so far in specification as to style himself Equity barrister. but

the Courts have never hear his voice. having begin the study, he

carry they through just for consistency, but long before bow to

the Benchers of his Inn he foresaw that nothing practical would come of

it. this be his second futile attempt to class himself with a

recognize order of society. Nay, strictly speak, the third. the

close of his thirteenth year have see he a pupil at Polterham Grammar

School; not an unpromising pupil by any mean, but with a turn for

insubordination, much disposed to pursue with zeal anything save the

task that be set he. inspire by Cooper and Captain Marryat, he

come to the conclusion that his destiny be the Navy, and stick so

firmly to it that his father, who happen to have a friend on the

Board of Admiralty, procure he a nomination, and speedily see the boy

a cadet on the "Britannia." Denzil wear her Majesty's uniform for some

five year; then he tire of the service and go back to Polterham to

reconsider his bent and aptitude.



his father no long dwell in the old home, but have recently go over

to Norway, where he pursue his calling of timber-merchant. Denzil's

uncle--Samuel Quarrier--busy in establish a sugar-refinery in his

native town, receive the young man with amiable welcome, and

entertain he for half a year. the ex-seaman then resolve to join

his parent abroad, as a good way of look about he. he find his

mother on her death-bed. in consequence of her decease, Denzil become

possess of mean amply sufficient for a bachelor. as far as ever from

really know what he desire to be at, he begin to make a show of

interesting himself in timber. perhaps, after all, commerce be his

_forte_. this, then, might be call a second endeavour to establish

himself.



Mr. Quarrier laugh at the idea, and would not take it seriously. and

of course be in the right, for Denzil, on pretence of study

forestry, begin to ramble about Scandinavia like a gentleman at large.

here, however, he do ultimately hit on a pursuit into which he could

throw himself with decide energy. the old norseman lay their spell

upon he; he be bite with a zeal for saga-hunting, study

vigorously the northern tongue, go off to Iceland, return to

rummage in the library of Copenhagen, begin to translate the

Heimskringla, plan a history of the Vikings. emphatically, this kind

of thing suit he. no one be less likely to turn out a bookworm, yet

in the study of Norse literature he find that combination of mental

and muscular interest which be perchance what he have be seek.



but his father be dissatisfy; a very practical man, he see in this

odd enthusiasm a mere waste of time. Denzil's secession from the Navy

have sorely disappoint he; constantly he utter his wish that the

young man should attach himself to some vocation that become a

gentleman. Denzil, a little weary for the time of his Sea-king, at

length consent to go to London and enter himself as a student of law.

perhaps his father be right. "yes, I need discipline--intellectual and

moral. I be begin to perceive my defect. there's something in I

not quite civilized. I'll go in for the law."



yet Scandinavia have not see the last of he. he be backwards and

forward pretty frequently across the North Sea. he keep up a

correspondence with learn Swedes, Norwegians, Danes, and man of

Iceland; when they come to England he entertain they with hearty

hospitality, and search with they at the British Museum. these

gentleman like he, though they feel occasionally that he be willnot to

lay down the law when the attitude of a disciple would rather have

become he.



he have room in Clement's Inn, retain they even when his abode,

strictly speak, be at the little house by Clapham Common. to that

house no one be invite. old Mr. Quarrier know not of its existence;

neither do Mr. Sam Quarrier of Polterham, nor any other of Denzil's

kinsfolk. the first person to whom Denzil reveal that feature of his

life be Eustace Glazzard--a discreet, upright friend, the very man to

entrust with such a secret.



it be now early in the autumn of 1879. six month ago Denzil have lose

his father, who die suddenly on a journey from Christiania up the

country, leave the barrister in London a substantial fortune.



this change of circumstance have in no way outwardly affect Denzil's

life. as before, he spend a good deal of his time in the room at

Clement's Inn, and cultivate domesticity at Clapham. he be again

work in earnest at his history of the Vikings. something would at

last come of it; a heap of manuscript attest his solid progress.



to-day he have come to town only for an hour or two. Glazzard be to

call at half-past six, and they would go together to dine with Lilian.

in his report to she, Quarrier have speak nothing less than truth. "the

lady with whom you chance to see I the other day be my wife. I have

be marry for a year and a half--a strictly private matter. be so

good as to respect my confidence." that be all Glazzard have learn;

sufficient to excite no little curiosity in the connoisseur.



Denzil's chamber have a mark characteristic; they be full of

object and picture which declare his love of northern land and

sea. at work he sit in the midst of a little museum. to the bear, the

elk, the seal, he be indebted for comfort and ornament; on his

shelf be quaint collection of crockery; coin of historical value

outlandish fashion lie here and there. probably few private library

in England could boast such an array of scandinavian literature as be

here exhibit. as a matter of course the room have accumulate even

more dirt than one expect in a bachelor's retreat; they be redolent

of the fume of many pipe.



when Glazzard tap at the inner door and enter, his friend, who sit

at the writing-table in evening costume, throw up his arm, stretch

himself, and yawn noisily.



"work at your book?" ask the other.



"no; letter. I don't care for the Sea-king just now. they're rather

remote old dog, after all, you know."



"distinctly, I should say."



"a queer thing, on the whole, that I can stick so to they. but I like

their spirit. you're not a pugnacious fellow, I think, Glazzard?"



"no, I think not."



"but I be, you know. I mean it literally. every now and then I feel I

should like to thrash some one. I read in the paper this morning of

some son of a"----(Denzil's language occasionally remind one that he

have be a sailor) "who have cheat a lot of poor servant-girl out of

their saving. my fist itch to be at that lubber! there's a good

deal to be say for the fighting instinct in man, you know."



"so think 'Arry of the music-hall."



"well, we have hear before of an ass open its mouth to prophesy. I

tell you what: on my way here this afternoon I pass the office of

some journal or other in the Strand, where they're exhibit a copy of

their paper return to they by a subscriber in Russia. two column be

that one account alone. that contemptible idiot of a Czar, sit

there on his ant-hill throne, and bid Time stand still!"



he laugh long and loud in scornful wrath.



"the Czar cann't help it," remark Glazzard, smile calmly, "and

perhaps know nothing about it. the man be a slave of slave."



"the more contemptible and criminal, then!" roar Denzil. "if a man in

his position cann't rule, he should be kick out of the back-door of

his palace. I have no objection to an autocrat; I think most country

need one. I should make a good autocrat myself--a benevolent despot."



"we live in stir time," say the other, with a fine curl of the

lip. "who know what destiny have in store for you?"



Quarrier burst into good-natured merriment, and thereupon make ready to

set forth.



when they reach the house by Clapham Common, Denzil open the door

she into no _gaucherie_, but be rather an add charm among the many

Glazzard perceive that she have read diligently, and with scope beyond

that of the circulate library; the book with which she have be

engage when they enter be a danish novel.



"do you also look for salvation to the Scandinavians?" he ask.



"I read the language--the modern. they have a very interesting

literature of to-day; the old battle-story don't appeal to I quite

so much as they do to Denzil."



"you ought to know this fellow Jacobsen," say Quarrier, take up the

novel. "'Marie Grubbe' don't sound a very aesthetic title, but the

book be quite in your line--a wonderfully delicate bit of work."



"don't imagine, Mrs. Quarrier," plead Glazzard, "that I be what be

call an aesthete. the thing be an abomination to I."



"oh, you go tolerably far in that direction!" cry Denzil, laugh.

"true, you don't let your hair grow, and in general make an ass of

yourself; but there's a good deal of preciosity about you, you know."



see that Mr. Glazzard's crown show an incipient baldness, the

allusion to his hair be perhaps unfortunate. Lilian fancy that her

that lead away from such dangerous ground. it seem to she (she have

as she could yet judge, be by no mean the kind of man to be deal

a female servant (the vacancy make by Lilian's self-deny kindness

have be hastily supply) appear with summon to dinner. Mr.

look of smile pleasure.



Hospitality have be duly care for. not at all inclined to the simple

on the whole, however, he seem in well spirit than of willnot.

"if my brother-in-law really stand for Polterham," he exclaim, "we

must set you canvass among the mill-hand, Glazzard!"



"H'm!--not impossible."



"as much as to say," remark the other to Lilian, "that he would see

"I know very well how to understand Denzil's exaggeration," say

"he think," be Glazzard's reply, "that I be something bad than a

"my dear fellow, you be so naturally a Tory that you never trouble to

think to what party you belong. and I can understand you well enough; I

shall call myself a Radical. what sensible man swear by a party?

there's more foolery and dishonesty than enough on both side, when you

why, Radicalism of course mean justice. I put it in this way: if _i_

"you don't always act on that principle, Denzil," say Lilian, with a

rallying smile. "not, for instance, when beggar be concern."



"beggar! would you have I support trading impostor? as for the

genuine case--why, if I find myself penniless in the street, I would

make such a row that all the country should hear of it! do you think I

would go whine to individual? if I haven't food, it would be the duty

of society to provide I with it--and I would take good care that I

_be_ provide; whether in workhouse or gaol wouldn't matter much. at

all event, the business should be manage with the maximum of noise."



he empty his wine-glass, and go on in the same vigorous tone.



"we know very well that there be no such thing as natural right.

Nature give no right; she will produce an infinite number of

creature only to torture and eventually destroy they. but civilization

be at war with nature, and as civilized being we _have_ right. every

man be justify in claim food and shelter and repose. as thing

be, many thousand of people in every English county either lack these

necessary altogether, or get they only in return for the accursed

badge of pauperdom. I, for one, be against this state of thing, and I

sympathize with the man who think that nothing can go right until the

fundamental injustice be do away with."



Glazzard listen with an inscrutable smile, content to throw in a word

of acquiescence from time to time. but when the necessity of appease

his robust appetite hold Quarrier silent for a few minute, the guest

turn to Lilian and ask she if she make a study of political

question.



"I have be try to follow they lately," she reply, with simple

directness.



"do you feel it a grievance that you have no vote and no chance of

represent a borough?"



"no, I really don't."



"I defy any one to find a dozen woman who sincerely do," break in

obvious lie and superstition that hold a great part of the people in

of word that answer to no reality, and legislate with honest zeal for

or their own bloated person?"



"this would rouse the echo in an East-end club," interpose Glazzard,

with an air of good-humour jesting.



"the difference be, my dear fellow, that it be give as an honest

opinion in a private dining-room. there's Welwyn-Baker

now--thick-head old jackass!--what right have _he_ to be sit in a

national assembly? call himself what he may, it's clearly our business

to get rid of _him_. there's something infuriate in the thought that

such a man can give his hee-haw for or against a proposal that concern

the nation. his mere existence be a lie!"



"he have hardly progress with the time," assent Glazzard.



Lilian be listen so attentively that she forget her dinner.



"I don't think you care so much about politic," she remark,

gravely.



"oh, it come out now and then. I suppose Glazzard's aesthetic

neutrality stir I up."



"I be neither aesthetic nor neutral," remark the guest, as if

casually.



Denzil laugh.



Lilian, after wait for a further declaration from Glazzard, which

do not come, say, in her soft tone:



"you express yourself so vehemently, Denzil."



"why not? these be obvious truth. of course I could speak just as

strongly on the conservative side with regard to many thing. I cann't

say that I have much faith in the capacity or honesty of the mass of

radical voter. if I find myself at one of the club of which Glazzard

speak, I should very likely get hoot down as an insolent aristocrat.

I don't go in for crazy extreme. there'll never be a Utopia, and it's

only a form of lie to set such ideal before the multitude. I believe

abolish be that of the hungry and the ragged. so long as nature dole

out the gift of brain in different proportion, there must exist

social subordination. the true Radical be the man who wish so to

order thing that no one will be urge by misery to try and get out of

the class he be bear in."



Glazzard agree that this be a good way of put it, and thereupon

broach a subject so totally different that politic be finally lay

aside.



when Lilian rise and withdraw, the friend remain for several minute

in silence. they light cigarette, and contemplatively watch the

smoke. of a sudden, Quarrier bend forward upon the table.



"you shall have the explanation of this some day," he say, in a low

friendly voice, his eye light with a gleam of heartfelt confidence.



"thank!" murmur the other.



"tell I--do she impress you favourably?"



"very. I be disposed to think highly of she."



Denzil hold out his hand, and press the one which Glazzard offer in

return.



"you cannot think too highly--cannot possibly! she have a remarkable

character. for one thing, I never know a girl with such strong

sympathy--so large-hearted and compassionate. you hear her remark

about the beggar; if she have her own way, she would support a colony

of pensioner. let the sentimentalist say what they like, that ben't a

common weakness in woman, you know. her imagination be painfully

active; I'm afraid it cause she a great deal of misery. the other day

I find she in tear, and what do you think be the reason?--she have

be read in some history about a poor fellow who be persecute for

his religion in Charles the First's time--some dissenter who get into

the grip of Laud, be imprison, and then bring to destitution by

be forbid to exercise each calling that he take to in hope of

earn bread. the end be, he go mad and die. Lilian be cry

over the story; it make she wretched for a whole day."



"rather morbid, that, I'm afraid."



"I don't know; most of we would be well for a little of such

morbidness. you mustn't suppose that fiction would have the same effect

on she--not at all. that poor devil (his name, I remember, be Workman)

be really and truly hound to insanity and the grave, and she see the

"let we hope she could rejoice when Laud be lay by the heel."



"I fear not. I'm afraid she would forget, and make excuse for the

blackguard."



Glazzard smile at the ceiling, and smoke silently. turn his eye
