honest face, then ask:



"how old be she?"



"just one-and-twenty."



"I should have think young."



nothing more be say of Lilian, and very soon they go to the room

where she await they.



"I know you be a musician, Mr. Glazzard," say Lilian before long.

"will you let I have the pleasure of hear you play something?"



"some enemy hath do this," the guest make reply, look towards

Denzil.



but without further protest he go to the piano and play two or

three short piece. any one with more technical knowledge than the

hearer would have perceive that he be do his good. as it be,

Lilian frequently turn to Denzil with a look of intense delight.



"Glazzard," exclaim his friend at length, "it puzzle I how such a

lazy fellow as you be have manage to do so much in so many direction."



the musician laugh carelessly, and, not deign any other reply,

go to talk with his hostess.









CHAPTER IV





the Polterham Literary Institute be a "hot-bed of Radicalism." for the

last year or two this have be generally understand. originate in the

editorial column of the _Polterham Mercury_, the remark be now a

commonplace on the lip of good conservative, and the liberal

themselves be not unwilling to smile an admission of its truth. at

the founding of the Institute no such thing be foresee; but in 1859

Polterham be hardly conscious of the stirring of that new life which,

in the course of twenty year, be to transform the town. in those day

a traveller descend the slope of the Banwell Hills seek out the

slim spire of Polterham parish church amid a tract of woodland, mead

and tillage; now the site of the thriving little borough be but too

distinctly mark by trail of smoke from several gaunt chimney--that

of Messrs. Dimes & Nevison's blanket-factory, that of Quarrier & Son's

sugar-refinery, and, high still (say, indeed, to be one of the

tall chimney in England), that of Thomas & Liversedge's soap-work.

with the character of Polterham itself, the Literary Institute have

suffer a noteworthy change. ostensibly it remain non-political: a

library, reading-room and lecture-hall, for the benefit of all the

townsfolk; but by a subtle process the executive authority have pass

into the hand of new man with new idea. a mere enumeration of the

committee suffice to frighten away all who hold by Church, State, and

Mr. Welwyn-Baker: the Institute be no long an Institute, but a

"hot-bed."



how could respectable people make use of a library which admit work

of irreligious and immoral tendency? it be an undoubted fact (the

_mercury_ make it know) that of late there have be add to the

catalogue not only the "essay of David Hume" and that notorious book

Buckle's "history of Civilization," but even a large collection of the

writing of George Sand and Balzac--these latter in the original

tongue; for who, indeed, would ever venture to publish an english

translation? as for the reading-room, be it not characterization

enough to state that two Sunday newspaper, reek fresh from Fleet

Street, regularly appear on the table? what possibility of peruse

the _Standard_ or the _spectator_ in such an atmosphere? it be clear

that the supporter of law and decency must bestir themselves to

establish a new Society. Mr. Mumbray, long prominent in the municipal

and political life of the town, have already make the generous offer of

a large house at a low rental--one of the ancient building which have

be spoil for family residence by the erection of a mill close by.

the revered Member for the borough be willing to start the new library

with a gift of one hundred volume of "sterling literature." with

dissolution of Parliament in view, not a day should be lose in

establish this centre of intellectual life for right-thinking

inhabitant. it be a strange thing, a very strange thing indeed, that

interloper should have be permit to oust the wealth and

reputability of Polterham from an Institute which ought to have be

one of the bulwark of Conservatism. laxity in the original

constitution, and a spirit of supine confidence, have lead to this sad

result. it seem impossible that Polterham could ever fall from its

honourable position among the conservative stronghold of the country;

but the time be corrupt, a revolutionary miasma be spread to

every corner of the land. Polterham must no long repose in the

security of conscious virtue, for if it _do_ happen that, at the

come election, the unprincipled multitude even come near to achieve

a triumph, oh what a fall be there!



thus speak the _Mercury_. and in the same week Mr. Mumbray's vacant

house be secure by a provisional committee on behalf of the Polterham

Constitutional Literary Society.



the fine old crust party have some reason for their alarm. since

Polterham be a borough it have return a Tory Member as a matter of

course. political organization be quite unknown to the supporter of

Mr. Welwyn-Baker; such trouble have never seem necessary. through the

anxious year of 1868 Mr. Welwyn-Baker sit firm as a rock; an endeavour

to unseat he end amid contemptuous laughter. in 1874 the high-tide

of Toryism cause only a slight increase of congratulatory gurgling in

the Polterham backwater; the triumphant party hardly care to notice

that a liberal candidate have score an unprecedented proportion of

vote. Welwyn-Baker sit on, stolidly oblivious of the change that be

affect his constituency, deny indeed the possibility of mutation

in human thing. yet even now the Literary Institute be pass into

the hand of people who aim at make it something more than a place

where retire tradesman could play draught and doze over _good word_;

already have offensive volume find harbourage on the shelf, and

revolutionary periodical be introduce into the reading-room. from

time to time the _Mercury_ utter a note of warning, of protest, but

with no echo from the respectable middle-class abode where Polterham

Conservatism doze in self-satisfaction. it need another five year

of liberal activity throughout the borough to awaken the good people

whose influence have seem unassailable, and to set they uttering

sleepy snort of indignation. but the _Mercury_ have a new editor, a man

who be determined to gain journalistic credit by make a good fight

in a desperate cause. Mr. Mumbray, who hold the post of Mayor, have at

length learn that even in municipal matter the old order be

threaten; on the Town Council be several man who give a great deal

of trouble, and who openly boast that in a very short time all the

affair of the town would be manage by member of the Progressive

party. if so, farewell public morality! farewell religion!



the reading-room of the Literary Institute hear many an animate

conversation among the zealous partisan who hope great thing from

the approach contest. the talker be not man of recognize

standing, the manufacturer and landowner whose influence be of most

importance--for these personage be seldom see at the Institute; but

certain "small" people, fidgety, or effervescent, or enthusiastic,

eager to hear their own voice raise in declamation, and to get speak

of in the town as representative of public opinion. such a group have

gather early one afternoon in this month of October. the hour be

unusual, for between one o'clock and four the reading-room be

generally abandon to a few very quiet, somnolent person; but to-day

an exciting piece of news have get about in Polterham, and two or three

ardent politician hasten from their dinner-table to discuss the

situation with Mr. Wykes, secretary of the Institute, or any one else

who might present himself. it be report that Mr. Welwyn-Baker have

have a seizure of some kind, and that he lie in a dangerous state at his

house just outside the town.



"it's perfectly true," affirm Mr. Wykes. "I see Dr. Staple on his way

there. he'll never survive it. we shall have a bye-election--the very

last thing desirable."



the Secretary be a man of intelligent feature but painfully

distorted body; his right leg, permanently bent double, be support

at the knee by metal mechanism, and his arm on the opposite side end

at the elbow. none the less he move with much activity, gesticulate

frequently with the normal arm, and seem always to be in excellent

spirit. he be a Cambridge graduate, but have never be able to make

much use of his education and ability; having reach middle age, and

find himself without resource, he be glad to accept this post at

the Institute.



about he stand three Polterham worthy: Mr. Chown, draper, a member

of the Corporation; Mr. Vawdrey, coal-merchant; and Mr. Murgatroyd,

dentist. the draper--tall, bearded, with goggle eye and prominent

cheek-bone--have just rush in; as soon as Mr. Wykes have speak, he

exclaim in a hard, positive voice:



"it's nothing! it's nothing! I have it on the good assurance that it

be only a fall over a footstool. muscle strain--a bruise or

two--nothing bad."



"I'm very glad to hear it, on every ground," say wyke. "but even if

that be quite correct, it'll be a warning. a fall at that age generally

date the beginning of decrepitude. he willn't come forward again--I'm

convince he willn't."



"let we hope they'll be foolish enough to set up his son," remark Mr.

Vawdrey, in deep tone, which harmonize with his broad, stunted body

and lower visage. "it'll be their ruin."



Mr. Wykes agree.



"the waverer can hardly doubt--between Tobias Liversedge and Hugh

Welwyn-Baker."



"Bear in mind," ring Mr. Chown's brassy voice, "that it's by no mean

certain Liversedge be to be our candidate. I be in a position to assure

you that many of our most reliable man be not at all satisfied with

that choice--not at all satisfied. I don't mind go so far as to

declare that I share this dissatisfaction."



"really," put in Mr. Murgatroyd, the dentist, "it's rather late in the

day, Mr. Chown"----



his accent of studious moderation be interrupt by a shout from the

dogmatic draper.



"late? late? I consider that nothing whatever have be decide. I

protest--I protest, most emphatically, against any attempt to force a

candidate on the advanced section of the liberal party! I will even go

so far as to say--purely on my own responsibility--that the advanced

section of the Liberal party be the _essence_ of the liberal party, and

must be recognize as such, if we be to fight this campaign in union.

I personally--I speak for myself--do _not_ feel prepared to vote for

Tobias Liversedge. I say it boldly, care not who may report my word.

I compromise no man, and no body of man; but my view be that, if we be

to win the next election against the Tory candidate, it must be with

the help, and in the name, of a _radical_ candidate!"



at the close of each period Mr. Chown raise his hand and make it

vibrate in the air, his head vibrate in company therewith. his eye

glare, and his beard wag up and down.



"speak as an individual," reply Mr. Murgatroyd, who, among other

sign of nervousness, have the habit of constantly pull down his

waistcoat, "I cann't say that I should regret to be call upon to vote

for a really advanced man. but I may say--I really must say--and I

think Mr. Wykes will support I--I think Mr. Vawdrey will bear I

out--that it wouldn't be easy to find a candidate who would unite all

suffrage in the way that Mr. Liversedge do. we have to remember"----



"well," break in the coal-merchant, with his muffled bass, "if any one

care to know what I think, I should say that we want a local man, a

popular man, and a christian man. I don't know whom you would set up in

preference to Liversedge; but Liversedge suit I well enough. if the

Tories be go to put forward such a speciman as Hugh Welwyn-Baker, a

gambler, a drinker, and a profligate, I don't know, I say, who would

look well oppose to he than Toby Liversedge."



Mr. Chown could not restrain himself.



"I fail altogether to see what Christianity have to do with politic!

Christianity be all very well, but where will you find it? Old

Welwyn-Baker call himself a Christian, and so do his son. and I

suppose the Rev. Scatchard Vialls call himself a Christian! let we

have do with this disgusting hypocrisy! I say with all

deliberation--I affirm it--that Radicalism must break with religion

that have become a sham! radicalism be a religion in itself. we have no

right--no right, I say--to impose any such test as Mr. Vawdrey insist

upon!"



"I willn't quarrel about name," return Vawdrey, stolidly, "what I

mean to say be that we must have a man of clean life, a moral man."



"and do you imply," cry Chown, "that such man be hard to find among

radical?"



"I rather think they're hard to find anywhere nowadays."



Mr. Wykes have make a gesture request attention, and be about to

speak, when a boy come up to he and hold out a telegram.



"what's this?" murmur the Secretary, as he open the envelope.

"well, well, how very annoying! our lecturer of to-morrow evening cann't

possibly keep his engagement. no reason give; say he will write."



"another blank evening!" exclaim Chown. "this be most unsatisfactory,

I must say."



"we must fill it up," reply the Secretary. "I have an idea; it

connect with something I be on the point of say." he look round

the room cautiously, but see only a young lad bend over an illustrate

paper. "there be some one," he continue, subdue his voice, "who

might possibly be willing to stand if Mr. Liversedge ben't finally

adopt as our candidate--some one who, in my opinion, would suit we

very well indeed. I be think of young Mr. Quarrier, Liversedge's

brother-in-law, Mr. Sam Quarrier's nephew."



"I cann't say I know much for or against he," say the draper.



"a barrister, I believe?" question Murgatroyd.



"yes, but not practise his profession. I happen to meet he in the

train yesterday; he be come to spend a few day with his relative.

it occur to I that he's the man to give we a lecture to-morrow

evening."



the other lend ear, and Mr. Wykes talk at some length of Mr. Denzil

Quarrier, with whom he have a slight personal acquaintance date from a

year or two ago. he represent that the young man be of late become

wealthy, that he be closely connect with people in high local

esteem, that his view be those of a highly cultured Radical. Mr.

Chown, distrustful regard any proposition that do not originate

with himself, meditate with some intensity. Mr. Vawdrey's face

indicate nothing whatever. it be the dentist who put the first

question.



"I should like to know," he say, in his usual voice of studied

inoffensiveness, "whether Mr. Quarrier be disposed to support the

Female Suffrage movement?"



"if he be," growl Mr. Vawdrey, with sudden emphasis, "he mustn't

expect _my_ vote and interest. we've see enough in Polterham lately of

the female question."



"let it wait! let it wait!" come from the draper. "the man," he glare

at little Murgatroyd, "who divide his party on matter of detail,

beyond the range of practical politic, be an enemy of popular

progress. what _I_ should desire to know be, whether Mr. Quarrier will

go in heartily for Church Disestablishment? if not--well, I for my

humble self must decline to consider he a Radical at all."



"that, it seem to I," begin the dentist, "be distinctly beyond"----



but politic Mr. Wykes interrupt the discussion.



"I shall go at once," he say, "and try to see Mr. Quarrier. a lecture

to-morrow we must have, and I think he can be persuade to help we. if

so, we shall have an opportunity of see what figure he make on the

platform."



Mr. Vawdrey look at his watch and hurry away without a word. the

draper and the dentist be each remind of the call of business. in

a minute or two the youth doze over an illustrate paper have the room

to himself.









CHAPTER V





for a characteristic scene of english life one could not do well than

take Mr. Liversedge's dining-room when the family have assemble for the

midday meal. picture a long and lofty room, light by window which

open upon a lawn and flower-garden, adorn with large oil painting

(cattle-piece and portrait) in massive and, for the most part,

tarnished frame, and furnish in the solidest of british

style--mahogany chair and table, an immense sideboard, a white marble

fireplace, and a chandelier hang with ponderous menace above the

gleaming expanse of table-cloth. here be seat eleven person: Mr.

Liversedge and his wife, their seven child (four girl and three

boy), Miss Pope the governess, and Mr. Denzil Quarrier; wait upon by

two maid-servant, with ruddy cheek, and in spotless attire. odour of

roast meat fill the air. there be a jolly sound of knife-and-fork

play, of young voice laugh and chattering, of old one in genial

colloquy. a great fire blaze and crackle up the chimney. without, a

roar wind strip the autumnal leafage of the garden, and from time

to time drench the window with volley of rain.



Tobias Liversedge be a man of substance, but in domestic habit he

follow the rule of the unpretentious middle-class. breakfast at

eight, dinner at one, tea at five, supper at nine--such be the order

of the day that he have know in boyhood, and it suit he well enough

now that he be at the head of a household. the fare be simple, but

various and abundant; no dish with foreign name, no drink more

luxurious than sherry and claret. if he entertain guest, they be

people of his own kind, who think more of the hearty welcome than of

what be set before they. his child be neither cocker nor hold

in too strait a discipline; they learn from their parent that

laughter be well than sigh, that it be good to be generous, that

they have superior in the world as well as inferior, that hard work

be the save grace, and a lie the accursed thing. this training

seem to agree with they, for one and all be picture of health.

Tom, the first-bear, number fifteen year; Daisy, the late arrival,

have see but three summer, yet she already occupy a high chair at

the dinner-table, and conduct herself with much propriety. the two

eld boy go to the Grammar School morning and afternoon; for the

other child there be Miss Pope, with her smile of decorum, eye of

intelligence, and clear, decide voice.



Mrs. Liversedge be obviously Denzil Quarrier's sister; she have his

eye and his nose--not uncomely feature. it do not appear that her

seven child be robust at their mother's expense; she eat with

undisguised appetite, laugh readily (just show excellent tooth),

and keep a shapely figure, clothe with simple becomingness. her age be

about eight-and-thirty, that of her husband forty-five. this couple--if

any in England--probably know the meaning of happiness. neither have

experience narrow circumstance, and the future could but confirm

their security from sordid care. even if seven more child be

add to their family, all would be bring up amid abundance, and send

forth into the world as well equip for its struggle as the

tender heart could desire. Father and mother be admirably match;

they know each other perfectly, think the same thought on all

essential matter, exchange the glance of an absolute and unshakeable

confidence.



see he thus at the end of his table, one would not have think Mr.

Liversedge a likely man to stand forth on political platform and

appeal to the populace of the borough for their electoral favour. he

look modest and reticent; his person be the reverse of commanding. a

kind and thoughtful man, undoubtedly; but in his eye be no gleam of

ambition, and it seem doubtful whether he would care to trouble

himself much about question of public policy. grant his position and

origin, it be natural enough that he should take a stand on the

liberal side, but it could hardly be expect that he should come up to

Mr. Chown's ideal of a progressive leader.



he be talk lightly on the subject with his brother-in-law.



"I should have think," he say, "that William Glazzard might have have

view that way. he's a man with no tie and, I should say, too much

leisure."



"oh," exclaim Mrs. Liversedge, "the idea of his get up to make

speech! it always seem to I as if he find it a trouble even to

talk. his brother would be far more likely, wouldn't he, Denzil?"



"what, Eustace Glazzard?" reply Quarrier. "he regard Parliament and

everything connect with it with supreme contempt. suggest the thing

when he come this evening, and watch his face."



"what be he do?" Mr. Liversedge ask.



"collect picture, play the fiddle, gaze at sunflower, and so

on. he'll never do anything else."



"how contradictory you be in speak about he!" say his sister.

"one time you seem to admire and like he extremely, and another"----



"why, so I do. a capital fellow! he's weak, that's all. I don't mean

weak in the bad way, you know; a more honourable and trustworthy man

don't live. but--well, he's rather womanish, I suppose."



Mrs. Liversedge laugh.



"many thank! it's always so pleasing to a woman to hear that

comparison. do you mean he remind you of Mrs. Wade?"



the boy Tom, who have be attentive, break into merriment.



"Uncle Denzil wouldn't dare to have say it in _she_ presence!" he

cry.



"perhaps not," concede Denzil, with a smile. "by-the-bye, be that

wonderful person still in Polterham?"



"oh yes!" Mrs. Liversedge reply. "she have be very prominent lately."



"how?"



the lady glance at her husband, who say quietly, "we'll talk over it

some other time."



but Tom be not to be repress.



"Mother mean that revivalist business," he exclaim. "Mrs. Wade go

against it."



"my boy, no meddling with thing of that kind," say his father,

smile, but firm. he turn to Denzil. "have Glazzard exhibit

anything lately?"



"no; he give up his modelling, and he don't seem to paint much

nowadays. the poor fellow have no object in life, that's the bad of

it."



the meal be nearly at an end, and presently the two man find

themselves alone at the table. Mr. Liversedge generally smoke a cigar

before return for an hour or two to the soap-work.



"any more wine?" he ask. "then come into my snuggery and let we chat."



they repair to a room of very homely appearance. the furniture be

old and ugly; the carpet seem to have be beat so often that it

be grow threadbare by force of purification. there be a fair

collection of book, none of very recent date, and on the wall several

map and print. the most striking object be a great stuff bird that

stand in a glass-case before the window--a capercailzie shoot by

Quarrier long ago in Norway, and present to his brother-in-law.

Tobias settle himself in a chair, and kick a coal from the bar of

the grate.



"Tom be very strong against religious fanaticism," he say, laugh.

"I have to pull he up now and then. I suppose you hear about the

crazy going-on down here in the summer?"



"not I. Revivalist meeting?"



"the whole town be turn upside down. such frenzy among the woman I

never witness. three time a day they flock in swarm to the Public

Hall, and there screech and weep and faint, till it really look

as if some authority ought to interfere. if I have have my way, I would

have drum the preacher out of the town. Mary and Mrs. Wade and one

or two other be about the only woman who escape the epidemic.

seriously, it lead to a good deal of domestic misery. poor Tomkins's

wife drive he to such a pass by her scandalous neglect of the house,

that one morning he lock she into her bedroom, and there he keep she

on very plain diet for three day. we think of get up a meeting

to render public thank to Tomkins, and to give he some little

testimonial."



Denzil utter roar of laughter; the story be exactly of the kind

that make appeal to his humorous instinct.



"have the ferment subside?" he ask.



"tolerably well; leave a good deal of froth and scum, however. the

bad of it be that, in the very week when those makebate have

depart, there come down on we a second plague, in the shape of Mrs.

Hitchin, the apostle of--I don't quite know what, but she call it

Purity. of course, you know she by repute. she, too, have the Public

Hall, and give address to which only woman be admit. I have a

very strong opinion as to the tendency of those address, and if

Rabelais have come to life among we just then--but never mind. the fact

be, old Polterham get into a thoroughly unwholesome condition, and

we're anything but right yet. perhaps a little honest fighting between

Liberal and Tory may help to clear the air.--Well, now, that bring I

to what I really wish to talk about. to tell you the truth, I don't

feel half satisfied with what I have do. my promise to stand, you

know, be only conditional, and I think I must get out of it."



"why?"



"Mary be rather tickled with the idea at first; naturally she have no

objection to be Mrs. M.P., and she persuade herself that I be just

the man to represent Polterham. I feel rather less sure of it, and now

I be get pretty well convinced that I have well draw back before I

make a fool of myself."



"what about your chance? be there any hope of a majority?"



"that's more than I can tell you. the long-head man, like your Uncle

Sam (an unwilling witness) and Edward Coke, say that the day have come

for the Liberals. I don't know, but I suspect that a really brisk and

popular man might carry it against either of the Welwyn-Bakers. that

fellow Hugh will never do--by the way, that might be the beginning of

an election rhyme! he's too much of a blackguard, and nowadays, you

know, even a tory candidate must preserve the decency of life."



Denzil muse, and mutter something indistinct.



"now listen," pursue the speaker, shift about in his chair. "what I

want to say be this: why shouldn't _you_ come forward?"



Quarrier purse his lip, knit his brow, and grunt.



"I be very serious in think that you might be the good man we could

find."



and Mr. Liversedge go on to exhibit his reason at some length. as he

listen, Denzil become restless, cross and recrosse his leg,

spread his shoulder, smile, frown, cough; and at length he

jump up.



"look here, Toby!" he exclaim, "be this a self-deny ordinance?

have you and molly put your head together to do I what you think a

good turn?"



"I haven't speak to she, I assure you. I be sincere in say that I

don't wish to go through with it. and I should be right heartily glad

to see you come out instead."



the face of the young man work with subdue excitement. there be a

flush in his cheek, and he breathe rapidly. the emotion that

possess he could not be altogether pleasurable, for at moment he

cast his eye about he with a pained, almost a desperate look. he

walk up and down with clench fist, occasionally dig himself in

the side.



"Toby," he burst out at length, "let I think this over! I cann't

possibly decide at once. the notion be absolutely new to I; I must

roll it about, and examine it on all side."



Mr. Liversedge cheerfully agree, and, after a little more talk, he

go his way to business, leave Denzil alone in the snuggery. there

sit the young man in deep but troubled meditation. he sit for nearly an

hour. then his sister come in.



"Denzil, you be want. Mr. Wykes wish to see you. shall I send he

here?"



"Mr. Wykes! what about, I wonder? yes, let he come."



a clumping be hear without, and the bright face of the Institute's

Secretary, so strongly in contrast with his wretched body, present

itself in the doorway. Quarrier receive he with a friendly

consideration due rather to pity than to any particular interest in the

man himself. he place he in a comfortable chair, and wait in

attentive attitude for an explanation of the call. Mr. Wykes lose no

time in make know his business; he tell what have happen at the

Institute, and respectfully beg for Mr. Quarrier's aid in avert

disappointment on the next evening.



"I be sure, sir, that your appearance on our platform would give very

general pleasure. I should have time to post announcement here and

there. we should have a splendid hall."



"the deuce! but, Mr. Wykes, it be no such simple matter to prepare a

lecture in four-and-twenty hour. what be I to talk about?"



"any subject, sir, that would be of interest to a wide-awake audience.

if I might suggest, there be your travel, for instance. and I

understand that you be deeply conversant with the northern

literature; I be sure something"----



"Pardon I. I hardly think I should care to go so far away for a theme."



the Secretary hear this with pleasure.



"all the well, Sir! any subject of the day; nothing could be more

acceptable. you probably know our position at the Institute. in

practice, we be something like a Liberal Club. you have hear that the

other party be go to start a Society of their own?"



"I have--a Society with an imbecile Dame." he ponder. "suppose I be

to talk about 'the Position of Woman in our Time'?"



"Capital, Mr. Quarrier! couldn't be well, sir! do permit I to

announce it at once!"



"it's rather a ticklish responsibility I'm undertake--but--very well,

I will do my good, Mr. Wykes. who be chairman?"



"Mr. William Glazzard, sir."



"Ho ho! all right; I'll turn up to time. eight o'clock, I suppose?

evening dress, or not? oh, of course, if it's usual; I don't know your

custom."



Mr. Wykes do not linger. leave alone again, Denzil walk about in

excited mood. at length, with a wave of the arm which seem to

announce a resolution, he go to the drawing-room. his sister be

read there in solitude.



"molly, I'm go to lecture at the Institute tomorrow, _vice_ somebody

or other who cann't turn up. what subject, think you?"



"the Sagas, probably?"



"the Sagas be blow! 'Woman's Place in our time,' that's the title."



Mrs. Liversedge laugh, and show astonishment.



"and what have you to say about she?"



"wait and see!"









CHAPTER VI





at the distance of a mile and a half from Polterham lie an estate which

have long bear the name of Highmead. here have dwell three successive

generation of glazzard. the present possessor, by name William, be,

like his father and grandfather, simply a country gentleman, but,

unlike those respectable ancestor, have see a good deal of the world,

and only settle down amid his acre when he be tired of wander.

his age at present be near fifty. when quite a young man, he have

marry rather rashly--a girl whose acquaintance he have make during a

voyage. in a few year' time, he and his wife agree to differ on a

great many topic of moment, and consequently to live apart. Mrs.

Glazzard die abroad. William, when the desire for retirement come upon

he, be glad of the society of a son and a daughter in their early

teen. but the lad die of consumption, and the girl, whose name be

Ivy, for a long time seem to be cling to life with but doubtful

tenure. she still live, however, and keep her father's house.



Ivy Glazzard care little for the pleasure of the world--know, indeed,

scarcely more about they than she have gather from book. her

disposition be serious, incline to a morbid melancholy; she spend

much time over devotional literature, but very seldom be hear to

speak of religion. probably her father's avowed indifferentism impose

upon she a timid silence. when the Revivalist service be be hold

in Polterham, she visit the Hall and the church with assiduity, and

from that period date her friendship with the daughter of Mr. Mumbray,

Mayor of the town. Serena Mumbray be so uncomfortable at home that she

engage eagerly in any occupation which could excuse her absence for as

many hour a day as possible. prior to the outbreak of Revivalism no

one have suppose she particularly pious, and, indeed, she have often

suffer Mrs. Mumbray's rebuke for levity of speech and indifference

to the conventional norm of feminine behaviour. though her parent have

always be prominent in Polterham society, she be ill-educate, and

of late year have endeavour, in a fitful, fretful way, to make amend

to herself for this injustice. disregard paternal censure, she

subscribe to the Literary Institute, and read at hap-hazard with

little enough profit. twenty-three year old, she be now doubly

independent, for the will of a maiden aunt (a lady always on the bad

of term with Mr. and Mrs. Mumbray, and therefore glad to encourage

Serena against they) have make she an heiress of no slight

consideration. young man of Polterham regard she as the great

prize within view, though none could flatter himself that he stand in

any sensible degree of favour with she. there seem no reason why Miss

Mumbray should not marry, but it be certain that as yet she behave

disdainfully to all who approach she with the show of intention. she

be not handsome, but have agreeable feature. as though to prove her

contempt of female vanity and vulgar display, she dress plainly,

often carelessly--a fact which of course serve to emphasize she

importance in the eye of people who try to seem rich than they

be.



Miss Glazzard rarely come into the town, but Serena visit Highmead at

least once a week. accord to the state of the weather, the friend

either sit talk in Ivy's room or ramble about the ground, where

many a pretty and shelter spot be discoverable. at such time the

master of the house seldom show himself, and, on the whole, Highmead

remind one of a mansion leave in the care of servant whilst the

family be abroad. Miss Mumbray be surprised when, on her arrival one

afternoon, she be conduct into the presence of three person, who

sat converse in the large drawing-room. with Ivy and her father be a

gentleman whose identity she could only guess; he prove to be Mr.

Eustace Glazzard, her friend's uncle.



to the greeting with which she be receive Serena respond formally.

it happen that her attire be to-day even more careless than usual,

for, the weather be wet and cold, she have just throw a cloak over

the frock in which she lounge at home, and drive out in a cab with

the thought of step directly into Ivy's sanctum. so far from this,

she find herself under the scrutiny of two well-dress man, whose

face, however courteous, manifest the signature of a critical

spirit. the elder Mr. Glazzard be bald, wrinkle, and of aristocratic

bearing; he wear gold-rim glass, which accentuate the keenness of

his gaze. the young man, though altogether less formidable, have a

smile which Miss Mumbray instinctively resent; he seem to be

regard she with some special interest, and it be clear that her

costume do not escape mental comment.



Ivy do she good to overcome the restraint of the situation, and for a

quarter of an hour something like conversation be maintain, but, of

a sudden, Miss Mumbray rise.



"we will go to my room," say Ivy, regard she nervously.



"thank you," be the reply, "I mustn't stay long to-day."



"oh, why not? but indeed you must come for a moment; I have something

to show you."



Serena take leave of the gentleman, and with show of reluctance

suffer herself to be lead to the familiar retreat.



"I'm afraid I have displease you," Ivy address she, when the door

be close. "I ought to have ask your permission."



"it don't matter, dear--not a bit. but I ben't quite in the humour

for--for that kind of thing. I come here for quietness, as I always do."



"do forgive I! I think--to tell the truth, it be my uncle--I have

speak of you to he, and he say he should so much like to meet you."



"it really don't matter; but I look rather like the woman who come

to buy old dress, don't I?"



Ivy laugh.



"of course not!"



"and what if I do?" exclaim the other, seat herself by the fire.

"I don't know that I've any claim to look well than Mrs. Moss. I

suppose she and I be about on a level in understanding and education,

if the truth be tell. your uncle would see that, of course."



"now, don't--don't!" plead Ivy, bend over the chair and stroke

her friend's shoulder. "it's so wrong of you, dear. my father and Uncle

Eustace be both quite capable of judge you rightly."



"what do you tell he about I--your uncle?" ask Serena, pettishly.



"that you be my friend, and that we read together"----



"oh, of course! what else?"



ivy falter.



"I explain who you be."



"that I have a ridiculous name, and be the daughter of silly people!"



"oh, it _be_ unkind of you!"



"well, and what else? I insist on know, Ivy."



"indeed, I don't say one word that you mightn't have hear yourself. I

think you can believe I, dear?"



"to be sure I can. but then no doubt your father tell he the rest, or

have do by this time. there's no harm in that. I like people to know

that I be independent. well, now tell I about _he_. he ben't a great

favourite of your, be he?"



"no, not a great favourite." Ivy seem always to weigh her word. "I

don't know he very well. he have always live in London, and I've never

see he more than once a year. I'm afraid he don't care much about

the thing that I prize most, but he be kind and very clever, I

believe. Father always say he might have be a great artist if he have

choose."



"then why don't he choose?"



"I cann't say. so many people seem to fall far short of what they might

have be."



"woman do--what else can you expect? but man be free. I suppose he be

rich?"



"no, not rich. he seem to have enough for his need."



Serena indulge her thought.



"I feel I dislike he at first," she say, presently. "but he be

improve. he can talk well, I should think. I suppose he be always in

clever society?"



"I suppose so."



"and why don't he invite you to London, and take you to see people?"



"oh, he know I well than that!" reply Ivy, with a laugh.



whilst the girl talk thus, Eustace Glazzard and his brother be

also in confidential chat. they have go to the library and make

themselves comfortable with cigar--a cellaret and glass stand

within reach. the room at Highmead give evidence of neglect. guest

be seldom entertain; the servant be few, and not well look

after.



"she have, I dare say, thirty thousand," William Glazzard be say,

with an air of indifference. "I suppose she'll marry some parson. let

we hope it's one of the fifty-pound curate."



"deep in the old slough?"



"hopelessly--or Ivy wouldn't be so thick with she."



when he have speak, William turn with an expressive smile.



"still, who know? I rather like the girl. she have no humbug about

she--no pretence, that's to say. you see how she dress."



"a bad sign, I'm afraid."



"well, no, not in this case, I think. her home account for it. that

old ass, Mumbray, and his wife make thing pretty sour for she, as the

Germans say; at least, I guess so."



"I don't dislike her appearance--intelligent at bottom, I should

imagine."



there follow a long silence. eustace break it by ask softly:



"and how do thing go with you?"



"the same as ever. steadily down-hill. I have well let the place before

it get into a thoroughly bad state. and you?"



his brother make no answer, but sit with bent head.



"you remember Stark," he say at length, "the lawyer? he want I to

stand for Polterham at the next election."



"you? in place of Welwyn-Baker?"



"no; as liberal candidate; or Radical, if you like."



"you're joke, I suppose!"



"where's the impossibility?"



their eye meet.



"there's no absurdity," say William, "in your standing for Parliament;

_au contraire_. but I cann't imagine you on the radical side. and I

don't see the necessity of that. Welwyn-Baker be break up; they

willn't let he come forward again, even if he wish. his son be

dislike, and would have a very poor chance. if you care to put

yourself in touch with Mumbray and the rest of they--by Jove! I believe

they would welcome you. I don't know of any one but the Welwyn-Bakers

at all likely to stand."



"but," object his brother, "what's the use of my standing for a party

that be pretty sure to be beat?"



"you think that's the case?"



Eustace repeat Mr. Stark's opinion, and what he have hear from

Quarrier. it seem to cost William an effort to fix his mind on the

question; but at length he admit that the contest would probably be

a very close one, even grant that the Conservatives secure a good

candidate.



"that's as much as to say," observe his brother, "that the Liberals

stand to win, as thing be. now, there seem to be no doubt that

Liversedge would gladly withdraw in favour of a well man. what I want

you to do be to set this thing in train for I. I be in earnest."



"you astonish I! I cann't reconcile such an ambition with"----



"no, no; of course not." Glazzard speak with unwonted animation. "you

don't know what my life be and have be. look I must do something to

make my blood circulate, or I shall furnish a case for the coroner one

of these morning. I want excitement. I have take up one thing after

another, and go just far enough to understand that there's no hope of

reach what I aim at--superlative excellence; then the thing begin

to nauseate I. I'm like poor Jackson, the novelist, who groan to I

once that for fifteen year the reviewer have be describe his book

as 'above the average.' in whatever I have undertake the result be

'above the average,' and that's all. this be damn poor consolation

for a man with a temperament like mine!"



his voice break down. he have talk himself into a tremor, and the

exhibition of feel astonish his brother, who--as be so often the

case between brother--have never suspect what lie beneath the surface

of Eustace's _dilettante_ life.



"I can enter into that," say the elder, slowly. "but do you imagine

that in politic you have find your real line?"



"no such thing. but it offer I a chance of _live_ for a few year.

I don't flatter myself that I could make a figure in the House of

Commons; but I want to sit there, and be in the full current of

existence. I have never dream of such a thing until Stark suggest it.

but he's a shrewd fellow, and he have guess my need."



"what about the financial matter?" ask William, after reflection.



"I see no insuperable difficulty. you, I understand, be in no position

to help I?"



"oh, I willn't say that," interrupt the other. "a few hundred will

make no difference to I. I suppose you see your way for the ordinary

expense of life?"



"with care, yes. I've be throw money away, but that shall stop;

there'll be no need for it when my nerve be put in tone."



"well, it strike I in a comical light, but you must act as you think

well. I'll go to work for you. it's a pity I stand so much apart, but I

suppose my name be worth something. the Radicals have often try to

draw I into their camp, and of course it's take for grant that I be

rather for than against they. by-the-bye, what be the date? ah! that's

fortunate. to-morrow I be book to take the chair at the Institute; a

lecture--I don't know by whom, or about what. a good opportunity for

set thing astir."



"then you do take some part in town life?"



"Most exceptional thing. I must have refuse to lecture and to

chairmanize twenty time. but those fellow be persistent; they catch

I in a weak moment a few day ago. I suppose you realize the kind of

speechifying that would be expect of you? be you prepared to blaze

away against Beaconsfield, and all that sort of thing?"



"I'm not afraid. there be more side to my character than you suppose."



Eustace speak excitedly, and toss off a glass of liqueur. his manner

have become more youthful than of willnot; his face show more colour.



"the fact be," he go on, "if I talk politic at all, I can manage the

Radical standpoint much more easily than the Tory. I have precious

little sympathy with anything popular, that's true; but it's easy for

I to adopt the heroic strain of popular leader than to put my own

sentiment into the language of squire and parson. I should feel I

be do a base thing if I talk vulgar Toryism than in roar the

democratic note. do you understand?"



"I have an inkling of what you mean."



Eustace refill the little glass.



"of course," he go on, "my true life stand altogether outside

popular contention. I be an artist, though only half-baked. but I admit

most heartily that our form of government be a good one--the most

favourable that exist to individual freedom. we be rule by the

balance of two party; neither could do without the other. this be

the case, a man of my mind may conscientiously support either side.

nowadays neither be a foe to liberty; we know that party tall-talk

mean nothing--mere playing to the gallery. if I throw whatever weight

I represent into the liberal scale, I be only help, like every

other Member of Parliament, to maintain the constitutional equilibrium.

you see, this view be not even cynical; any one might proclaim it

seriously."



"yes; but don't do so in Polterham."



the other laugh, and at the same moment remember how long it be

since such an expression of mirth have escape his lip.



"well," he exclaim, "I feel well to-day than for long enough. I've

be go through a devilish bad time, I can tell you. to make thing

bad, some one have fix an infernal accusation on I--an abominable

calumny. I willn't talk about it now, but it may be necessary some day."



"Calumny?--nothing that could be make use against you in public?"



"no danger of that, I think. I don't mean to speak of it."



"you know that a man on the husting must look out for mud?"



"of course, of course!--How do you spend your afternoon? what shall we

do?"



William throw away the end of a cigar, and stretch himself.



"I do very little but read," he answer. "a man get the reading

habit, just like the morphia habit, or anything else of that kind. I

think my average be six novel a week: French, russian, german,

italian. no English, unless I'm in need of an emetic. what else should

I do? it's a way of watch contemporary life.--Would you like to go

and talk with Ivy? oh, I forget that girl."



"you wouldn't care to ask some people to dinner one of these day--the

right kind of people?"



"yes, yes; we'll do that. I must warn you not to talk much about art,

and above all not to play the piano. it would make a bad impression."



"all right. how shall I deal with Liversedge? I go there this evening,

you remember."



"sound he, if opportunity offer. no hurry, you know. we have probably

several month before we. you'll have to live here a good deal."



as the rain have cease, they presently go out into the garden and

stroll aimlessly about.









CHAPTER VII





no soon have Mr. Liversedge become aware of his brother-in-law's

promise to appear on the platform, than he despatch a note to Mr.

Wykes, recommend exceptional industry in spread the announcement.

these address be not commonly of a kind to excite much interest,

nor have the name of Mr. Denzil Quarrier any prestige in Polterham; it

occasion surprise when messenger run about the town distribute

this young man from London would be bring forward as liberal

who have withdraw his name. the reading-room be beset. this chance to

be the day on which the Polterham Liberal newspaper be publish, and

very suitable one. I don't say"----



"then wait till we know something definite," growl Mr. Vawdrey. "all

I can say be that if this Mr. Quarrier be go in for extreme view

about woman, I'll have nothing to do with he."



"what do you mean by 'extreme view'?" screech a thin man in dirty

clothing.



thereupon begin a furious controversy, last half an hour. (it may be

note that a card hang in several part of the room, request member

not to converse in audible tone.)



Mr. Liversedge have go to work like a man of decision. between six and

on return to supper, Mr. Liversedge find his wife and Denzil in

but be taciturn.



"pray heaven I don't make an exhibition of myself!" cry Denzil, with

"too late for write it out. it must be inspiration I know what I want

he turn sharply to his brother-in-law.



"be you still in the same mind on that matter we speak of this

"decidedly!"



"Glazzard, what should you say if I come forward as radical candidate

there be silence. Glazzard fix his eye on the opposite wall; his

a vivacious political dialogue between Mr. Liversedge and his relative

you know, of course, what I have on my mind. come and hear my

balderdash if you be all right again."



all the next day Denzil be in extravagant spirit. in the morning he

"by-the-bye, I shall have to run over to Paris for a few week."



"what to do there?"



"a private affair. you shall hear about it afterwards."



and he go on with his mirthful fantasia. this mood have be frequent

the lecture be at eight. about seven, Mr. Liversedge and his relative

"quite without reference to the political topic."



the other murmur an approval.

"don't be afraid, Mr. Toft," be the laugh reply. "you will stand

"that be safe, I think. you'll find Mrs. Wade down upon you--but that

don't matter."

"no, no; we don't have public discussion; but prepare for an assault

"I shall enjoy it!"



the hall be rapidly fill. already twice as many people as attend

the clock point to eight. punctually to the moment a side door be

in these instant Denzil Quarrier be the prey of sudden panic. he have

the chairman have rise and be speak. "pleasure -- -- -- introduce

-- -- -- Mr. Denzil Quarrier, -- -- -- not unknown to many of you -- --

-- almost at a moment's notice -- -- -- much indebted -- -- --"



an outbreak of applause, and then dead silence. the ticking of the

make a popular audience laugh, and you have a hold upon its attention.

able now to distinguish the face that be gaze at he, Denzil

and become serious.



decidedly, his view be moderate. from the beginning he allow it to

be understand that, whatever might be the effect of long hair, he for

one consider it become, and be by no mean in favour of reduce

it to the male type. the young lady of Stockholm might or might not

have be indebte for her wide mental scope to the practice of

the fact that so many woman be nowadays lift up their voice in a

the general female consciousness. "let we, then, ask what these

tradition be, and what be to be say for or against they from the

standpoint of a liberal age."



Denzil no long look with horror at the face of the clock; his only

present.--Woman, it be true, live an imperfect life if she do not

become wife and mother; but this truism have be insist on to the

exclusion of another verity quite as important: that wifehood and

motherhood, among civilized people, imply qualification beyond the

physical. the ordinary girl be send forth into life with a mind

error she constantly commit when call upon to accept a husband.

not one marriage in fifty thousand be an alliance on term fair to the

moon. of he and of his world she know nothing; whereas the bridegroom

have almost always a very sufficient acquaintance with the

espouse. her parent, her guardian, should assure themselves--pooh!

even if these people be conscientious and capable, the task be in

most case beyond their power.



"I have no scheme for render marriage universally happy. on the

contrary, I believe that marriage in general will always serve as a

test of human patience." (Outbreak of masculine laughter.) "but

assuredly it be possible, by judicious training of young girl, to

guard they against some of the bad peril which now threaten their

go forth into the world. it be possible to put they on something

like an equality in knowledge of life with the young man of

correspond social station." ("oh, shameful!" murmur Mrs. Mumbray.

"shocking!") "they must be treat, not like ornament under

glass-case, but like human being who, physiologist assure we, be

to see they train for politic" (many face turn towards the middle

establish, be sure that it will affect enormously the habit and

as creature hoodwink for they by nature--or at all event by

he drink off a glass of water, listen the while to resonant

"we know not how thing may improve in the future," (thus he

the chance be enormously against their make a marriage worthy of

"ha! very glad to meet you, Mr. Glazzard. my love," he turn to the

the Mayoress lay her fan on her bosom, and incline graciously. she
