"I fail to recognize the grievance.--How do you do, Mr. Lovett?--I be

conscious of no tyranny."



"but that be just what Mr. Glazzard mean, papa, put in Serena, with

scarcely disguise contempt.



"ha! oh! to be sure--to be sure! quite so, Mr. Glazzard.--A very

amoose lecture, all the same. not of course to be take

seriously.--Good evening, Mr. Glazzard--good evening!"



the Mayoress again incline. Serena give her acquaintance an enigmatic

look, murmur a leave-taking, and, with an affectionate nod to Ivy,

pass on. Glazzard draw near to his niece.



"your friend be not a disciple of Mrs. Wade?"



"oh dear no, uncle!"



"not just a little bit?" he smile, encouragingly.



"perhaps she would agree with what Mr. Quarrier say about girl have

a right to well instruction."



"I see. don't wait with I if there's any one you would like to speak

to."



Ivy shake her head. she have a troubled expression, as if the experience

of the evening have agitate she.



close at hand, a circle of man have form about Mr. Chown, who be

harangue on the Woman question. what he want be to emancipate the

female mind from the yoke of superstition and of priestcraft. time

enough to talk about give woman vote when they be no long the

slave of an obstructive religion. there be good thing in the

lecture, but, on the whole, it be flabby--flabby. a man who would

discourse on this topic must be courageous; he must dare to shock and

give offence. now, if _he_ have be lecturing----



Glazzard beckon to his niece, and lead she out of ear-shot of these

utterance. in a minute or two they be join by the chairman, who

have already equip himself for departure.



"Bah! I have a splitting headache," say William. "let we get home."



Quarrier be still on the platform, but at this moment he catch

Glazzard's eye, and come hasten down. his friend step forward to

meet he.



"well, how do it go?" Denzil ask, gaily.



"you have great aptitude for that kind of thing."



"so it strike me.--Will you engage yourself to dine with I the day

after to-morrow?"



"Willingly."



"I have an idea. you remember the Coach and horse--over at Rickstead?"



it be a fine old country inn, associate in their memory of boyhood

with hare-and-hound and other sportive excursion. Glazzard nod.



"let we have a quiet dinner there; six-thirty. they can drive we back."



Glazzard rejoin his relative. Denzil, turn come face to face with

Mr. Samuel Quarrier.



"so you take the trouble to come and hear I?"



"to be sure," reply the old man, in a gruff but good-natured voice.

"be it true what they be say? be it to be you instead of Toby?"



"I believe so."



"I shall do my good to get you a licking. all in good part, you know."



"perfectly natural, but I shall win!"









CHAPTER VIII





"do you know of any good house to let in or near the town?" inquire

Denzil of his sister the next morning, as they chat after Toby's

departure to business.



"a house! what do you want with one?"



"oh, I must have a local habitation--the more solid the well."



Mrs. Liversedge examine he.



"what be go on, Denzil?"



"my candidature--that's all. any house advertise in this rag?" he

take up yesterday's _Examiner_, and begin to search the page.



"you can live very well with we."



Denzil do not reply, and his sister, summon by a servant, leave he.

there be indeed an advertisement such as he seek. an old and

pleasant family residence, situate on the outskirt of Polterham (he

remember it very well), would be vacant at Christmas. application

could be make on the premise. still in a state of very high pressure,

unable to keep still or engage in any quiet pursuit, he set off the

instant to view this house. it stand in a high-walled garden, which be

enter through heavy iron-bar gate, one of they now open. the

place have rather a forlorn look, due in part to the decay of the

foliage which in summer shade the lawn; blind be draw on all the

front window; the porch need repair. he ring at the door, and be

quickly answer by a dame of the housekeeper specie. on learn his

business, she begin to conduct he through the room, which be in

habitable state, though with furniture muffle.



"the next room, sir, be the library. a lady be there at present.

perhaps you know her?--Mrs. Wade."



"Mrs. Wade! yes, I know she slightly."



the coincidence amuse he.



"she come here to study, sir--be a friend of the family. will you

go in?"



foresee a lively dialogue, he release his attendant till she should

hear his voice again, and, with preface of a discreet knock, enter

the room. an agreeable warmth meet he, and the aspect of the interior

contrast cheerfully with that of the chamber into which he have

look. there be no great collection of book, but some fine

engraving fill the vacancy around. at the small of two

writing-table sit the person he be prepared to discover; she have

several volume open before she, and appear to be make note. at

his entrance she turn and gaze at he fixedly.



"forgive my intrusion, Mrs. Wade," Denzil begin, in a genial voice. "I

have come to look over the house, and be just tell that you be here.

as we be not absolute strangers"----



he have never meet she in the social way, though she have be a resident

at Polterham for some six year. through Mrs. Liversedge, her repute

have long ago reach he; she be universally consider eccentric,

and, by many people, hardly proper for an acquaintance. on her first

arrival in the town she wear the garb of recent widowhood; relative

here she have none, but an old friendship exist between she and the

occupant of this house, a childless couple name Hornibrook. her age

be now about thirty.



Quarrier be far from regard she as an attractive woman. he think

well of her intelligence than before hear her speak, and it be

not difficult for he to imagine that the rumour of Polterham go much

astray when it concern itself with her characteristic; but the face

now direct to he have no power whatever over his sensibility. it

might be that of a high-spirited and large-brain woman; beautiful it

could not be call. there be something amiss with the eye. all the

other feature might pass: they be neither plain nor comely: a

forehead of good type, a very ordinary nose, largish lip, chin

suggest the masculine; but the eye, to begin with, be prominent,

and they glisten in a way which make it very difficult to determine

their colour. they impress Denzil as of a steely-grey, and seem

hard as the metal itself. his preference be distinctly for soft

feminine eye--such as Lilian gaze with.



her figure be slight, but seem strong and active. he have notice the

evening before that, in stand to address an audience, she look

anything but ridiculous--spite of bonnet. here too, though allow she

surprise to be see, she have the bearing of perfect self-possession,

and perhaps of conscious superiority. fawn-colour hair, less than

luxuriant, lie in soft fold and plait on the top of her head;

possibly (the thought be not incongruous) she hope to gain half an

inch of seem stature.



they shake hand, and Denzil explain his object in call.



"then you be go to settle at Polterham?"



"probably--that be, to keep an abode here."



"you be not married, I think, Mr. Quarrier?"



"no."



"there be a report at the Institute last night--may I speak of it?"



"political? I don't think it need be keep a secret. my brother-in-law

wish I to make friend with the Liberals, in his place."



"I dare say you will find they very willing to meet your advance. on

one question you have take a pretty safe line."



"much to your disgust," say Denzil, who find himself speak very

freely and incline to face debatable point.



"Disgust be hardly the word. will you sit down? in Mrs. Hornibrook's

absence, I must represent she. they be good enough to let I use the

library; my own be poorly supply."



Denzil take a chair.



"be you busy with any particular subject?" he ask.



"the history of woman in Greece."



"Profound! I have as good as forget my classic. you read the

original?"



"after a fashion. I don't know much about the enclitic _de_, and I

couldn't pass an exam. in the hypothetical sentence; but I pick up the

sense as I read on."



her tone seem to imply that, after all, she be not ill-verse in

grammatical nicety. she curtail the word "examination" in an

off-hand way which smack of an undergraduate, and her attitude on the

chair suggest that she have half a mind to cross her leg and throw

her hand behind her head.



"then," say Quarrier, "you have a good deal more right to speak of

woman's claim to independence than most female orator."



she look at he with a good-humour curl of the lip.



"excuse I if I mention it--your tone remind I of that with which you

begin last evening. it be rather patronizing."



"heaven forbid! I be very sorry to have be guilty of such

ill-manner."



"in a measure you atone for it afterwards. when I get up to offer you

my thank, I be think of the good part of your lecture--that where

you speak of girl be entrap into monstrous marriage. that be

generous, and splendidly put. it seem to I that you must have have

case in mind."



for the second time Denzil be unable to meet the steely gaze. he

look away and laugh.



"oh, of course I have; who hasn't--that know anything of the world?

but," he change the subject, "don't you find it rather dull, live in

a place like Polterham?"



"I have my work here."



"Work?--the work of propagandism?"



"precisely. it would be pleasant enough to live in London, and

associate with people of my own way of thinking; but what's the

good?--there's too much of that centralization. the obscurantist take

very good care to spread themselves. why shouldn't those who love the

light try to keep little beacon go in out-of-the-way place?"



"well, do you make any progress?"



"oh, I think so. the mere fact of my existence here ensure that. I

dare say you have hear tell of I, as the countryfolk say?"



the question help Denzil to understand why Mrs. Wade be content with

Polterham. he smile.



"your influence willn't be exert against I, I hope, when the time

come?"



"by no mean. don't you see that I have already begin to help you?"



"by make it clear that my Radicalism be not of the most dangerous

type?"



they laugh, together, and Quarrier, though the dialogue entertain

he, rise as if to depart.



"I will leave you with your Greeks, Mrs. Wade; though I fear you

haven't much pleasure in they from that special point of view."



"I don't know; they have give we important type of womanhood. the

astonishing thing be that we have get so little ahead of they in the

fact of female life. woman be still enslave, though man nowadays

think it necessary to disguise it."



"do you really attach much importance to the right of voting, and so

on?"



"'and so on!' that cover a great deal, Mr. Quarrier. I attach all

importance to a state of thing which take for grant that woman

stand on a level with child."



"so they do--with an inappreciable number of exception. you must be

perfectly well aware of that."



"and so you expect I to be satisfied with it?--I insist on the

franchise, because it symbolize full citizenship. I willn't aim at

anything less than that. woman must be teach to keep their eye on

that, as the irreducible minimum of their demand."



"we mustn't argue. you know that I think they must be teach to look at

quite different thing."



"yes; but what those thing be you have leave I in doubt. we will talk

it over when you have more time to spare. do you know my address?

pear-tree Cottage, Rickstead Road. I shall be very glad to see you if

ever you care to call."



Denzil make his acknowledgment, shake hand, and leave the room.



when his step sound in the hall, the housekeeper appear and

conduct he to the upper story. he examine everything attentively,

but in silence; his feature express grave thought. Mr. and Mrs.

Hornibrook, he be tell, be live in Guernsey, and have resolve to

make that island their permanent abode. a Polterham solicitor be their

agent for the property.



Denzil be give to act on the spur of the moment. there might be

dwelling obtainable that would suit he well than this, but he do

not care to linger in the business. as he pass out of the iron gate

he make up his mind that the house, with necessary repair, would do

very well; and straightway he turn his step to the office of the

agent.









CHAPTER IX





the village of Rickstead lie at some five mile' distance from that

suburb of Polterham where dwelt Mr. Toby Liversedge, Mr. Mumbray (the

Mayor), Mr. Samuel Quarrier, and sundry other distinguished townsfolk.

a walk along the Rickstead Road be a familiar form of exercise with

the less-favour people who have their home in narrow street; for on

either side of the highway lie an expanse of meadow, cross here and

there by pleasant path which lead to the surround hamlet. in this

direction no factory have as yet rise to deform the scene.



darkness be fall when Quarrier set forth to keep his appointment

with Eustace Glazzard at the Coach and Horses Inn. the road-lamp

already glimmer; there would be no moon, but a soft dusky glow

linger over half the sky, and give promise of a fair night. Denzil

feel his boyhood revive as he get clear of the new house, and begin to

recognize gate, tree, bank, and stile; he could not say whether he

enjoy the sensation, but it serve to combat certain troublesome

thought which have beset he since the morning. he be experience

reaction after the excitement of the last two day. a change from the

orderly domesticity of his sister's house have become necessary to

he, and he look forward with satisfaction to the evening he have

plan.



at a turn of the road, which, as he well remember, have be a

frequent limit of his nurse-guard walk five-and-twenty year ago, his

eye fall upon a garden gate mark with the white inscription,

"pear-tree cottage." it bring he to a pause. this must be Mrs.

Wade's dwelling; the intellectual lady have quite slip out of his

thought, and with amusement he stop to examine the cottage as well

as dusk permit. the front be overgrow with some creeper; the low

roof make an irregular line against the sky one window on the

ground-floor show light through a red blind. Mrs. Wade, he have

learn, enjoy but a small income; the interior be probably very

modest. there she sit behind the red blind and meditate on the

servitude of her sex. repress an inclination to laugh aloud, he

step briskly forward.



Rickstead consist of twenty or thirty scatter house; an ancient,

slumberous place, remarkable chiefly for its time-honour inn, which

stand at the crossing of two high road. the landlord have receive

notice that two gentleman would dine under his roof, and the unwonted

event be make quite a stir in the hostelry. Quarrier walk in at

about a quarter-past six, savoury odour salute he from the

threshold. Glazzard have not yet arrive, but in less than five minute

a private carriage draw up to the door, and the friend hail each

other.



the room prepare for they lie well apart from the bar, with its small

traffic. a great fire have be blaze for an hour or two; and the

table, not too large, be lay with the good service the house could

afford--nothing very grand, to be sure, in these day of its decline,

but the general effect be invite to man with a good appetite and

some historical imagination.



"a happy idea of your!" say Glazzard, as he rub his hand before

the great hearth. "be we to begin with a cup of sack?"



punctually the meal be serve; the liquor provide therewith, though

of small dignity, do no discredit to the host. they talk and laugh

over old Grammar School day, old acquaintance long since dead or lose

to sight, boyish ambition and achievement. Dinner dismiss, a bottle

of whisky on the table, a kettle steam by the fire, Denzil's pipe

and Glazzard's cigar comfortably glow, there come a long pause.



"well, I have a story to tell you," say Quarrier, at length.



"so I suppose," murmur the other, without eagerness.



"I don't know that I _should_ have tell it but for that chance

encounter at Kew. but I'm not sorry. I think, Glazzard, you be the one

man in the world in whom I have perfect confidence."



the listener just bend his head. his feature be impassive.



"it concern Lilian, of course," Quarrier pursue, when he have take a

few puff less composedly than hitherto. "I be tell the story

without her leave, but--well, in a way, as I say, the necessity be

force upon I. I cann't help do many thing just now that I should

avoid if I have my choice. I have undertake to fight society by

stratagem. for my own part, I would rather deal it a plain blow in the

face, and bid it do its bad; but"----He wave his hand.



Glazzard murmur and nod comprehension.



"I'll go back to the beginning. that be about three year ago. I be

cross the North Sea (you remember the time; I say good-bye to you

in the Academy, where your bust be), and on the boat I get into

conversation with a decent kind of man who have his wife and family with

he, go to settle for a time at Stockholm; a merchant of some sort.

there be three child, and they have a governess--Lilian, in fact,

who be then not much more than eighteen. I like the look of she from

the first. she be very still and grave,--the kind of thing that take

I in a woman, provide she have good feature. I manage to get a word

or two with she, and I like her way of speak. well, I be

sufficiently interested to say to myself that I might as well spend a

week or two at Stockholm and keep up the acquaintance of these people;

Becket, their name be. I'm not exactly the kind of fellow who go

about fall in love with nursery governess, and at that time

(perhaps you recollect?) I have somebody else in mind. I dare say it be

partly the contrast between that shark of a woman and this modest girl;

at all event, I want to see more of Lilian, and I do. I be in

Stockholm, off and on, for a couple of month. I become good friend

with the Beckets, and before come back to England I make an offer to

Miss Allen--that be the governess's name. she refuse I, and I be

conceited enough to wonder what the deuce she mean."



Glazzard laugh. he be listen with more show of interest.



"well," pursue Quarrier, after puff vainly at his extinguish

pipe, "there be reason for wonder. before I take the plunge, I have

a confidential talk with Mrs. Becket, who as good as assure I that I

have only to speak; in fact, she be rather angry with I for disturb

her family arrangement. Miss Allen, I learn from she, be an

uncommonly good girl--everything I imagine she. Mrs. Becket don't

know her family, but she have engage she on the strength of excellent

testimonial, which don't seem exaggerated. yet after that I be

floor--tell that the thing couldn't be. no weeping and wail; but a

face and a voice that puzzle I. the girl like I well enough; I feel

sure of it. all the same I have to come back to England alone, and in a

devilish bad temper. you remember that I half quarrel with you about

something at our first meeting."



"you be rather bearish," remark Glazzard, knock the ash off his

cigar.



"as I often be. forgive I, old fellow!"



Denzil relit his pipe.



"the next summer I go over to Sweden again. Miss Allen be still with

the Beckets, as I know; but she be only go to stay a few month

more. one of the child have die, and the other two be to be send

to a boarding-school in England. again I go through the proposing

ordeal, and again it be useless. 'confound it!' I shout, 'do deal

honestly with I! what's the matter? be you engage already?' she keep

silent for a long time, then say 'yes!' 'then why in the name of the

Jotuns don't you tell I so before?' I be brutal (as I often be), and

the poor girl begin to cry. then there be a scene--positive stage

business. I wouldn't take her refusal. 'this other man, you don't

really care for he--you be go to sacrifice yourself! I willn't have

it! she weep and moan, and threaten hysteric; and at last, when I

be lose patience (I cann't stand woman's idiotic way of fling

themselves about and make a disturbance, instead of discuss

difficulty calmly), she say at last that, if ever we meet in England,

she would explain her position. 'why not now?'--no, not in the Beckets'

house. very well then, at least she might make it certain that I

_should_ see she in England. after trouble enough, she at last

consent to this. she be to come back with Mr. Becket and the boy,

and then go to her people. I get her promise that she would write to I

and make an appointment somewhere or other.--More whisky?"



Glazzard decline; so Denzil replenish his own glass, and go on. he

be now tremulous with the excitement of his reminiscence; he fidget

on the chair, and his narrative become more jerky than ever.



"her letter come, post in London. she have take leave of the Becket

party, and be suppose to be travel homeward; but she would keep

her word with I. I be to go and see she at an hotel in the West End.

go, I do, punctually enough; I believe I would have go to Yokohama

for half an hour of her society. I find she in a private sitting-room,

look wretched enough, confoundedly ill. and then and there she tell

I her story. it be a queer one; no one could have guess it."



he seize the poker and stir the fire savagely.



"I shall just give you the plain fact. her father be a builder in a

small way, live at Bristol. he have make a little money, and be able

to give his child a decent education. there be a son, who die

young, and then two girl, Lilian the elder of they. the old man must

have be rather eccentric; he bring up the girl very strictly

(their mother die when they be children)--would scarcely let they go

out of his sight, preach to they a sort of mixture of Christianity

and Pantheism, forbid all pleasure except those of home, don't like

they to make acquaintance. their mother's sister keep the house; a

feeble, very pious creature, probably know as much about life as the

cat or the canary--so Lilian describe she. the man come to a sudden

end; a brick fall on his head whilst he be go over a new building.

Lilian be then about fifteen. she have pass the Oxford Local, and be

prepare herself to teach--or rather, be prepare at a good school.



"Allen leave enough money to provide his daughter with about a hundred

a year each; this be to be theirs absolutely when they come of age, or

when they marry. the will have be carefully draw up, and provide

against all sort of real and imaginary danger. the one thing it

couldn't provide against be the imbecility of the old aunt, who still

have the girl in her care.



"a couple of year go by, and Lilian become a teacher in the school

she have attend. do you know anything about Bristol and the

neighbourhood? it seem that the people there be in the habit of go

to a place call Weston-super-mare--excursion steamer, and so on.

well, the girl and their aunt go to spend a day at Weston, and on

the boat they somehow make acquaintance with a young man name

Northway. that mean, of course, he make up to they, and the aunt be

idiot enough to let he keep talk. he stick by they all day, and

accompany they back to Bristol.--Pah! it sicken I to tell the

story!"



he take the glass to drink, but it slip from his nervous finger and

crash on the ground.



"never mind; let it be there. I have have whisky enough. this damned

fellow Northway soon call upon they, and be allow to come as often

as he like. he be a clerk in a commercial house--give reference

which be find to be satisfactory enough, a great talker, and of

course a consummate liar. his special interest be the condition of the

low class; he make speech here and there, go slum, call

himself a Christian Socialist. this kind of thing be no doubt

attractive to Lilian--you know enough of she to understand that. she

be a girl of seventeen, remember. in the end, Northway ask she to

marry he, and she consent."



"do he know of the money?" inquire Glazzard.



"undoubtedly. I shouldn't wonder if the blockhead aunt tell. well, the

wedding-day come; they be marry; and--just as they come out of the

church, up walk a detective, clap his hand on Northway's shoulder,

and arrest he for forgery."



"H'm! I see."



"the fellow be try. Lilian wouldn't tell I the detail; she give I

an old newspaper with full report. Northway have already, some year

before, be in the hand of the police in London. it come out now that

he be keep a mistress; on the eve of marriage he have dispense with

her service, and the woman, in revenge, go to his employer to let

they know certain suspicious fact. he be send to penal servitude for

three year."



"three year!" murmur Glazzard. "about so long ago, I suppose?"



"yes; perhaps he be already restore to society. pleasant reflection!"



"moral and discreet law," remark the other, "which maintain the

validity of such a marriage!"



Denzil utter a few violent oath, reminiscence of the Navy.



"and she go at once to Sweden?" Glazzard inquire.



"in a month or two the head-mistress of her school, a sensible woman,

help she to get an engagement--with not a word say of the

catastrophe. she go as Miss Allen. it be her firm resolve never

again to see Northway. she would not acknowledge that that ceremony in

the church make she a wife. of course, you understand that it ben't

only the forgery that revolt she; that, I suppose, could have be

pardon. in a few day she have learn more of herself and of the world

than in all the previous year. she understand that Northway be really

nothing to she. she accept he because he be the first man who

interest she and make love to she--like thousand of girl. lilian be

rather weak, unfortunately. she cann't stand by herself. but for I, I

be convinced she would now be at the mercy of the blackguard, when he

come out. horror and despair enable she to act firmly three year

ago; but if she have no one to support she--well, she have!"



"what do you propose," ask Glazzard, "when you persuade she to live

with you?"



Denzil wrinkle his brow and look gloomily at the fire.



"we agree to live a life of our own, that be all. to tell you the

truth, Glazzard, I have no clear plan. I be desperately in love,

and--well, I think of emigration some day. you know I too well to

doubt my honesty. Lilian become my wife, for good and all--no doubt

about that! but I don't trouble much about the future--it's my way."



"she cut herself loose from the Bristol people?"



"no; she have correspond with they at long interval. they think she

be teach in London. the tragedy excuse she from visit they. Aunt

and sister be swear to secrecy concern her whereabouts. a good

thing she have no male relative to hunt she up."



"do she draw she income?--I beg your pardon, the question escape I.

of course it's no business of mine."



"never mind. yes, the money be at her disposal; thank to the

settlement require by her father's will. I'm afraid she give away a

lot of it in indiscriminate charity. I needn't say," he add, with a

characteristic movement of the head, "that I have nothing to do with

it."



he pause.



"my real position she don't understand. I have never tell she of how

it be change at my father's death.--Poor girl! about that time she

be disappoint of a child, and have a month or two of black misery. I

keep try to make up my mind what course would be the wise, and in

the meanwhile say nothing. she be marvellously patient. in fact, what

virtue haven't she, except that of a strong will? whatever happen, she

and I stand together; nothing on earth would induce I to part from

she! I want you to understand that. in what I be now go to do, I be

lead solely and absolutely by desire for our common good. you see, we

be face to face with the world's immoral morality. to brave it would

be possible, of course; but then we must either go to a foreign country

or live here in isolation. I don't want to live permanently abroad, and

I do want to go in for activity--political by preference. the result be

we must set our face, tell lie, and hope that fortune will favour we."



there be a strong contrast between Quarrier's glow vehemence and

the show of calm reflection which the other maintain as he listen.

Denzil's face be fully light by the fire; his friend's receive the

shadow of an old-fashioned screen which Glazzard, find the heat

oppressive, have pull forward a few minute ago. the frank, fearless

gaze with which Denzil's word be accompany meet no response; but to

this habit in the listener he be accustom.



"yes, we must tell lie!" Quarrier emphasize the word savagely.

"social law be stupid and unjust, impose its obligation without

regard to person or circumstance. it presume that no one can be

_trust_. I decline to be level with the unthinke multitude. you

and I can be a law to ourselves. what I shall do be this: on return

to town next week, I shall take Lilian over to Paris. we shall live

there for several week, and about the end of the time I shall write to

my people here, and tell they that I have just be marry."



he pause. Glazzard make no motion, and utter no sound.



"I have already drop a mysterious word or two to my sister, which

she will be able to interpret afterwards. happily, I be think a

likely fellow to do odd, unconventional thing. again and again Mary

have hear I rail against the idiocy of ordinary wedding; this

private marriage will be quite in character. I shall state that Lilian

have hitherto be a governess at Stockholm--that I make she

acquaintance there--that I send for she to meet I in Paris. now, tell

I, have you any objection to offer?"



Glazzard shift his position, cough, and draw from his case a new

cigar, which he scrutinize closely from tip to end--even draw it

along under his nose. then he speak very quietly.



"it's feasible--but dangerous."



"but not _very_ dangerous, I think?"



"I cann't say. it depend greatly on your wife's character."



"thank you for use that word, old fellow!" burst from Denzil. "she be

my wife, in every sense of the word that merit the consideration of a

rational creature!"



"I admit it; but I be afraid of lie."



"I be not only afraid of they; I hate they bitterly. I can say with a

clear conscience that I abhor untruthfulness. I have never tell a

deliberate lie since I be old enough to understand the obligation of

truth! but we have to do with monstrous social tyranny. Lilian can no

long live in hiding. she must have a full and enjoyable life."



"yes. but be it possible for she, under these condition?"



"I think so. I have still to speak to she, but I know she will see

thing as I do."



a very faint smile flit over Glazzard's lip.



"good! and you don't fear discovery by--what's his name--Northway?"



"not if Lilian can decide to break entirely with her relative--at all

event for some year. she must cease to draw her dividend, of course,

and must announce to the Bristol people that she have determine on a

step which make it impossible for she to communicate with they

henceforth. I don't think this will be a great sacrifice; her aunt and

her sister have no great hold upon she affections.--You must remember

that her whole being be transform since she last see they. she think

differently on all and every subject."



"you be assure of that?"



"absolutely sure! I have educate she. I have free she from

superstition and conventionality. to she, as to I, the lie we

shall have to tell will be burdensome in the extreme; but we shall both

forget in time."



"that be exactly what you can never do!" say Glazzard, deliberately.

"you enter upon a lifetime of dissimulation. ten, twenty year hence

you will have to act as careful a part as on the day when you and she

first present yourself in Polterham."



"oh, in a sense!" cry the other, impatiently.



"a very grave sense.--Quarrier, why have you take up this political

idea? what's the good of it?"



he lean forward and speak with a low earnest voice. Denzil could not

instantly reply.



"give it up!" pursue Glazzard. "take Lilian abroad, and live a life of

quiet happiness. go on with your literary work"----



"Nonsense! I cann't draw back now, and I don't wish to."



"would you--if--if _i_ be willing to become the liberal candidate?"



Denzil stare in astonishment.



"you? liberal candidate?"



"yes, I!"



a peal of laughter ring through the room. Glazzard have speak as if

with a great effort, his voice indistinct, his eye furtive. when the

burst of merriment make answer to he, he fall back in his chair,

cross his leg, and set his feature in a hard smile.



"you be joke, old fellow!" say Denzil.



"yes, if you like."



Quarrier wish to discuss the point, but the other keep an obstinate

silence.



"I understand," remark Denzil, at length. "you hit upon that thought

out of kindness to I. you don't like my project, and you wish to

save I from its danger. I understand. hearty thank, but I have make

up my mind. I willn't stunt my life out of regard for an imbecile

superstition. the danger be _not_ great; and if they be, I should

prefer to risk they. you electioneer! Ho, ho!"



Glazzard's lip be close draw, his eye veil by the droop lid.

he have cease to smoke, and when, a few minute later, he throw away

his cigar, it be all but squeeze flat by the two finger which have

seem to hold it lightly.



"it be settle!" cry Denzil, jump up, with a return of his

extravagant spirit. "you, Glazzard, will stand by and watch--our

true friend. you on the husting! ha, ha, ha! come, one more glass of

whisky, and I will tell they to get our cab ready. I say, Glazzard,

from this evening forth never a word between we about the secret. that

be understand, of course. you may let people know that you be in my

confidence about the private marriage. but I can trust your discretion

as my own. your glass--pledge I in the old style!"



ten minute more, and they be drive back to Polterham.









CHAPTER X





but for domestic warfare, Mrs. Mumbray would often have be at a loss

how to spend her time. the year of her husband's Mayoralty supply, it

be true, a good many unwonted distraction, but in the middle of the

morning, and late in the evening (if there be no dinner-party),

_ennui_ too frequently weigh upon she. for relief in the former case,

she could generally resort to a quarrel with Serena; in the latter, she

prefer to wrangle with her spouse.



one morning early in December, having indulge her ill-humour with even

more than usual freedom among the servant, she repair to the small

after hover for a few moment with eye that gather wrath, the

"so you pay no attention to my wish, Serena! I will not have you

read such book!"



her daughter rustle the dictionary, impassive. conscious of reduced

authority, Mrs. Mumbray glare and breathe hard, her spacious bosom

work like a troubled sea.



"your behaviour astonish me!--after what you hear Mr. Vialls say."



"Mr. Vialls be an ignorant and foolish man," remark Serena, without

look up.



then do the mother's rage burst forth without restraint, eloquent,

horrisonous. as if to save her ear, Serena go to the piano and begin

to play. when the voice be silence, she turn round.



"you have rather have I play than read that book? that show how little

you understand of either. this be an _immoral_ piece of music! if you

know what it mean you would scream in horror. it be _immoral_, and I

be go to practise it day after day."



the Mayoress stand awhile in mute astonishment, then, with purple face,

sweep from the room.



the family consist of four person. Serena's brother, a young

"we have company this evening, Raglan. take care that you're not late."



"who's come?" ask the young man, without look up.



"Mr. Eustace Glazzard and Miss Glazzard."



"any one else?"



"Mr. Vialls."



"then you don't catch I here! I have an appointment at eight."



"I insist upon your dining with we! if you be not at dinner, I will

have your allowance stop! I mean what I say. not one penny more

shall you receive until you have learn to behave yourself!"



"we'll see about that," reply Raglan, with finished coolness; and,

fold his newspaper, he walk off.



nor do the hour of dinner see his return. the expect guest arrive;

the dinner be portentously dull. Ivy Glazzard scarcely utter a

"what be your opinion of the work of Bawlzac?"



the guest be at a loss for an instant, but he quickly recover

"ah, the french novelist? a man of great power, but--hardly accord

"should you consider he suitable reading for young lady?"



"well, hardly. some of his book be unobjectionable."



Mr. Vialls shoot a fierce glance at he.



"in my opinion, his very name be pollution! I would not permit a page

one and all be drench with impurity!"



"certainly many of they be," concede Glazzard.



"lamentable," sigh the Mayor, raise his glass, "to think that quite

the theatre be discuss, Mr. Vialls assail it as a mere agent of

"Shakespeare need a great deal of expurgate. but some of his play

the Mayor beam with gratification.



"no one deny," strike in Mr. Vialls, "that to a pure mind all thing

"Pardon I," interrupt the Mayor--"Longfellow?--the author of that

"yes."



"now, really--I be surprised--I should have think--the fact be, when

quite forget that it contain what seem to I very gross

must ask your pardon for quote it, Mr. Vialls)--



  'oh stay, the maiden cry, and rest

  Thy weary head upon this breast.'



surely, that be all but indecency. in fact, I write at once to the

master and draw his attention to the passage, request that my boy

might never be ask to repeat such a poem. the force of my objection

Mr. Vialls screw up his lip and frown at the table-cloth, but say

"our task nowadays," pursue the Mayor, with confidence, "be to

he linger on the last word, and his gaze become abstract.



"very true, very true indeed!" cry the clergyman. "for one thing, how

careful a parent should be with regard to the periodical literature

they rise at length, and go to the drawing-room. there Glazzard

"you be altogether wrong, as usual," reply Mr. Mumbray, with his

on reach his brother's house, he find a letter wait for he; it

bear the Paris postmark. the content be brief.





"DEAR GLAZZARD:



"I announce to you the fact of our marriage. the L.s will hear of it

simultaneously. we be enjoy ourselves.



  "ever your,

    "D.Q."





he go at once to the room where William be sit, and say, in a

quiet voice:



"Quarrier have just got marry--in Paris."



"oh? to whom?"



"an english girl who have be a governess at Stockholm. I know it be

impending."



"have he make a fool of himself?" ask William, dispassionately.



"I think not; she seem to be well educate, and

good-look--accord to his report."



"why don't you mention it before?"



"oh, his wish. we talk it all over when he be here. he have an idea

that a man about to be marry always cut a ridiculous figure."



the eld man look puzzle.



"no mystery--eh?"



"none whatever, I believe. a decent girl without fortune, that's all. I

suppose we shall see they before long."



the subject be shortly dismiss, and Eustace fall to report the

remarkable conversation in which he have take part at the Mayor's

such savage contemptuousness as distinguish the narrator. William

Glazzard view the world from a standpoint of philosophic calm; he

expect so little of man in general, that disappointment or vexation

could rarely befall he.



"these people," he observe, "think themselves pillar of society, and

the good of the joke be, that they really _be_ what they imagine.

without tolerably honest fool, we should fare badly at the hand of

mean. I see no dawn as yet of the millennium of brain."









CHAPTER XI





the weather, for this time of year, be unusually bright in Paris. each

morning glisten with hoar-frost; by noon the sky shine blue over

clean, dry street, and garden which make a season for themselves,

leafless, yet defiant of winter's melancholy. lilian see it all with

the eye of a stranger, and often be able to forget her anxiety in the

joy of wonderful, new impression.



one afternoon she be rest in the room at the hotel, whilst Quarrier

go about the town on some business or other. a long morning at the

Louvre have tired she, and her spirit droop. in imagination she go

back to the day of silence and solitude in London; the memory affect

she with something of homesickness, a wish that the past could be

restore. the little house by Clapham Common have grow dear to she; in

its shelter she have shed many tear, but also have know much happiness:

that sense of security which be now lose, the hope that there she

might live always, hide from the world's inquisitive gaze, justify

to her own conscience by love and calm. what now be before she? not

only the elaborate deceit, the perpetual risk, weigh upon her heart;

she be summon to a position such as she have never foresee, for

which she have receive no training. when Denzil reveal to her his

real standing in the world, speak laughingly of the wealth he have

inherit, and of his political ambition, her courage fail before

the prospect. she have not dare to let he see all her despondency, for

his impatient and sanguine temper would have resent it. to please he

and satisfy his utmost demand be the one purpose of her life. but the

task he have impose seem to she, in these hour of faintness, no less

than terrible.



he enter, gay as usual, ready with tender word, pet name and

diminutive, the "little language" of one who be still a lover. see

how thing be with she, he sit down to look over an English

newspaper. presently his attention stray, he fall into reverie.



"well," he exclaim at length, rouse himself, "they have the news by

now."



she give no answer.



"I can imagine how Mary will talk. 'oh, nothing that Denzil do can

surprise I! whoever expect he to marry in the ordinary way?' and

then they'll laugh, and shrug their shoulder, and hope I mayn't have

play the fool--good, charitable folk!"



still she say nothing.



"rather out of sort to-day, Lily?"



"I wish we be go to stay here--never to go back to England."



"live the rest of our life in a Paris hotel!"



"no, no--in some quiet place--a home of our own."



"that wouldn't suit I, by any mean. Paris be all very well for a

holiday, but I couldn't make a home here. there's no place like

England. don't you ever think what an unspeakable blessing it be to

have be bear in England? every time I go abroad, I rejoice that I be

not as these foreigner. even my scandinavian friend I cann't help

despise a little--and as for Frenchmen! there's a great deal of the

old island prejudice in I."



Lilian smile, raise herself slightly upon the sofa.



"these old Latin nation have have their day," he continue, with a wave

of the arm. "France, Italy, Spain--they have play their part in

civilization, and have nothing leave now but old relic and modern

bluster. the future's with we Teutons. if I be not an Englishman, I

would be an American. the probability be that we shall have a hard

fight one of these day with the Slavs--and all the well, perhaps; I

don't think the world can do without fight yet awhile."



"I should be sorry to hear you teach people that," say Lilian.



"oh," he laugh, "it wouldn't fit into our electoral campaign! no

danger of my preach bloodthirstiness. but how I shall enjoy the

bloodless fight down at Polterham! I want you to look forward to it in

the same way. do cheer up, Lily!--you see I have be gradually move

in this direction. when I find myself a man of mean, I know that the

time have come for stir. write about the Sea-king be all very

well in its way, but I be no bear literary man. I must get that book

finish and publish, though. it might help I with the constituency.

a book give a man distinction."



"you seem to I to have change very much."



"no; it's only that you don't know I thoroughly. to tell you the

truth, that life of hide away in London ben't a very good thing for

not the kind of man to profit I. Glazzard--well, Glazzard be an odd

sort of fellow--helpful now and then, but on the whole musty. he have no

ambition, think it enough to doze on among his picture, and that kind

of thing. the fact be, such companionship have make I conceited. I want

to get among my equal and my superior--as I shall do if I become a

Member of Parliament."



"your equal--perhaps."



"confound it! _your_ influence have tend the same way. you spoil

I--make I think myself a fine fellow. I suppose one's wife ought to

talk like that--I don't dislike it, you know; but if I end by never

do anything at all, I should be confoundedly ashamed of myself. but

the more I think of it, the well satisfied I be that a political

career be the good thing for I. you see, this be the age of political

progress--that before everything. we English be work out our

revolution in a steady and sensible way,--no shrieking and

slaughter--we leave that to people who don't really know what they

want, and will never get much to speak of. we go ahead soberly on the

constitutional highway--with a little hearty swearing to clear the air

now and then."



Lilian laugh.



"well, I be say it be a political age, and I think a man ought to

go in for the first interest of his time. what have we to do just now

with artistic aim? the English, at any time, care little or nothing

for art; one have to recognize that. our task in the world be

practical--to secure all man a sufficiency of beef and beer, and honest

freedom. I like to feel that I be on the advance wave; I don't care

for your picturesque pond; they generally have a bad smell."



the effect of his vigorous talk be manifest in Lilian's face. she

yield her spirit to his, be bear whither he would.



"you talk of live in Paris--why, if you really know Paris, you would

hate the place. underneath all this show of civilization, refinement,

brilliancy--I'm glad to say you cann't even guess what it cover. the

town reek with abomination. I'm get sick of it."



the sincerity of his moral disgust be obvious. no one know so well as

Lilian the essential purity--even the puritanism--of Quarrier's temper.



"for all that," he add, merrily, "we'll go and dine at the

restaurant, and then look in at the Francais. they know how to cook

here, and they know how to play the fool--no deny it."



when Lilian go forth with he she have once more succeed in
