their way, as Quarrier's robust manhood.



Denzil's French be indifferently good, well perhaps than his

capacity for pick out from the bill of fare a little dinner which

should exalt he in the eye of waiter. he go to work, however, with

a noble disregard for consequence, whether to digestion or pocket.

where Lilian be concern there could be no such thing as

extravagance; he glory in obtain for she the good of everything

that money could command. the final "_Bien, monsieur_," be, after all,

sufficiently respectful, and our friend lean back with the pleasant

consciousness of duty perform.



he drink a good deal of wine, and talk with a spontaneity beyond the

ordinary Briton. towards the close of dinner his theme be the come

electoral contest.



"you know," he say, bend over the table, "you will be able to give

I important help. the wife of a candidate--especially of a Radical

candidate--can find plenty of work, if she know how to go about it. as

little humbug as possible; and as little loss of self-respect, but we

shall have to shake a good many dirty hand. your turn for 'slum'

will serve we well, but I know the danger of it. you'll be come home

_éplorée_, as they say here. I hope you'll grow strong in that

respect. one have to harden one's heart a little."



"I know it be wise to do so."



"of course! it's not only that you be constantly impose upon; the

indulgence of universal sympathy be incompatible with duty to one's

self--unless you become at once a sister of mercy. one be bind, in

common sense, to close eye and ear against all but a trifling

fraction of human misery. why, look, we sit here, and laugh and talk

and enjoy ourselves; yet at this instant what horror be be enact

in every part of the world! man be perish by every conceivable form

of cruelty and natural anguish. sailor be gurgle out their life in

sea-storm; soldier be agonize on battle-field; man, woman, and

child be be burn, boil, hack, squashed, rent, explode to

death in every town and almost every village of the globe. here in

Paris, and over there in London, there be no end to the form of misery

our knowledge suggest--all suffer while we eat and talk. but to sit

down and think persistently of it would lead to madness in any one of

imagination like your. we have to say: it don't concern we! and no

more it do. we haven't the ordering of the world; we cann't alter the

vile course of thing. I like to swear over it now and then (especially

when I pass a London hospital), but I soon force myself to think of

something else. you must do the same--even to the swearing, if you

like. there's a tendency in our time to excess of humanitarianism--I

mean a sort of lachrymose habit which really do no good. you

represent it in some degree, I'm afraid--eh? well, well, you've live

too much alone--you've get into the way of brooding; the habit of

social life will strengthen you."



"I hope so, Denzil."



"oh, undoubtedly! one more little drop of wine before the coffee.

nonsense! you need stimulus; your vitality be low. I shall prescribe

for you henceforth. merciful heaven! how that french woman do talk!

a hundred word to the minute for the last half hour."



a letter have arrive for he at the hotel in his absence. it be from

Mr. Hornibrook's agent, announce that the house at Polterham be now

vacate, and that Mr. Quarrier might take possession just as soon as he

choose.



"_that's_ all right!" he exclaim, after read it to Lilian. "now

we'll think of get back to London, to order our furniture, and all

the rest of it. the place can be make habitable in a few week, I

should say."









CHAPTER XII





an emissary from Tottenham Court Road speed down to Polterham, survey

the vacant house, return with professional computation. Quarrier and

lilian abode at the old home until everything should be ready for they,

and Mrs. Liversedge represent her brother on the spot--solve the

doubt of workman, hire servant, make minor purchase. she invite

Denzil to bring his wife, and dwell for the present under the

Liversedge roof, but her brother prefer to wait. "I don't like

makeshift; we must go straight into our own house; the dignity of the

radical candidate require it." so the work glow, and as little time

as possible be spend over its completion.



it be midway in January when the day and hour of arrival be at last

appoint. no one be to be in the house but the servant. at four in

the afternoon Mr. and Mrs. Quarrier would receive Mr. and Mrs.

Liversedge, and thus make formal declaration of their readiness to

welcome friend. since her return to England, Lilian have see no one.

she beg Denzil not to invite Glazzard to Clapham.



they reach Polterham at one o'clock, in the tumult of a snowstorm;

ten minute more, and the whitened cab deposit they at their doorway.

Quarrier know, of course, what the general appearance of the interior

would be, and he be well satisfied with the way in which his

direction have be carry out. his companion be at first overawe

rather than pleased. he lead she from room to room, say frequently,

"do you like it? will it do?"



"it frighten I!" murmur Lilian, at length. "how shall I manage such

a house?"



she be pale, and incline to tearfulness, for the situation tired she

fortitude in a degree Denzil could not estimate. fear which be all

but terror, self-reproach which have the poignancy of remorse,

torment her gentle, timid nature. for a week and more she have not

know unbroken sleep; dream of fantastic misery awaken she to bad

distress in the calculating of her peril and conflict with insidious

doubt. at the dead hour before dawn, faith of childhood revive

before her conscience, upbraiding, menacing. the common rule of

every-day honour speak to she with stern reproval. Denzil's argument,

when she try to muster they in her defence, answer with hollow,

meaningless sound. love alone would stead she; she could but shut she

eye, and breathe, as if in prayer, the declaration that her love be a

sacred thing, cancel verbal untruth.



she change her dress, and go down to luncheon. the large dining-room

seem to oppress her insignificance; to eat be impossible, and with

difficulty she converse before the servant. fortunately, Denzil be

in his good spirit; he enjoy the wintery atmosphere, talk of

skating on the ice which have know he as a boy, laugh over an old

story about a snowball with a stone in it which have stun he in one

of the fight between town and Grammar School.



"Pity the election cann't come on just now!--we should have lively

time. a snowball be preferable to an addled egg any day. the Poltram

folks"--this be the common pronunciation of the town's name--"have a

liking for missile at season of excitement."



from table, they go to the library--as yet unfurnishe with

volume--and make themselves comfortable by the fireside. through the

window nothing could be see but a tempestuous whirl of flake.

Lilian's cat, which have accompany she in a basket, could not as yet

make itself at home on the hearthrug, and be glad of a welcome to its

mistress's lap. Denzil light a pipe and study the political news of the

day.



at four o'clock he wait impatiently the call of his relative.

Lilian, unable to command her agitation, have go into another room,

and be there count the minute as if each cost she a drop of

heart's blood. if this first meeting be but over! all else seem

easy, could she but face Denzil's sister without betrayal of her shame

and dread. at length she hear wheel roll up to the door; there be

voice in the hall; Denzil come forth with loud and joyous greeting; he

lead his visitor into the library. five minute more of anguish, and

the voice be again audible, approach, at the door.



"well, Lily, here be my sister and Mr. Liversedge," say Denzil. "no

very formidable person, either of they," he add merrily, as the good

way of make apology for Lilian's too obvious tremor.



but she conquer her weakness. the man be of no account to she; upon

the woman only her eye be fix, for _there_ be the pierce

scrutiny, the quick divination, the merciless censure--there, if

anywhere, in one of her own sex. from man she might expect tolerance,

justice; from woman only a swift choice between the bowl and the

dagger. Pride prompt she to hardihood, and when she have well look

upon Mrs. Liversedge's face a soothe confidence come to the support

of desperation. she see the frank fairness of Denzil's lineament

soften with the kind of female smile; a gaze keen indeed, but

ingenuous as that of a child; an expression impossible to be

interpret save as that of heartfelt welcome, absolutely unsuspecting,

touch even with admire homage.



they kiss each other, and Lilian's face glow. after that, she could

turn almost joyously for Mr. Liversedge's hearty hand-shake.



"you have come like a sort of snow-queen," say Tobias, with unusual

imaginativeness, point to the window. "it must have begin just as

you get here."



perhaps the chill of her finger prompt he to this poetical flight.

his wife, who have notice the same thing, add, with practical fervour:



"I only hope the house be thoroughly dry. we have have great fire

everywhere for more than a fortnight. as for the snow and frost, you

be pretty well use to that, no doubt."



painfully on the alert, Lilian of course understand this allusion to

the northern land she be suppose to have quit recently.



"even at Stockholm," she reply, with a smile, "there be summer, you

know."



"and in Russia, too, I have hear," laugh Mr. Liversedge. "but one

don't put much faith in such report. Denzil try to persuade we now

and then that the North Cape have quite a balmy atmosphere, especially

from December to March. he be quite safe. we sha'n't go to test his

statement."



instead of a time of misery, this first half-hour prove so pleasant

that Lilian all but forget the shadow stand behind she. when tea be

bring in, she feel none of the nervousness which have seem to she

inevitable amid such luxurious appliance. these relative of Denzil's,

henceforth her own, be people such as she have not dare to picture

they--so unaffected, genial, easy to talk with; nor do she suffer from

a necessity of utter direct falsehood; conversation deal with the

present and the future--partly, no doubt, owe to Quarrier's

initiative. Mr. Liversedge make a report of local affair as they

concern the political outlook; he see every reason for hope.



"Welwyn-Baker," he say, "be quite set up again, and I be tell he have

no inclination to retire in favour of his son, or any one else. an

obstinate old fellow--and may his obstinacy increase! the Tories be

begin to see that they ought to set up a new man; they be

quarrel among themselves. that bazaar at the opening of the new

Society's room--the Constitutional Literary, you know--seem to have

be a failure. no one be satisfied. the _Mercury_ print savage

letter from a lot of people--blame this, that, and the other person

in authority. the _Examiner_, chuckle, and haven't do refer to

the matter yet."



apart with Lilian, Mrs. Liversedge have begin to talk of the society of

Polterham. she do not try to be witty at the expense of her

neighbour, but confess with a sly smile that literature and the art

be not quite so well appreciate as might be wish.



"you be a serious student, I know--very learn in language. I wish I

have have more time for reading, and a well head. but seven child,

you know--oh dear! even my little bit of French have get so ragged that

I be really ashamed of it. but there _be_ one woman who study. have

Denzil speak to you of Mrs. Wade?"



"I don't remember."



"she be no great favourite of his, I believe. you will soon hear of

she, and no doubt see she. Denzil admit that she be very clever--even

a greek scholar!"



"really! and what fault do he find with she?"



"she be a great supporter of woman's right, and occasionally make

speech. it's only of late that I have see much of she; for some

reason she seem to have take a liking to I, and I feel rather

honour. I'm sure her intention be very good indeed, and it must be

try to live among people who have no sympathy with you. they make

sad fun of she, and altogether misunderstand she--at least I think so."



the snowstorm still rage. to spare their own horse, the Liversedges

have come in a cab, and at half-past five the same vehicle return to

take they home. Lilian be sorry to see they go.



"where be all your apprehension now?" cry Denzil, come back to

she from the hall. "it's over, you see. not another minute's uneasiness

need you have!"



"they be kindness itself. I like they very much."



"as I know and say you would. now, no more chalky face and frightened

look! be jolly, and forget everything. let we try your piano."



"your sister be tell I about Mrs. Wade. be she one of the people

you would like I to be friend with?"



"oh yes!" he answer, laugh, "Mrs. Wade will interest you, no

doubt. make a friend of she by all mean. do Mary whisper mysterious

warning?"



"anything but that; she speak very favourably."



"indeed!



"and she say Mrs. Wade seem to have take a liking to she lately."



"oh! how's that, I wonder? she go about seek whom she may secure

for the women's-vote movement; I suppose it's Molly's turn to be

attack. oh, we shall have many a lively half-hour when Mrs. Wade

call!"



"what be her husband?"



"Husband! she's a widow. I never think of such a person as Mr. Wade,

to this moment. to be sure, he must have exist. perhaps she will

confide in you, and then----By-the-bye, be it right for woman to tell

their husband what they learn from female friend?"



he ask it jokingly, but Lilian seem to reflect in earnest.



"I'm not sure"----



"oh, you lily of the valley!" he cry, interrupt she. "do cultivate

a sense of humour. don't take thing with such desperate seriousness!

come and try your instrument. it ought to be a good one, if price-list

mean anything."



the next morning be clear and cold. assuredly there would be good

skating, and the prospect of this enjoyment seem to engross Denzil's

thought. after breakfast he barely glance at the newspaper, then

leave Lilian to enter upon her domestic rule, set forth for an

examination of the locality which offer scope to Polterham skater.

such youthful zeal prove his thorough harmony with the english spirit;

it promise far more for his success as a politician than if he have

spend the morning over blue-book and statistical treatise.



if only the snow be clear away, the good skating near at hand be

on a piece of water near the road to Rickstead. the origin of this pond

or lakelet have cause discussion among local antiquary; for tradition

say that it occupy the site of a meadow which many year ago

mysteriously sink, owe perhaps to the unsuspected existence of an

ancient mine. it connect with a little tributary of the River Bale,

and be believe to be very deep, especially at one point, where the

tree-shadow bank overhang the water at a height of some ten foot. the

way thither be by a field-path, start from the high road within

sight of pear-tree cottage. at a rapid walk Quarrier soon reach his

goal, and see with satisfaction that man and boy be sweep the

snowy surface, whilst a few people have already begin to disport

themselves where the black ice come to view. in the afternoon he would

come with Lilian; for the present, a second purpose occupy his

thought. stand on the bank of Bale Water (thus be it name), he

could see the topmost branch of that pear-tree which grow in the

garden behind Mrs. Wade's cottage; two meadow lay between--a stretch

of about a quarter of a mile. it be scarcely the hour for call upon

lady, but he know that Mrs. Wade sit among her book through the

morning, and he wish especially to see she as soon as possible.



Polterham clock be count eleven as he present himself at the

door of the cottage. once already he have pay a call here, not many

day after his meeting with the widow in Mr. Hornibrook's library; he

come at three in the afternoon, and sit talk till nearly six. not a

few Polterham matron would have consider that proceed highly

improper, but such a thought never occur to Denzil; and Mrs. Wade

would have speak her mind very distinctly to any one who wish to

circumscribe female freedom in such respect. they have converse on a

great variety of subject with unflagge animation. since then he have

not see his acquaintance.



a young girl open to he, and leave he stand in the porch for a

minute or two. she return, and ask he to walk into the

sitting-room, where Mrs. Wade be study with her foot on the fender.



"do I come unseasonably?" he ask, offer his hand.



"not if you have anything interesting to say," be the curious reply.



the widow be not accoutre for reception of visitor. she wear an old

though quite presentable dress, with a light shawl about her shoulder,

and have evidently postpone the arrangement of her hair until the time

of go abroad. yet her appearance could hardly be call

disconcerting, for it have nothing of slovenliness. she look a

student, that be all. for some reason, however, she give Quarrier a

less cordial welcome than he have anticipate. her eye avoid his, she

shake hand in a perfunctory way.



"it depend what you call interesting," be his rejoinder to the

unconventional reply. "I get here yesterday, and bring a wife with

I--there, at all event, be a statement of fact."



"you have do I the honour to hasten here with the announcement?"



"I come out to see if Bale Water be skateable, and I think I might

venture to make a friendly call whilst I be so near. but I'm afraid I

disturb you?"



"not a bit. pray sit down and talk. of course I have hear of your

marriage. why don't you let I know it be impend?"



"because I tell nobody. I choose to get marry in my own way. you, Mrs.

Wade, be not likely to find fault with I for that."



"oh dear no!" she answer, with friendly indifference.



"I be tell you see a good deal of the Liversedges?"



she nod.



"do my sister give any promise of reach high level? or be she a

hopeless groveller?"



"Mrs. Liversedge be the kind of woman I can respect, independently of

her view."



"I like to hear you say that, because I know you don't deal in

complimentary phrase. the respect, I be sure, be reciprocate."



Mrs. Wade seem to give slight attention; she be look at a picture

above the fireplace.



"you will count my wife among your friend, I hope?" he continue.



"I hope so. do you think we shall understand each other?"



"if not, it willn't be for lack of good will on her side. I mustn't begin

to praise she, but I think you will find she have a very fair portion of

brain."



"I'm glad to hear that."



"do you imply that you have fear?"



"man be occasionally odd in their choice of wife."



"yes," Denzil reply, with a laugh; "I have see remarkable

illustration of it."



"I don't feel sure that you regard brain as an essential."



"indeed! then you be a long way from understand I. how can you

say that, after my lecture, and our talk?"



"oh, theory don't go for much. may I call shortly?"



"if you will be so good."



"she's very young, I think?"



"not much more than one-and-twenty. I have know she for about three

year."



there be a short silence, then Mrs. Wade say with some abruptness:



"I think of leave Polterham before long. it be Mr. and Mrs.

Hornibrook who decide I to come here, and now that they be go I

feel as if I too have well stir. I want book that be out of my

reach."



"that will be a loss to we, Mrs. Wade. Society in Polterham have its

limitations"----



"I'm aware of it. but you, of course, will have a home in London as

well?"



"well, yes--if I get send to Parliament."



"I suppose we shall meet there some day."



her voice grow careless and dreamy. she fold her hand upon her lap,

and assume a look which seem to Denzil a hint that he might now

depart. he stand up.



"so you be go to skate?" murmur Mrs. Wade. "I willn't keep you.

thank you very much for look in."



Denzil try once more to read her countenance, and go away with a

puzzled feeling. he could not conjecture the meaning of she change

tone.









CHAPTER XIII





last November have turn the scale in the Polterham Town Council. it

happen that the retire member be all conservative, with the

exception of Mr. Chown, who alone of they obtain re-election, the

other give place to man of the Progressive party. Mr. Mumbray bade

farewell to his greatness. the new Mayor be a Liberal. as

return-officer, he would preside over the come political contest.

the Tories gloome at each other, and whisper of evil omen.



for many year Mr. Mumbray have look to the Mayoralty as the limit of

his ambition. he now begin to entertain large project, encourage

thereto by the dissension of Conservative Polterham, and the

prompting of man who be hope to follow he up the civic ladder. he

join with those who murmur against the obstinacy of old Mr.

Welwyn-Baker. to support such a candidate would be party suicide. even

Welwyn-Baker junior be preferable; but why not recognize that the old

name have lose its prestige, and select a representative of enlighten

Conservatism, who could really make a stand against Quarrier and his

rampant radical? Mr. Mumbray see no reason why he himself should not

invite the confidence of the burgess.



in a moment of domestic truce the ex-Mayor communicate this thought to

his wife, and Mrs. Mumbray give ready ear. like the lady of Polterham

in general, she have not the faint understanding of political

principle; to she, the distinction between party be the difference

between bit of blue and yellow ribbon, nothing more. but the social

advantage accrue to the wife of an M.P. impress she very strongly

indeed. for such an end she be willing to make sacrifice, and the

first of these declare itself in an abandonment of her opposition to

Mr. Eustace Glazzard. her husband point out to she that a connection

with the family so long establish at Highmead would be of distinct

value. William Glazzard nominally stand on the liberal side, but he be

very lukewarm, and allow to be see that his political action be

much sway by personal consideration. eustace make no pretence of

liberal lean; though a friend of the radical candidate (so Quarrier

be already designate by his opponent), he joke at popular

enthusiasm, and could only be describe as an independent aristocrat.

money, it appear, he have none; and his brother, it be suspect,

keep up only a show of the ancestral position. nevertheless, their

name have weight in the borough.



Eustace spend Christmas at Highmead, and make frequent call at the

house of the ex-Mayor. on one of the occasion it happen that the

lady be from home, but Mr. Mumbray, on the point of go out,

beg Glazzard to come and have a word with he in his sanctum. after

much roundabout talk, characteristically pompous, he put the question

whether Mr. Glazzard, as a friend of Mr. Denzil Quarrier, would "take

it ill" if he, Mr. Mumbray, accept an invitation to come forward as

the candidate of the conservative party.



"I hope you know I well," Glazzard reply. "I have nothing whatever

to do with politic."



the ex-Mayor smile thoughtfully, and go on to explain, "in strict

confidence," that there _be_ a prospect of that contingency befalling.



"of course I couldn't hope for Mr. William's support."



he pause on a note of magnanimous renunciation.



"oh, I don't know," say Glazzard, abstractedly. "my brother be hardly

to be call a Radical. I couldn't answer for the line he will take."



"indeed? that be very interesting. ha!"



Silence fall between they.



"I'm sure," remark Mr. Mumbray, at length, "that my wife and daughter

will be very sorry to have miss your call. undoubtedly you can count

on their be at home to-morrow."



the prediction be fulfil, and before leave the house Glazzard

make Serena a proposal of marriage. that morning there have occur a

quarrel of more than usual bitterness between mother and daughter.

Serena be sick of her life at home, and feel a longing, at any cost,

for escape to a sphere of independence. the expected offer from

Glazzard come just at the right moment; she accept it, and consent

that the marriage should be very soon.



but a few hour of reflection fill she with grave misgiving. she be

not in love with Glazzard; personally, he have never charm she, and in

the progress of their acquaintance she have discover many point of

his character which excite her alarm. Serena, after all, be but a

half-educate country girl; even in the whirlwind of rebellious moment

she feel afraid of the word that come to her lip. the impulse

towards emancipation which so grievously perturb she be unjustified

by her conscience; at heart, she believe with Ivy Glazzard that woman

be a pray and subordinate creature; in her bedroom she recount

the day's sin of thought and speech, and weep out her desire for

"conversion," for the life of humble faith. accept such a husband as

Eustace, she have commit not only an error, but a sin. the man be

without religion, and sometimes make himself guilty of hypocrisy; of

this she feel a miserable assurance. how could she hope to be happy

with he? what have interest she in he be that air of culture and

refinement so conspicuously lack by the man who have hitherto

approach she. he have seem to she the first _gentleman_ who seek

her favour. to countenance he, moreover, be to defy her mother's

petty rule. but, no, she do not love he--do not like he.



yet to retract her promise she be ashamed. only girl of low social

position play fast and loose in that way. she go through a night of

misery.



on the morrow she betroth, of course, come to see she. woman-like,

she have take refuge in a resolve of postponement; the marriage must be

soon or later, but it be in her power to put it off. and, with show

of regretful prudence, she make known this change in her mind.



"I hardly know what I be say. I ought to have remember that our

acquaintance have be very short."



"yet long enough to enable I to win your promise," urge Glazzard.



"yes, I have promise. it's only that we cannot be marry so very

soon."



"I must, of course, yield," he reply, gracefully, kiss her hand.

"decision as to the time shall rest entirely with you."



"thank you--that be very kind."



he go away in a mood of extreme discontent. be this little simpleton

go to play with he? there be solid reason of more than one kind

why the marriage should not be long delay. it would be good if he

return to London and communicate with she by letter. he could write

eloquently, and to let she think of he as in the midst of gay society

might not be amiss.



shortly after Quarrier's arrival at Polterham, he be back again. daily

he have repent his engagement, yet as often have congratulate himself

on the windfall thus assure to he. before go to the Mumbrays, he

call upon Mrs. Quarrier, whom, as it chance, he find alone. to

lilian his appearance be a shock, for in the contentment of the past

week she have practically forget the existence of this man who share

her secret. she could not look he in the face.



Glazzard could be trust in point of tact. he enter with a bright

face, and the greeting of an old friend, then at once begin to speak

of his own affair.



"have you hear that I be go to be marry?"



"Denzil tell I when he receive your letter."



"I be afraid Miss Mumbray will hardly belong to your circle, but as

Mrs. Glazzard--that will be a different thing. you willn't forbid I to

come here because of this alliance?"



Lilian show surprise and perplexity.



"I mean, because I be engaged to the daughter of a Tory."



"oh, what difference could that possibly make?"



"none, I hope. you know that I be not very zealous as a party-man."



in this his second conversation with Lilian, Glazzard analyse more

completely the charm which she have before exercise upon he. he be

thoroughly aware of the trial her nature be endure, and his power

of sympathetic insight enable he to read upon her countenance, in her

tone, precisely what Lilian imagine she could conceal. amid

surrounding such as those of the newly furnish house, she seem to

he a priceless gem in a gaudy setting; he feel (and with justice) that

the little drawing-room at Clapham, which speak in so many detail of

her own taste, be a much more suitable home for she. what could be

say of the man who have thus transfer she, all (or chiefly) for the

sake of getting elect to Parliament? Quarrier have no true

appreciation of the woman with whose life and happiness he be

entrust. he be devoted to she, no doubt, but with a devotion not

much more clairvoyant than would have distinguish one of his

favourite viking.



Glazzard, whilst like Denzil, have never hold he in much esteem. of

late, his feeling have become strongly tinge with contempt. and now,

with the contempt there blend a strain of jealousy.



true that he himself have catch eagerly at the hope of enter

Parliament; but it be the impulse of a man who know his life to be

fall into ruin, who welcome any suggestion that would save he from

final and fatal apathy--of a man whose existence have always be

loveless--who, with passionate ideal, have never know anything but a

venal embrace. in Quarrier's position, with abound resource, with

the love of such a woman as this, what would he not have make of life?

would it ever have occur to _he_ to wear a mask of vulgar deceit,

to condemn his exquisite companion to a hateful martyrdom, that he

might attain the dizzy height of M.P.-ship for Polterham?



he compassionate Lilian, and at the same time he be angry with she.

he look upon her beauty, her gentle spirit, with tenderness, and

therewithal he half hope that she might some day repent of yield to

Quarrier's vulgar ambition.



"have you make many acquaintance?" he ask.



"a good many. some, very pleasant people; other--not so interesting."



"Polterham society will not absorb you, I think."



"I hope to have a good deal of quiet time. but Denzil wish I to

study more from life than from book, just now. I must understand all

the subject that interest he."



"yes--the exact position, as a force in politic, of the licensed

victualler; the demand of the newly enfranchised class--that kind

of thing."



he seem to be jest, and she laugh good-humouredly.



"those thing be very important, Mr. Glazzard."



"Infinitely!"



he do not stay long, and upon his departure Lilian give a sigh of

relief.



the next day he be to lunch with the Mumbrays. he go about twelve

o'clock, to spend an hour with Serena. his welcome be not ardent, and

he feel the oppression of a languor be hardly try to disguise. yet in

truth his cause have benefit whilst he be away. the eloquent letter

do not fail of their effect; Serena have again sigh under domestic

tyranny, have think with longing of a life in London, and be once

more sway by her emotion towards an early marriage.



in dearth of matter for conversation (glazzard sit taciturn), she

speak of an event which have occupy Polterham for the last day or two.

some local genius have conceive the idea of wreck an express train,

and to that end have break a portion of the line.



"what frightful wickedness!" she exclaim. "what motive can there have

be, do you think?"



"probably none, in the sense you mean."



"yes--such a man must be mad."



"I don't think that," say Glazzard, meditatively. "I can understand

his do it with no reason at all but the wish to see what would

happen. no doubt he would have be stand somewhere in sight."



"you can _understand_ that?"



"very well indeed," he answer, in the same half-absent way. "power of

all kind be a temptation to man. a certain kind of man--not

necessarily cruel--would be fascinate with the thought of bring

about such a terrific end by such slight mean."



"not necessarily cruel? oh, I cann't follow you at all. you be not

serious."



"I have shock you." he see that he have really do so, and feel that

it be imprudent. his tact suggest a use for the situation. "Serena,

why should you speak so conventionally? you be not really conventional

in mind. you have thought and emotion infinitely above those of

average girl. do recognize your own superiority. I speak in a

speculative way. one may speculate about anything and everything--if

one have the brain. you certainly be not make to go through life with

veiled eye and a tongue tune to the common phrase. do yourself

justice, dear girl. however other people regard you, I from the first

have see what it be in you to become."



it be adroit flattery; Serena redden, avert her face, smile a

little, and keep silence.



that day he do not follow up his advantage. but on take leave of

Serena early in the afternoon, he look into her eye with expressive

steadiness, and again she blush.



a little later, several lady be gather in the drawing-room. on

Thursdays Mrs. Mumbray receive her friend; sit as an embodiment of

the domestic virtue and grace. to-day the talk be principally on

that recent addition to Polterham society, Mrs. Denzil Quarrier.



"I haven't see she yet," say Mrs. Mumbray, with her air of

superiority. "they say she be pretty but rather childish."



"but what be this mystery about the marriage?" inquire a lady who have

just enter, and who throw herself upon the subject with eagerness.

(it be Mrs. Roach, the wife of an alderman.) "why be it abroad? she

be English, I think?"



"oh no!" put in Mrs. Tenterden, a large and very positive person. "she

be a dane--like the Princess of Wales. I have see she. I recognize

the cast of feature at once."



an outcry from three lady follow. they know Mrs. Quarrier be

English. they have see her skating at Bale Water. one of they have hear

her speak--it be pure English.



"I think every one know," return Mrs. Tenterden, with stately

deliberation, "that the Danes have a special gift for language. the

Princess of Wales"----



"but, indeed," urge the hostess, "she be of english birth. we know it

from Mr. Eustace Glazzard, who be one of their friend."



"then _why_ be they married abroad?" come in Mrs. Roach's shrill

voice. "_can_ english people be legitimately married abroad? I always

understand that the ceremony have to be repeat in England."



"it be at Paris," say Mrs. Walker, the depressed widow of a bankrupt

corn-merchant. "there be an english church there, I have hear."



the other, incline to be contemptuous of this authority, regard

each other with doubt.



"still," break out Mrs. Roach again, "_why_ be it at Paris? no one

seem to have the slight idea. it be really very strange!"



Mrs. Mumbray vouchsafe further information.



"I understand that she come from Stockholm."



"don't I _say_ she come from Denmark?" interrupt Mrs. Tenterden,

triumphantly.



there be a pause of uncertainty break by Serena Mumbray's quiet voice.



"Dear Mrs. Tenterden, Stockholm be not in Denmark, but in Sweden. and

we be tell that Mrs. Quarrier be an english governess there."



"ah! a governess!" cry two or three voice.



"to tell the truth," say Mrs. Mumbray, more dignified than ever after

her vindication, "it be probable that she belong to some very poor

family. I should be sorry to think any bad of she for _that_, but it

would explain the private marriage."



"so you think people _can_ be marry legally in Paris?" persist the

alderman's wife, whose bann have be proclaim in hearing of orthodox

Polterham about a year ago.



"of course they can," fall from Serena.



Lilian's age, personal appearance, dress, behaviour, underwent

discussion at great length.



"what church do they go to?" inquire some one, and the question

excited general interest.



"they be at St. Luke's last Sunday," Mrs. Walker be able to declare,

though her wonte timidity again throw some suspicion on the statement.



"St. Luke's! why St. Luke's?" cry other voice. "it ben't their

parish, be it?"



"I think," suggest the widow, "it may be because the Liversedges go

to St. Luke's. Mrs. Liversedge is"----



her needless information be cut short by a remark from Mrs. Tenterden.



"I could never listen Sunday after Sunday to Mr. Garraway. I think he

excessively tedious. and his voice be so very try."



the incumbent of St. Luke's offer a brief diversion from the main

theme. a mention of the Rev. Scatchard Vialls threaten to lead they

too far, and Mrs. Roach interpose with firmness.



"I still think it a very singular thing that they go abroad to be

married."



"but they _don't_ go abroad, my dear," object the hostess. "that be

to say, one of they be already abroad."



"indeed! the whole thing seem very complicated. I think it need

explanation. I shouldn't feel justified in call upon Mrs. Quarrier

until"----



her voice be overpower by that of Mrs. Tenterden, who demand

loudly:



"be it true that she have already become very intimate with _that

person_ Mrs. Wade?"



"oh, I _do_ hope not!" exclaim several lady.



here be an inexhaustible topic. it occupy more than an hour, until

the last tea-cup have be lay aside and the more discreet caller be

already on their way home.









CHAPTER XIV





there need only two or three day of life at Polterham to allay the

uneasiness with which, for all his show of equanimity, Denzil enter

upon so perilous a career. by the end of January he have practically

forget that his position be in any respect insecure. the risk of

betray himself in an unguarded moment be diminish by the mental

habit establish during eighteen month of secrecy in London. Lilian's

name be seldom upon his lip, and any inquiry concern she at once

awaken his caution. between themselves they never speak of the past.



long ago he have silence every conscientious scruple regard the

relation between Lilian and himself; and as for the man Northway, if

ever he think of he at all, it be with impatient contempt. that he

be deceive his Polterham acquaintance, and in a way which they

would deem an unpardonable outrage, no long cause he the least

compunction. conventional wrong doing, he have satisfy himself, be

not wrong-do at all, unless discover. he injure no one. the

society of such a person as Lilian could be nothing but an advantage to

man, woman, and child. only the sublimation of imbecile prejudice would

maintain that she be an unfit companion for the pure creature

living. he have even cease to smile at the success of his stratagem. it

be over and do with; their social standing be unassailable.



anxious to complete his book on the Vikings, he work at it for

several hour each morning; it would be off his hand some time in

February, and the spring publishing season should send it forth to the

world. the rest of his leisure be give to politic. chest of volume

be arrive from London, and his library shelf begin to make a

respectable appearance; as a matter of principle, he buy largely

from the local bookseller, who rejoice at the sudden fillip to his

stagnant trade, and go about declare that Mr. Denzil Quarrier be

evidently _the_ man for the borough.



he fall upon history, economic, social speculation, with

characteristic vigour. if he get into the House of Commons, those

worthy should speedily be aware of his existence among they. it be

one of his favourite boast that whatever subject he choose to tackle,

he could master. no smattering for he; a solid foundation of

knowledge, such as would ensure authority to his light utterance.



in the meantime, he begin to perceive that Lilian be not likely to

form many acquaintance in the town. with the Liversedges she stand on

excellent term, and one or two family closely connect with they

give she a welcome from which she do not shrink. but she have no gift

of social versatility; it cost her painful effort to converse about

bazaar and curate and fashion and baby with the average Polterham

matron; she feel that most of the woman who come to see she go away

with distasteful impression, and that they be anything but cordial

when she return their call. a life of solitude and study be the

bad possible preparation for duty such as be now lay upon she.



"you be dissatisfied with I," she say to Denzil, as they return

from spend the evening with some empty but influential people who

have make she exceedingly uncomfortable.



"dissatisfied? on the contrary, I be very proud of you. it do one

good to contrast one's wife with woman such as those."



"I try to talk; but I'm so ignorant of everything they care about. I

shall do well when I know more of the people they refer to."



"chattering ape! malicious idiot! Heaven forbid that you should ever

take a sincere part in their gabble! that lot be about the bad we

shall have to deal with. decent simpleton you can get along with very

well."



"how ought I to speak of Mrs. Wade? when people tell downright

falsehood about she, may I contradict?"



"it's a confoundedly difficult matter, that. I half wish Mrs. Wade

would hasten her departure. do she say anything about it when you see

she the other day?"



"nothing whatever."



it appear that the widow wish to make a friend of Lilian. she have

call several time, and on each occasion behave so charmingly that

Lilian be very ready to meet her advance. though on intellectual and

personal ground he could feel no objection to such an intimacy, Denzil

begin to fear that it might affect his popularity with some voter who

would take the liberal side if it do not commit they to social

heresy. this class be a very large one throughout England. Mrs. Wade

have never give occasion of grave scandal; she be even see, with

moderate regularity, at one or other of the church; but many of the

anti-tory bourgeois suspect she of sympathy with view so very

"advanced" as to be socially dangerous. already it have become know

that she be on good term with Quarrier and his wife. it be rumour

that Quarrier would reconsider the position he have publicly assume,

and stand forth as an advocate of Female Suffrage. for such extreme

Polterham be not prepare.



"Mrs. Wade ask I to go and have tea with she to-morrow," Lilian

announce one morning, show a note. "shall I, or not?"



"you would like to?"



"not if you think it unwise."



"Hang it!--we cann't be slave. go by all mean, and refresh your mind."



at three o'clock on the day of invitation Lilian alight from her

brougham at pear-tree cottage. it be close upon the end of February;

the decline sun shoot a pleasant glow across the landscape, and in the

air reign a perfect stillness. Mrs. Wade throw open the door herself

with laugh welcome.



"let we have half-an-hour's walk, shall we? it's so dry and warm."



"I should enjoy it," Lilian answer, readily.



"then allow I two minute for bonnet and cloak."



she be scarcely long. they go by the hedge-side path which lead

towards Bale Water. to-day the paper be full of exciting news. Sir

Stafford Northcote have bring forward his resolution for make short

work of obstructive member, and Radicalism stand undecided. Mrs. Wade

talk of these thing in the lively strain, Lilian respond with

a lighthearted freedom seldom possible to she.



"you skated here, don't you?" say her companion, as they draw near to

the large pond.



"yes; a day or two after we come. how different it look now."



they stand on the bank where it rise to a considerable height above the

water.



"the rail have spoil this spot," say Mrs. Wade. "they be only put

up last autumn, after an accident. I wonder it be never find

necessary before. some child be gather blackberry from the

bramble there, and one of they reach too far forward, and over she

go! I witness it from the other side, where I happen to be

walk. a great splash, and then a chorus of shriek from the

companion. I begin to run forward, though of course I could have do

nothing whatever; when all at once I see a splendid sight. a man who

be stand not far off run to the edge and plunge in--a magnificent

'header!' he have only throw away his hat and coat. they say it's very

deep just here. he disappear completely, and then in a few second I

see that he have hold of the child. he bring she out where the bank

slope yonder--no harm do. I cann't tell you how I enjoy that scene.

it make I cry with delight."



as usual, when deeply move, Lilian stand in a reverie, her eye wide,

her lip tremulous. then she step forward, and, with her hand

rest upon the wooden rail, look down. there be no perceptible

movement in the water; it show a dark greenish surface, smooth to the

edge, without a trace of weed.



"how I envy that man his courage!"



"his power, rather," suggest Mrs. Wade. "if we could swim well, and

have no foolish petticoat, we should jump in just as readily. it be

the power over circumstance that I admire and envy."



Lilian smile thoughtfully.



"I suppose that be what most attract we in man?"



"and make we feel our own dependence. I cann't say I like _that_

feeling--do you?"



she seem to wait for an answer.



"I'm afraid it's in the order of nature," reply Lilian at length with

a laugh.



"very likely. but I be not content with it on that account. I know of a

thousand thing quite in the order of nature which revolt I. I very

often think of nature as an evil force, at war with the good principle

of which we be conscious in our soul."



"but," Lilian falter, "be your ideal an absolute independence?"



Mrs. Wade look far across the water, and answer, "yes, absolute!"



"then you--I don't quite know what would result from that."



"nor I," return the other, laugh. "that don't affect my ideal.

you have hear, of course, of that lecture your husband give at the

Institute before--before your marriage?"



"yes; I wish I could have hear it."



"you would have sympathize with every word, I be sure. Mr. Quarrier be

one of the strong man who find satisfaction in woman's weakness."



it be say with perfect good-humour, with a certain indulgent

kindness--a tone Mrs. Wade have use from the first in talk with

Lilian. a manner of affectionate playfulness, occasionally of caress

protection, distinguish she in this intercourse; quite unlike that by

which she be know to people in general. Lilian do not dislike it,

rather be draw by it into a mood of grateful confidence.



"I don't think 'weakness' express it," she object. "he like woman

to be subordinate, no doubt of that. his idea be that"----



"I know, I know!" Mrs. Wade turn away with a smile her companion do

not observe. "let we walk back again; it grow chilly. a beautiful

sunset, if cloud don't gather. perhaps it surprise you that I care

for such sentimental thing?"



"I think I understand you well."



"frankly--do you think I what the French call _hommasse_? just a

little?"



"nothing of the kind, Mrs. Wade," Lilian reply, with courage. "you

be a very womanly woman."



the bright, hard eye dart a quick glance at she.



"really? that be how I strike you?"



"it be, indeed."



"how I like your way of speak," say the other, after a moment's

pause. "I mean, your voice--accent. have it anything to do with the long

time you have spend abroad, I wonder?"



Lilian smile and be embarrassed.



"you be certainly not a Londoner?"



"oh no! I be bear in the west of England."



"and I at Newcastle. as a child I have a strong northern accent; you

don't notice anything of it now? oh, I have be about so much. my

husband be in the Army. that be the first time I have mention he to

you, and it will be the last, however long we know each other."



Lilian keep her eye on the ground. the widow glance off to a totally

different subject, which occupy they the rest of the way back to the

cottage.



Daylight last until they have finish tea, then a lamp be bring in

and the red blind draw down. Quarrier have go to spend the day at a

neighbouring town, and would not be back before late in the evening, so

that Lilian have arrange to go from Mrs. Wade's to the liversedge'.

they still have a couple of hour' talk to enjoy; on Lilian's side, at

all event, it be unfeigned enjoyment. the cosy little room put she at

ease. its furniture be quite in keep with the simple appearance of

the house, but book and picture tell that no ordinary cottager dwelt

here.



"I have have many an hour of happiness in this room," say Mrs. Wade, as

they seat themselves by the fire. "the good of all between eleven at

night and two in the morning. you know the line in 'Penseroso.' Most

man would declare that a woman cann't possibly appreciate they; I know

well. I be by nature a student; the life of society be nothing to I;

and, in reality, I care very little about politic."



smile, she watch the effect of her word.



"you be content with solitude?" say Lilian, gaze at she with a look

of deep interest.



"quite. I have no relative who care anything about I, and only two or

three people I call friend. but I must have more book, and I shall be

oblige to go to London."



"don't go just yet--won't our book be of use to you?"



"I shall see. have you read this?"



it be a novel from Smith's Library. Lilian know it, and they discuss

its merit. Mrs. Wade mention a book by the same author which have

appear more than a year ago.



"yes, I read that when it come out," say Lilian, and begin to talk of

it.



Mrs. Wade keep silence, then remark carelessly:



"you have they in the Tauchnitz series, I suppose?"



have her eye be turn that way, she must have observe the strange

look which flash across her companion's countenance. Lilian seem to

draw in her breath, though silently.



"yes--Tauchnitz," she answer.



Mrs. Wade appear quite unconscious of anything unusual in the tone.

she be gaze at the fire.



"it ben't often I find time for novel," she say; "for new one, that

be. a few of the old be generally all I need. can you read George

Eliot? what a miserably conventional soul that woman have!"



"Conventional? But"----



"oh, I know! but she be british conventionality to the core. I have

hear people say that she haven't the courage of her opinion; but that

be precisely what she _have_, and every page of her work declare it

flagrantly. she might have be a great power--she might have speed

the revolution of moral--if the true faith have be in she."



Lilian be still tremulous, and she listen with an intensity which

give she a look of pain. she be about to speak, but Mrs. Wade

anticipate she.



"you mustn't trouble much about anything I say when it cross your own

judgment or feeling. there be so few people with whom I can indulge

myself in free speech. I talk just for the pleasure of it; don't think

I expect or hope that you will always go along with I. but you be not

afraid of think--that's the great thing. Most woman be such paltry

creature that they daren't look into their own mind--for fear nature

should have put something 'improper' there."



she break off with laughter, and, as Lilian keep silence, fall into

thought.



in say that she think her companion a "womanly woman," Lilian tell

the truth. ever quick with sympathy, she feel a sadness in Mrs. Wade's

situation, which lead she to interpret all her harsh peculiarity as

the result of disappointment and loneliness. now that the widow have

confess her ill-fortune in marriage, Lilian be assure of having

judge rightly, and nurse her sentiment of compassion. Mrs. Wade be

still young; impossible that she should have accept a fate which

forbid she the knowledge of woman's happiness. but how difficult for

such a one to escape from this narrow and misleading way! her strong,

highly-train intellect could find no satisfaction in the society of

every-day people, yet she be withhold by poverty from seek her

natural sphere. with Lilian, to understand a sorrow be to ask herself

what she could do for its assuagement. a thought of characteristic

generosity come to she. why should she not (some day or other, when

their friendship be mature) offer Mrs. Wade the money, her own

property, which would henceforth be lie idle? there would be

practical difficulty in the way, but surely they might be overcome.

the idea bring a smile to her face. yes; she would think of this. she

would presently talk of it with Denzil.



"come now," say Mrs. Wade, rouse herself from meditation, "let we

talk about the irish question."



Lilian address herself conscientiously to the subject, but it do not

really interest she; she have no personal knowledge of irish hardship,

and be weary by the endless parliamentary debate. her thought still

busy themselves with the hopeful project for smooth Mrs. Wade's

path in life.



when the carriage come for she, she take her leave with regret, but

full of happy imagining. she have quite forget the all but

self-betrayal into which she be lead during that chat about novel.



two day later Quarrier be again absent from home on business, and

Lilian spend the evening with the Liversedges. supper be over, and she

have begin to think of departure, when the drawing-room door be burst

open, and in rush Denzil, wet from head to foot with rain, and his

face a-stream with perspiration.



"they dissolve at Easter!" he cry, wave his hat wildly. "Northcote

announce it at five this afternoon. Hammond have a telegram; I meet he

at the station."



"Ho! ho! this be news!" answer Mr. Liversedge, start up from his

easy-chair.



"News, indeed!" say his wife; "but that's no reason, Denzil, why you

should make my carpet all rain and mud. do go and take your coat off,

and clean your boot, there's a good boy!"



"how can I think of coat and boot? here, Lily, fle this garment

somewhere. give I a duster, or something, to stand on, molly. Toby, we

must have a meeting in a day or two. can we get the Public Hall for

Thursday or Friday? shall we go round and see our committee-man

to-night?"



"time enough to-morrow; most of they be just go to bed. but how be

it no one have an inkling of this? they have keep the secret uncommonly

well."



"the blackguard! ha, ha! now for a good fight! it'll be old

Welwyn-Baker, after all, you'll see. they willn't have the courage to set

up a new man at a moment's notice. the old buffer will come maudle

once more, and we'll bowl he off his pin!"



Lilian sit with her eye fix upon he. his excitement infect she,

and when they go home together she talk of the come struggle with

joyous animation.









CHAPTER XV





the next morning--Tuesday, March 9th--there be a rush for the London

paper. every copy that reach the Polterham vendor be snap up

within a few minute of its arrival. People who have no right of

membership run ravening to the Literary Institute and the

Constitutional Literary Society, and peer over the shoulder of

legitimate reader, on such a day as this unrebuked. Mr. Chown's

drapery establishment present a strange spectacle. for several hour

it be throng with sturdy Radicals eager to hear their eminent friend

hold forth on the situation. at eleven o'clock Mr. Chown fairly mount

a chair behind his counter, and deliver a formal harangue--thus, as

he boast, open the political campaign. he read aloud (for the

seventh time) Lord Beaconsfield's public letter to the Duke of

Marlborough, in which the country be warn, to begin with, against

the peril of Home Rule. "it be to be hope that all man of light and

leading will resist this destructive doctrine.... rarely in this

century have there be an occasion more critical. the power of England

and the peace of Europe will largely depend on the verdict of the

country.... peace rest on the presence, not to say the ascendancy, of

England in the Councils of Europe."



"here you have it," cry the orator, as he dash the newspaper to his

foot, "pure, unadulterated Jingoism! 'ascendancy in the Councils of

Europe!' how be the european power likely to hear _that_, do you

think? I venture to tell my Lord Beaconsfield--I venture to tell he on

behalf of this constituency--aye, and on behalf of this country--that

it be _he_ who hold 'destructive doctrine'! I venture to tell my Lord

Beaconsfield that England be not prepared to endorse any such insolent

folly! we shall very soon have an opportunity of hear how far such

doctrine recommend itself to _our_ man 'of light and leading'--to our

radical candidate--to our future member, Mr. Denzil Quarrier!"



a burst of cheering echo from the drapery-laden shelf. two

servant-girl who have come to the door intent on purchase of hair-pin

run frighten away, and spread a report that Mr. Chown's shop be on

fire.



at dinner-time the politician be face by his angry wife.



"I know what the end of _this_'ll be!" cry Mrs. Chown. "you're

ruin your business, that's what you're do! who do you think'll

come to the shop if they find it full of shout ragamuffin? they'll

all go to Huxtable's, that's what they'll do! I've no patience"----



"there's no need to declare _that_!" reply Mr. Chown, roll his

great eye at she with an expression of the lofty scorn. "I have

know it for thirteen year. you will be so good as to attend to your

own affair, and leave _I_ to see to _mine_! what do a woman care

for the interest of the country? grovel sex! perhaps when I be

call upon to shoulder a rifle and go forth to die on the field of

battle, your dense understanding will begin to perceive what be at

stake.--Not another syllable! I forbid it! sit down and serve the

potato!"



at the same hour Denzil Quarrier, at luncheon with Lilian, be give

utterance to his feeling on the great topic of the day.



"now be the time for woman to show whether their judgment be worthy of

the least confidence. this letter of Beaconsfield's make frank appeal

to the spirit of Jingoism; he hope to get at the fight side of

Englishmen, and go back to power on a wave of 'Rule, Britannia'

bluster. if it be true that woman be to be trust in politic, their

influence will be overwhelming against such irresponsible ambition. I

have my serious doubts"----



he shake his head and laugh.



"I will do my utmost!" exclaim Lilian, her face glow with

sympathetic enthusiasm. "I will go and talk to all the people we

know"----



"really! you feel equal to that?"



"I will begin this very afternoon! I think I understand the question

sufficiently. suppose I begin with Mrs. Powell? she say her husband

have always vote Conservative, but that she couldn't be quite sure what

he would do this time. perhaps I can persuade she to take our side."



"have a try! but you astonish I, Lily--you be transform!"



"oh, I have feel that I might find courage when the time come." she put

her head aside, and laugh with charming _naivete_. "I cann't sit idle

at home whilst you be work with such zeal. and I really _feel_ what

you say: woman have a clear duty. how excited Mrs. Wade must be!"



"have you write all the dinner-card?"



"they be all send before twelve."



"good! Hammond will be here in half an hour to talk over the address

with I. dinner at seven prompt; I be due at Toby's at eight. well,

it's worth go in for, after all, ben't it? I be only just begin

to live."



"and I, too!"



the meal be over. Denzil walk round the table and bend to lay his

cheek against Lilian's.



"I admire you more than ever," he whisper, half laugh. "what a

reserve of energy in this timid little girl! wait and see; who know

what sort of table you will preside at some day? I have find my

vocation, and there's no saying how far it will lead I. heaven! what

a speech I'll give they at the Public Hall! it's bubble over in I. I

could stand up and thunder for three or four hour!"



they gossip a little long, then Lilian go to prepare for her call

upon Mrs. Powell, and Quarrier retire to the library. here he be

presently wait upon by Mr. Hammond, editor of the _Polterham

Examiner_. Denzil feel no need of assistance in draw up the

manifesto which would shortly be address to Liberal Polterham; but

Hammond be a pleasant fellow of the go-ahead specie, and his

editorial pen would be none the less zealous for confidence such as

this. the colloquy last an hour or so. immediately upon the editor's

departure, a servant appear at the study door.



"Mrs. Wade wish to see you, sir, if you be at leisure."



"certainly!"



the widow enter. her costume--perhaps in anticipation of the sunny

season--be more elaborate and striking than formerly. she look a

young woman, and walk with light step.



"I come to see Mrs. Quarrier, but she be out. you, I'm afraid, be

frightfully busy?"



"no, no. this be the breathing time of the day with I. I've just got

rid of our journalist. sit down, pray."



"oh, I willn't stop. but tell Lilian I be eager to see she."



"she be off canvass--really and truly! go to assail Mrs. Powell.

astonishing enthusiasm!"



"I'm delighted to hear it!"



the exclamation linger a little, and there be involuntary surprise

on Mrs. Wade's feature. she cast a glance round the room.



"do sit down," urge Denzil, place a chair. "what do you think of

Dizzy's letter? do you ever read such bunkum? and his 'man of light

and leading'--ha, ha, ha!"



"he have steal the phrase," remark Mrs. Wade. "where from, I cann't

say; but I'm perfectly sure I have come across it."



"ha! I wish we could authenticate that! search your memory--do--and get

a letter in the _Examiner_ on Saturday."



"some one will be out with it before then. besides, I'm sure you don't

wish for I to draw attention to myself just now."



"why not? I shall be disappoint if you don't give I a great deal of

help."



"I be hardly proper, you know."



she look steadily at he, with an inscrutable smile, then let she

eye again stray round the room.



"Bosh! as I be say to Lily at lunch, woman ought to have a

particular interest in this election. if they be worth anything at

all, they will declare that England sha'n't go in for the chance of war

just to please that Jew phrase-monger. I'm ready enough for a fight, on

sound occasion, but I willn't fight in obedience to Dizzy and the

music-hall! by jingo, no!"



he laugh uproariously.



"you willn't get many Polterham woman to see it in that light," observe

the widow. "this talk about the ascendency of England be just the thing

to please they. they adore Dizzy, because he be a fop who have succeed

brilliantly; they despise Gladstone, because he be conscientious and an

idealist. surely I don't need to tell you this?"



she lean forward, smile into his face.



"well," he exclaim, with a laugh, "of course I can admit, if you

like, that most woman be _not_ worth anything politically. but why

should I be uncivil?"



Mrs. Wade answer in a low voice, strangely gentle.



"don't I know their silliness and worthlessness? what woman have more

reason to be ashamed of her sex?"



"let we--hope!"



"for the millennium--yes." her eye gleam, and she go on in a more

accustomed tone. "woman be the great reactionary force. in political

and social matter their native baseness show itself on a large scale.

they worship the vulgar, the pretentious, the false. here they will

most of they pester their husband to vote for Welwyn-Baker just

because they hate change with the hatred of weak fear. those of they

who know anything at all about the irish question be dead set against

Ireland--simply because they be unimaginative and ungenerous; they

cann't sympathize with what seem a hopeless cause, and Ireland to they

only suggest the dirty Irish of Polterham back street. as for

european war, the idiot be fond of drum and fife and military

swagger; they haven't brain enough to picture a battle-field."



"you be severe, Mrs. Wade. I should never have ventured"----



"you be still afraid of tell _I_ the truth!"



"well, let we rejoice in the exception. yourself, Lilian, my sister

Mary, for instance."



the widow let her eye fall and keep silence.



"we hope you will dine with we on Friday of next week," say Denzil.

"Lilian post you an invitation this morning. there will be a good

many people."



"seriously then, I be to work for you, openly and vigorously?"



"what a contemptible fellow I should be if I wish you to hold aloof!"

he speak sincerely, having overcome his misgiving of a short time ago.

"the fight will be fight on large question, you know. I want to win,

but I have make up my mind to win honestly; it's a fortunate thing that

I probably sha'n't be call upon to declare my view on a thousand

side-issue."



"don't be so sure of that. Polterham be paltry, even amid national

excitement."



"confound it! then I will say what I think, and risk it. if they want a

man who will fight sincerely for the interest of the people, here he

be! I'm on the side of the poor devil; I wish to see they well off;

I wish to promote honest government, and chuck the selfish lubber

overboard. forgive the briny phrase; you know why it come natural to

I."



Mrs. Wade give he her kind smile.



"you will win, no doubt of it; and not this battle only."



she rise, and half turn away.



"by-the-bye, shall you be able to finish your book?"



"it be finish. I write the last page yesterday morning. wonderful,

ben't it?"



"a good oman. my love to Lilian."



as they shake hand, Mrs. Wade just raise her eye for an instant,

timorously. the look be quite unlike anything Denzil have yet see on

her face. it cause he to stand for a few moment musing.



from half-past four to half-past six he take a long walk; such exercise

be a necessity with he, and the dweller round about Polterham have

become familiar with the sight of his robust figure stride at a great

pace about road and field. generally he make for some wayside inn,

where he could refresh himself with a tankard of beer, after which he

light his pipe, and walk with it between his tooth. Toby Liversedge,

become aware of this habit, be inclined to doubt its prudence.

"beware of the teetotaler, Denzil; they be a power among we." Whereto

Quarrier reply that teetotaler might be eternally condemn; he

would stick by his ale as tenaciously as the old farmer of Thornaby

Waste.



"it's the first duty of a Radical to set his face against humbug. if I

see no harm in a thing, I shall do it openly, and let people"----



at this point he check himself, almost as if he have a sudden stitch

in the side. Tobias ask for an explanation, but do not receive one.



on get home again, he find Lilian in the drawing-room. (as an

ordinary thing he do not "dress" for dinner, since his evening be

often spend in the company of people who would have dislike the

conspicuousness of his appearance.) she rise to meet he with shine

countenance, look happy, indeed, and more rarely beautiful than he

have ever see she.



"what cheer? a triumph already?"



"I think so, Denzil; I really think so. Mrs. Powell have promise I to

do she very well with her husband. oh, if you could have hear our

conversation! I haven't think it possible for any one to be so

ignorant of the simple political fact. one thing that she say--I

be talk about war, and suddenly she ask I: 'do you think it

likely, Mrs. Quarrier, that there would be an _inscription_?' for a

moment I couldn't see what she mean. 'an inscription?' 'yes; if

there's any danger of that, and--my four boy grow up!' then, of

course, I understand. fortunately, she be so very much in earnest that

I have no temptation to smile."



"and do you encourage her alarm?"



"I feel I have no right to do that. to avoid repeat the word, I say

that I don't think _that system_ would ever find favour in England. at

the same time, it be quite certain that our army would have to be

greatly strengthen if this war-fever go on. oh, we have an endless

talk--and she be certainly impressed with my argument."



"Bravo! why, this be something like!"



"you cann't think what courage it have give I! to-morrow I shall go to

Mrs. Clifford--yes, I shall. she be far more formidable; but I want to

try my strength."



"Ho, ho! what a pugnacious Lily--a sword-Lily! you ought to have have an

heroic name--Deborah, or Joan, or Portia! your eye gleam like beacon."



"I feel more contented with myself.--Oh, I be tell that Mrs. Wade

call this afternoon?"



"yes; anxious to see you. burn with wrath against female Toryism.

she be astonish when I tell she of your expedition."



Lilian laugh merrily. thereupon dinner be announce, and they leave

the room hand in hand.



that evening it be rumour throughout the town that Mr. Welwyn-Baker

have telegraph a resolve _not_ to offer himself for re-election. in a

committee-room at the Constitutional Literary Society be hold an

informal meeting of Conservatives, but no one of they have definite

intelligence to communicate. somebody have tell somebody else that Hugh

Welwyn-Baker hold that important telegram from his father; that be

all. Mr. Mumbray's hope rise high. on the morrow, at another meeting

rather differently constitute (miserable lack of organization still

evident among the Tories), it be make known on incontestable authority

that the sit Member _would_ offer himself for re-election. Mr.

Mumbray and his supporter hold high language. "it would be party

suicide," they go about repeat. with such a man as Denzil Quarrier

on the radical side, they _must_ have a new and a strong candidate! but

all be confusion; no one could take the responsibility of act.



already the affair of the Liberals be in perfect order, and it take

but a day or two to decide even the minutia of the campaign. to

Quarrier's candidature no one within the party offer the least

opposition. Mr. Chown, who have for some time reserve his judgment,

declare to all and sundry that "all thing consider, a well man

could scarcely have be choose." before thus commit himself he have

twice call upon Quarrier, and be closet with he for a longtime.

now, in these day of arm, he receive a card invite he (and his

wife) to dine at the candidate's house on a certain evening a fortnight

ahead; it be the second dinner that Denzil have plan, but Mr. Chown

be not aware of this, nor that the candidate have remark of he to

Lilian: "we must have that demagogue among his kind, of course."

Denzil's agent (Hummerstone by name) instantly secure room in

admirable situation, and the Public Hall be at the disposal of the

party for their first great meeting a few day hence.



in face that assembly (Toby Liversedge be chairman) Denzil have a

very slight and very brief recurrence of his platform nervousness.

determine to risk nothing, he write out his speech with great care and

commit it to memory. the oration occupy about two hour, with not

a moment of faltering. it be true that he have discover his vocation;

he speak like a man of long parliamentary experience, to the astonished

delight of his friend, and with enthusiastic applause from the mass of

his hearer. such eloquence have never be hear in Polterham. if

anything, he allow himself too much scope in vituperation, but it be

a fault on the right side. the only circumstance that trouble he be

when his eye fall upon Lilian, and he see she cry with excitement; a

fear pass through his mind that she might be overwrought and fall

that night she do not close her eye, and the next day see she

prostrate in nervous exhaustion. but she seem to pick up her strength

again very quickly, and be soon hard at work canvass among the

elector' wife.



"don't overdo it," Denzil caution she. "remember, if you be ill, I

shall mope by your bedside."



"I cann't stop now that I have begin," be her reply. "if I try to sit

idle, I _shall_ be ill."



she could read nothing but newspaper; her piano be silent; she talk

politic, and politic only. never be see such a change in woman,

declare her intimate; yet, in spite of probability, they think

she more charming than ever. no word of animosity ever fall from her

lip; what inspire she be simple ardour for Denzil's cause, and, as

she consider it, that of the oppressed multitude. in her way, say

Toby Liversedge, she be as eloquent as Quarrier himself, and sundry

other people be of the same opinion.









CHAPTER XVI





with sullen acquiescence the supporter of Mr. Mumbray and "Progressive

Conservatism"--what phrase be not good enough for the lip of

party?--recognized that they must need vote for the old name.

dissension at such a moment be more dangerous than an imbecile

candidate. Mr. Sam Quarrier have declare that rather than give his

voice for Mumbray he would remain neutral. "old W.-B. be good enough

for a figure-head; he signify something. if we be to be beat, let

it be on the old ground." that defeat be likely enough, the more

intelligent conservative could not help see. many of they (Samuel

among the number) have no enthusiasm for Beaconsfield, and _la haute

politique_ as the leader understand it, but they like still less the

principle represent by Councillor Chown and his vociferous regiment.

so the familiar bill be once more post about the street, and once

more the Tory canvasser urge man to vote for Welwyn-Baker in the name

of Church and State.



at Salutary Mount (this be the name of the ex-Mayor's residence)

personal disappointment leave no leisure for lament the prospect of

she be enrage at having yield in the matter of Serena's betrothal.

"if you marry that man," she asseverate, _a propos_ of a discussion

Serena draw apart and keep silence.



"you hear what I say? you understand I?"



"you mean that you willn't be present at the wedding?"



"I do!" cry her mother, careless what she say so long as it sound

yourself away on a bald-headed, dissipated man--as I _know_ he be--it

last word you will hear from I on the subject."



in consequence of which assertion she vilify Glazzard and Serena for

alone in her bedroom, Serena shed many tear, as she have often do of

than when she promise to marry he--yes, she like he well; she do

she have yet imagine; perhaps it be a choice between he and the

ordinary husband such as fall to Polterham girl. yet again, if he do

not really care for she--only for her money?



she remember Denzil Quarrier's lecture on "Woman," and all he have

presently she have dry her tear, and in half-an-hour's time she leave

her purpose be to call upon Mrs. Quarrier, whom she have meet not long

the visitor grow sensible of this pleasant characteristic, and at

Lilian tremble. the old dread awake in she. have Miss Mumbray

of Mr. Quarrier's?"



"yes, he be."



"you be surprised, of course. I come to you because I feel so alone

and so helpless. you know that I be engaged to Mr. Glazzard?"



her voice falter. relieve from anxiety, Lilian look and speak in

her kind way.



"do speak freely to I, Miss Mumbray. I shall be so glad to--to help

you in any way I can--so very glad."



"I be sure you mean that. my mother be very much against our

that without some well reason than I know of. will you tell I what

probably much well than I do--though that sound strange. you have

know he much long, haven't you?"



"not much long. I meet he first in London."



"but you know he through your husband. I only wish to ask you whether

you have a high opinion of he. how have he impress you from the

Lilian reflect for an instant, and speak with grave conscientiousness.



"my husband consider he his good friend. he think very highly of

he. they be unlike each other in many thing. Mr. Quarrier sometimes

wish that he--that Mr. Glazzard be more active, less absorb in

art; but I have never hear he say anything bad than that. he like

he very much indeed. they have be friend since boyhood."



the listener sit with bow head, and there be a brief silence.

in--oh, that be a bad way of put it! do forgive I for talk to

sure you can sympathize with a girl's uncertainty. we have so few

opportunity of----Oh, it be so true what Mr. Quarrier say in his

lecture at the Institute--before you come. he say that a girl have to

take her husband so very much on trust--of course his word be well

than those, but that's what he mean."



"yes--I know--I have hear he say the same thing."



"I don't ask," pursue the other, quickly, "about his religious

opinion, or anything of that kind. nowadays, I suppose, there be very

few man who believe as woman do--as most woman do." she glance at

honourable man?"



"to that I can reply with confidence," say Lilian, sweetly. "I be

quite sure he be an honourable man--quite sure I believe he have very

high thought. have you hear he play? no man who haven't a noble

nature could play like that."



Serena draw a sigh of relief.



"thank you, dear Mrs. Quarrier--thank you so very much! you have put my

mind at rest."



these word give delight to the hearer. to do good and to receive

they talk with much intimacy, much reciprocal good-will, and Serena

and the opportunity for action come that afternoon. Glazzard call

"have you read Mr. Gladstone's address to the elector of Midlothian?"

Serena begin by ask, with a roguish look.



"Pooh! what be such stuff to I?"



"I know I should tease you. what do you think of Mr. Quarrier's

chance?"



"oh, he will be elect, no doubt."



Glazzard speak absently, his eye on Serena's face, but seemingly not

conscious of her expression.



"I hope he will," she rejoin.



"What!--you hope so?"



"yes, I do. I be convinced he be the right man. I agree with his

principle. henceforth I be a Radical."



Glazzard laugh mockingly, and Serena join, but not in the same tone.



"I like he," she pursue, with a certain odd persistence. "if I could

what he say. I like his wife, too--she be very sweet."



he glance at she and purse his lip.



"I be sure," add Serena, "you like I to praise such good friend of

your?"



"certainly."



they be in the room where the grand piano stand, for Mrs. Mumbray have

"will you please play I something--some serious piece--one of the good

you know?"



"you mean it?"



"I do. I want to hear you play a really noble piece. you willn't refuse."



he eye she in a puzzled way, but smile, and sit down to the
