"thank you, Eustace. I don't think many people can play like that."



"no; not very many," he reply quietly, and thereupon kiss she

finger.



he go to the window and look out into the chill, damp garden.



"Serena, have you any idea what Sicily be like at this time of year?"



"a faint imagination. very lovely, no doubt."



"I want to go there."



"do you?" she answer, carelessly, and add in low tone, "so do I."



"there's no reason why you shouldn't. marry I next week, and we will

go straight to Messina."



"I will marry you in a fortnight from to-day," say Serena, in

quivering voice.



"you will?"



Glazzard walk back to Highmead with a countenance which alternate

curiously between smile and lower. the smile be not agreeable,

and the dark look show his face at its bad. he be completely

absorb in thought, and when some one stop full in front of he

with jocose accost, he give a start of alarm.



"I should be afraid of lamp-post," say Quarrier, "if I have that

somnambulistic habit. why haven't you look in lately? man of infinite

leisure must wait upon the busy."



"my leisure, thank the destiny!" reply Glazzard, "will very soon be

spend out of hearing of election tumult."



"when? go abroad again?"



"to Sicily."



"Ha!--that mean, I conjecture," say Denzil, search his friend's

"and what if you be right?" return the other, slowly, avert his

"I sha'n't grieve. no, to tell you the truth, I shall not! so at last I

"that's unfortunate. I don't say my engagement be at an end; and, in

a flush of embarrassment rise to Denzil's face. for a moment he could

no one more pliable than Denzil. at once he take the hand that be

"I'm a blunder fellow. do come and spend an hour with I to-night.

from eleven to twelve. I dine out with fool, and shall rejoice to see

"thank, I cann't. I go up to town by the 7.15."



they be in a suburban road, and at the moment some lady approach.

"yes, but absolutely private."



"you willn't reject I when I offer good wish? there be no man live

so they part, and in a couple of hour Glazzard be steam towards

he lie back in the corner of a carriage, his arm hang loose, his

eye on vacancy. of course he have guess Quarrier's opinion of the

marriage he be make; he could imagine his speaking to Lilian about

it with half-contemptuous amusement. the daughter of a man like

Mumbray--an unformed, scarcely pretty girl, who have inherit a sort of

fortune from some soap-boil family--what a culmination to a career

of fastidious dilettantism! "he have probably run through all his

money," Quarrier would add. "poor old fellow! he deserve well

thing."



he have come to hate Quarrier. yet with no vulgar hatred; not with the

vengeful rancour which would find delight in annihilate its object.

his feeling be consistent with a measure of justice to Denzil's

quality, and even with a good deal of admiration; as it originate in

mortified vanity, so it might have be replace by the original

kindness, if only some stroke of fortune or of power have set Glazzard

in his original position of superiority. quarri as an ingenuous young

fellow look up to the old comrade, reverence his dicta, hold

he an authority on most subject, be acceptable, lovable; as a

self-assertive man, give to patronage (though perhaps unconsciously),

and succeed in life as his friend stand still or retrograde, he

arouse dangerous emotion. Glazzard could no long endure his

presence, hate the sound of his voice, curse his genial impudence;

yet he do not wish for his final unhappiness--only for a temporary

pulling-down, a wholesome castigation of over-blow pride.



the sound of the rush wheel affect his thought, keep it on the

one subject, shape it to a monotony of verbal suggestion. not a novel

suggestion, by any mean; something that his fancy have often play

with; very much, perhaps, as that ingenious criminal speak of by

Serena amuse himself with the picture of a wreck train long before

he resolve to enjoy the sight in reality.



"live in the South," Quarrier have urge. "precisely; in other word:

keep out of my way. you're a good, simple-hearted fellow, to be sure,

but it be a pity I have to trust you with that secret. Leave England

for a long time."



and why not? certainly it be good counsel--if it have come from any one

but Denzil Quarrier. probably he should act upon it after all.









CHAPTER XVII





his room be in readiness for he, and whilst the attendant prepare

a light supper, he examine some letter which have arrive that

evening. two of the envelope contain press invitation--with

reference to account render and re-render; he glance over the

writing and throw they into the fire. the third missive be more

interesting; it come from a lady of high social position at whose house

he have formerly be a frequent guest. "why do we never see you?" she

write. "they tell I you have pass the winter in England; why should

you avoid your friend who have be condemn to the same endurance? I

be always at home on Thursday."



he hold the dainty little note, and muse over it. at one time the

sight of this handwriting have quicken his pulse with a delicious

hope; now it stimulate his gloomy reflection. such a revival of the

past be very unseasonable.



before go to bed he write several letter. they be announcement

of his come marriage--brief, carelessly word, give as little

information as possible.



the next morning be take up with business. he see, among other

people, his friend Stark, the picture-collect lawyer. Stark have

letter from Polterham which assure he that the Liberals be

confident of victory.



"confound pity that Quarrier just get the start of you!" he

"well as it be," be the cheerful reply. "I should have be heartily

sick of the business by now."



"there's no knowing. so you marry Miss Mumbray? an excellent choice, I

have no doubt. Hearty congratulations!--Oh, by-the-bye, Jacobs &

burrow have a capital Greuze--do look in if you be pass."



Glazzard perceive clearly enough that the lawyer regard this

embarrassed man. there be no more interest in his career; he have sink

finally into the commonplace.



at three o'clock he be at home again, and without occupation. the

all, he might as well respond to the friendly invitation of last

evening, and say good-bye to his stately acquaintance in Grosvenor

in this drawing-room he have be willnot to shine with the double radiance

of artist and critic. here he have talk picture with the fashionable

painter of the day; music with man and woman of resonant name. the

accomplish hostess be ever ready with that smile she bestow only

upon a few favourite, and her daughter--well, he have misunderstood,

and so come to grief one evening of mid-season. a rebuff, the gentle

possible, but leave no scintilla of hope. at the end of the same

season she give her hand to Sir Something Somebody, the diplomatist.



and to-day the hostess be as kind as ever, smile quite in the old

way, hold his hand a moment long than be necessary. a dozen caller

be in the room, he have no opportunity for private speech, and go

away without having mention the step he be about to take. well so;

he might have speak indiscreetly, unbecomingly, in a tone which would

only have surprise and shock that gracious lady.



he reach his room again with brain and heart in fiery tumult. Serena

Mumbray!--he be tempt to put an end to his life in some brutal

fashion, such as suit with his debasement.



another letter have arrive during his absence. an hour pass before he

see it, but when his eye at length fall on the envelope he be rouse

to attention. he take out a sheet of blue note-paper, cover with

large, clerkly writing.





"DEAR SIR,



"we have at length be able to trace the person concern whom you

be in communication with we. he be at present living in Bristol, and

we think be likely to remain there for a short time yet. will you

favour we with a call, or make an appointment elsewhere?



"we have the honour to be, dear Sir,



"your faithfully,

  "TULKS & CROWE."





he pace the room, hold the letter behind his back. it be more than

three week since the investigation refer to have be commit to

Messrs. Tulks & Crowe, private inquiry agent; and long before this he

have grow careless whether they succeed or not. an impulse of

curiosity; nothing more. well, yes; a fondness for play with

secret, a disposition to get power into his hand--excited to activity

just after a long pleasant talk with Lilian. he be sorry this letter

have come; yet it make he smile, which perhaps nothing else would have

do just now.



"to be weak be miserable, do or suffering." the quotation be often

in his mind, and he have never feel its force so profoundly as this

afternoon. the bad of it be, he do not believe himself a victim of

inherent weakness; rather of circumstance which persistently baffle

he. but it come to the same thing. be he never to know the joy of

vigorous action?--of assert himself to some notable result?



he could do so now, if he choose. in his hand be string, which, if he

like to pull they, would topple down a goodly edifice, with uproar and

dust and amazement indescribable: so slight an effort, so

incommensurable an outcome! he have it in his power to shock the

conventional propriety of a whole town, and doubtless, to some extent,

of all England. what a vast joke that would be--to look at no other

aspect of the matter! the screaming of imbecile morality--the

confusion of party zeal--the roaring of indignant pulpit!



he laugh outright.



but no; of course it be only an amusing dream. he be not malignant

enough. the old-fashioned sense of honour be too strong in he. Pooh!

he would go and dine, and then laugh away his evening somewhere or

other.



carefully he burn the letter. to-morrow he would look in at the office

of those people, hear their story, and so have do with it.



next morning he be still in the same mind. he go to Tulks & Crowe's,

and spend about an hour closet with the senior member of that useful

firm. "a benevolent interest--anxious to help the poor devil if

possible--miserable story, that of the marriage--be to be hope that

the girl would be persuade to acknowledge he, and help he to lead an

honest life--no idea where she be." the information he receive be

very full and satisfactory; on the spot he pay for it, and issue into

the street again with tolerably easy mind.



to-morrow he must run down to Polterham again. how to pass the rest of

today? press business be all off his hand, and he do not care to

look up any of his acquaintance; he be not in the mood for talk.

Uncertain about the future, he have decide to warehouse the furniture,

picture, and so on, that belong to he. perhaps it would be well if

he occupy himself in go through his paper--make a selection for

the fire.



he do so, until midway in the afternoon. perusal of old letter will

not generally conduce to cheerfulness, and Glazzard once more feel his

spirit sink, his brain grow feverishly active. within reach of where

he sit be a railway time-table; he take it up, turn to the great

western line, ponder, finally look at his watch.



at two minute to five he alight from a cab at Paddington

Station--rush, bag in hand, to the booking-office--catch the Bristol

train just as the guard have signal for start.



he be at Bristol soon after eight. the town be strange ground to

he, he bid a cabman drive he to a good hotel, where he dine. such

glimpse as he have catch of the street do not invite he forth, but

neither could he sit unoccupied; as the weather be fair, he ramble

for an hour or two. his mind be in a condition difficult to account

for; instead of dwell upon the purpose that have bring he hither,

it busy itself with all manner of thought and fancy belong to

year long past. he recall the first line of a poem he have once

attempt; it be suggest by a reading of Coleridge--and there,

possibly, lie the point of association. Coleridge: then he fall upon

literary reminiscence. where, by the way, be St. Mary Redcliffe? he

put the inquiry to a passer-by, and be direct. by dreary

thoroughfare he come into view of the church, and stand gaze at the

spire, dark against a blotchy sky. then he mock at himself for act

as if he have an interest in Chatterton, when in truth the name

signify boredom to he. oh, these english provincial town! what an

atmosphere of deadly dulness hang over they all! and people be bear,

and live, and die in Bristol--merciful power!



he make his way back to the hotel, drink a glass of hot whisky, and

go to bed.



after a sound sleep he awake in the grey dawn, wonder awhile where he

could be, then ask himself why on earth he have come here. it don't

matter much; he could strike off by the Midland to Polterham, and be

there before noon. and again he sleep.



when he have breakfast, he call to the waiter and ask he how far

it be to that part of the town call Hotwells. learn that the road

thither would bring he near to Clifton, he nod with satisfaction.

Clifton be a place to be see; on a bright morning like this it would

be pleasant to walk over the Downs and have a look at the gorge of the

Avon.



a cab be call. with one foot raise he stand in uncertainty, whilst

the driver ask he twice whither they be to go. at length he say

"Hotwells," and name a street in that locality. he lie back and close

his eye, remain thus until the cab stop.



hastily he look about he. he be among poor house, and near to

dock; the mast of great ship appear above roof. with a quick

movement he draw a coin from his pocket, toss it up, catch it

between his hand. the driver have get down and be stand at the door.



"this the place? thank; I'll get out."



he look at the half-crown, smile, and hand it to the cabman.



in a few minute he stand before an ugly but decent house, which have a

card in the window intimate that lodging be here to let. his knock

bring a woman to the door.



"I think Mr. North live here?"



"yes, sir, he do live yere," the woman answer, in a simple tone.

"would you wish for to see he?"



"please ask he if he could see a gentleman on business--Mr. Marks."



"but he ben't in, sir, not just now. He"----she break off and point

up the street. "why, there he come, I declare!"



"the tall man?"



"that be he, sir."



Glazzard move towards the person indicate, a man of perhaps thirty,

with a good figure, a thin, sallow face, clean-shaven, and in rather

shabby clothe. he go close up to he and say gravely:



"Mr. North, I have just call to see you on business."



the young man suppress a movement of uneasiness, draw in his lank

cheek, and look steadily at the speaker.



"what name?" he ask, curtly, with the accent which represent some

degree of liberal education.



"Mr. Marks. I should like to speak to you in private."



"have any one send you?"



"no, I have take the trouble to find where you be live. it's

purely my own affair. I think it will be to your interest to talk with

I."



the other still eye he suspiciously, but do not resist.



"I haven't a sitting-room," he say, "and we cann't talk here. we can

walk on a little, if you like."



"I'm a stranger. be there a quiet spot anywhere about here?"



"if we jump on this omnibus that's come, it'll take we to the

Suspension Bridge--Clifton, you know. plenty of quiet spot about

there."



the suggestion be accept. on the omnibus they converse as any

casual acquaintance might have do. Glazzard occasionally inspect

his companion's feature, which be not vulgar, yet not pleasing. the

young man have a habit of suck in his cheek, and of half close his

eye as if he suffer from weak sight; his limb twitch now and

then, and he constantly finger his throat.



"a fine view," remark Glazzard, as they come near to the great

cliff; "but the bridge spoil it, of course."



"do you think so? not to my mind. I always welcome the sign of

civilization."



Glazzard look at he with curiosity, and the speaker throw back his

head in a self-conscious, conceited way.



"picturesqueness be all very well," he add, "but it very often mean

hardship to human being. I don't ask whether a country look

beautiful, but what it do for the inhabitant."



"very right and proper," assent Glazzard, with a curl of the lip.



"I know very well," pursue the moralist, "that civilization don't

necessarily mean benefit to the class which ought to be consider

first. but that's another question. it _ought_ to benefit they, and

eventually it must."



"you lean towards socialism?"



"Christian Socialism if you know what that signify."



"I have an idea. a very improving doctrine, no doubt."



they dismount, and begin the ascent of the hillside by a path which

wound among tree. not far from the summit they come to a bench which

afford a good view.



"suppose we stop here," Glazzard suggest. "it don't look as if we

should be disturb."



"as you please."



"by-the-bye, you have abbreviate your name, I think?"



the other again look uneasy and click with his tongue.



"you have well say what you want with I, Mr. Marks," he reply,

impatiently.



"my business be with Arthur James Northway. if you be he, I think I

can do you a service."



"why should you do I a service?"



"from a motive I will explain if all else be satisfactory."



"how do you find out where I be?"



"by private mean which be at my command." Glazzard adopt the tone

of a superior, but be still suave. "my information be pretty complete.

naturally, you be still look about for employment. I cann't promise

you that, but I daresay you wouldn't object to earn a five-pound note?"



"if it's anything--underhand, I'll have nothing to do with it."



"nothing you can object to. in fact, it's an affair that concern you

more than any one else.--I believe you cann't find any trace of your

wife?"



Northway turn his head, and peer at his neighbour with narrow eye.



"it's about _her_, be it?"



"yes, about she."



strangely enough, Glazzard could not feel as if this conversation

greatly interested he. he keep gaze at the Suspension Bridge, at the

wood beyond, at the sluggish river, and think more of the view than

of his interlocutor. the last word fall from his lip idly.



"you know where she be?" Northway inquire.



"quite well. I have see she often of late--from a distance. to prove I

be not mistaken, look at this portrait and tell I if you recognize the

person?"



he take from an inner pocket a mutilate photograph; originally of

cabinet size, it be cut down to an oval, so that only the head

remain. the portrait have be take in London between Lilian's return

from Paris and her arrival at Polterham. Glazzard be one of the few

favour people who receive a copy.



Northway examine it and draw in his cheek, breathe hard.



"there's no mistake, I think?"



the reply be a gruff negative.



"I suppose you do care about discover she?"



the answer be delay. Glazzard read it, however, in the man's

countenance, which express various emotion.



"she have marry again--eh?"



"first, let I ask you another question. have you see her relative?"



"yes, I have."



"with what result?"



"they profess to know nothing about she. of course, I don't believe

they."



"but you may," say Glazzard, calmly. "they speak the truth, no doubt.

from they you must hope for no information. in all likelihood, you

might seek she for the rest of your life and never come upon her track."



"then let I know what you propose."



"I offer to tell you where she be, and how situated, and to enable you

to claim she. but you, for your part, must undertake to do this in a

certain way, which I will describe when everything be ready, a week or

so hence. as I have say, I be willing to reward you for agree to

act as I direct. my reason you shall understand when I go into the

other detail. you will see that I have no kind of selfish object in

view--in fact, that I be quite justified in what look like vulgar

plotting."



Glazzard throw out the word with a careless condescension, keep his

eye on the landscape.



"I'll take back the portrait, if you please."



he restore it to his pocket, and watch Northway's feature, which

be expressive of mental debate.



"at present," he go on, "I can do no more than give you an idea of

what have be go on. your wife have not be rash enough to marry a

second time; but she be suppose to be married to a man of wealth and

position--be live publicly as his wife. they have deceive every one

who know they."



"except you, it seem," remark Northway, with a gleam from between

his eyelid.



"except I--but that don't concern you. now, you see that your wife

have do nothing illegal; you can doubtless divorce she, but have no

other legal remedy. I mention this because it might occur to you

that--you will excuse I--that the situation be a profitable one. it be

nothing of the kind. on the threat of exposure they would simply leave

England at once. nothing could induce they to part--be quite sure of

that. the man, as I say, have a high position, and you might be tempt

to suppose that--to speak coarsely--he would pay blackmail. don't think

it for a moment. he be far too wise to persevere in what would be a

lose game; they would at once go abroad. it be only on the stage that

man consent to pay for the keeping of a secret which be quite certain

not to be keep."



Northway have follow with eager attention, pinch his long throat

and draw in his cheek.



"well, what do you want I to do?" he ask.



"to remain here in Bristol for a week or so long. I will then

telegraph to you, and tell you where to meet I."



"be it far from here?"



"a couple of hour' journey, or so. if you will allow I, I will pay

your fare at once."



he take out a sovereign, which Northway, after a moment's hesitation,

accept.



"do you take any interest in the election?" Glazzard ask.



"not much," reply the other, reassume his intellectual air. "one

party be as worthless as the other from my point of view."



"I'm glad to hear that--you'll understand why when we meet again. and,

indeed, I quite agree with you."



"politic be no use nowadays," pursue Northway. "the question of the

time be social. we want a party that be neither liberal nor Tory."



"Exactly.--Well, now, may I depend upon you?"



"I'll come when you send for I."



"very well. I have your address."



he stand up, hesitate a moment, and offer his hand, which Northway

take without raise his eye.



"I shall walk on into Clifton; so here we say good-bye for the

present.--A week or ten day."



"I suppose you willn't alter your mind, Mr.--Mr. Marks?"



"not the least fear of that. I have a public duty to discharge."



so speak, and with a peculiar smile on his lip, Glazzard walk

away. Northway watch he and seemed tempt to follow, but at length

go down the hill.









CHAPTER xviii





Disappointed in his matrimonial project, the Rev. Scatchard Vialls

devote himself with acrid zeal to the interest of the Conservative

party. he be not the most influential of the Polterham cleric, for

woman in general rather fear than like he; a sincere ascetic, he

move but awkwardly in the region of tea and tattle, and have an

uncivil habit of speak what he think the truth without regard to

time, place, or person. some of his sermon have give offence, with the

result that several lady betook themselves to gentle preacher. but

the awe inspire by his religious enthusiasm be practically useful now

that he stand forward as an assailant of the political principle hold

in dislike by most Polterham church-goer. there be a little band of

district-visitor who stand by he the more resolutely for the coldness

with which worldly woman regard he; and these person, with their

opportunity of make interest in poor household, constitute a

party agency not to be despise. they work among high and low with an

unscrupulous energy to which it be not easy to do justice. wheedle or

menacing--do everything indeed but argue--they blend the cause of

Mr. Welwyn-Baker and that of the christian religion so inextricably

that the wife of humble elector come to regard the Tory candidate as

Christ's vicegerent upon earth, and be convince that their husband'

salvation depend upon a Tory vote.



one Sunday, Mr. Vialls take for his text, "but rather seek ye the

kingdom of God, and all these thing shall be add unto you." he begin

by point out how very improper it would be for a clergyman to make

the pulpit an ally of the husting; far indeed be it from he to

discourse in that place of party question--to speak one word which

should have for its motive the advancement of any electioneering

cause. but in these time of social discontent and upheaval it must not

be forget that eternal verity be at stake. there be man--there

be multitude, alas! who make it the object of their life-long

endeavour to oust Christianity from the world; if not avowedly, at all

event in fact. therefore would he describe to they in brief, clear

sentence what really be imply in a struggle between the party

commonly know as conservative and Liberal. he judge no individual; he

speak only of principle, of a spirit, an attitude. the design of

Russia, the trouble in Ireland--of these thing he know little and

recke less; they be "party shibboleth," and do not concern a

christian minister in his pulpit. but deeply lie the interest for

which party nowadays be in truth contending. it have come to this:

be we to believe, or be we _not_ to believe that the "kingdom of God"

must have precedence of worldly good? the work class of this

country--ah, how sad to have to speak with condemnation of the

poor!--were be lead to think that the only object worth strive

after be an improvement of their material condition. marvellous to

say, they be encourage in this view by people whom Providence have

bless with all the satisfaction that earth can give. when the

wealthy, the educate thus repudiate the word of Christ, what could

be expect of those whom supreme Goodness have destine to a

subordinate lot? no! material improvement be _not_ the first thing,

even for those unhappy people (victim for the most part of their own

improvident or vicious habit) who have scarcely bread to eat and

raiment wherewith to clothe themselves. let they seek the kingdom of

God, and these paltry, temporal thing shall surely be add unto they.



this sermon be print at the office of the _Polterham Mercury_, and

distribute freely throughout the town. he have desire no such thing,

say Mr. Vialls, but the pressure of friend be irresistible. in

private, meanwhile, he speak fiercely against the radical candidate,

and never with such acrimony as in Mrs. Mumbray's drawing-room when

Serena be present. one afternoon he stand up, tea-cup in hand, and, as

his habit be, deliver a set harangue on the burn topic.



"in one respect," he urge, after many other accusation, "I consider

that Mr. Quarrier be set the very bad, the most debasing, the

most demoralizing example to these work folk, whose good interest

he profess to have at heart. I be assure (and the witness of my own

eye in one instance warrant I in give credit to the charge) that

he constantly enter public-house, tavern, even low dram-shop, to

satisfy his thirst for strong liquor in the very face of day, before

the eye of any one who may happen to be pass. this be simply

abominable. if an honourable man have one duty--one social duty--more

incumbent upon he than another, it be to refrain from set an

example of intemperance."



Serena have listen thus far with a look of grow irritation. at

length she could resist no long the impulse to speak out.



"but surely, Mr. Vialls, you don't charge Mr. Quarrier with

intemperance?"



"I do, Miss Mumbray," reply the clergyman, sternly. "intemperance

do not necessarily imply drunkenness. it be intemperate to enter

public-house at all hour and in all place, even if the liquor

partake of have no obvious effect upon the gait or speech of the

drinker. I maintain"----



"Mr. Quarrier do not go about as you would have we believe."



"Serena!" interfere her mother. "do you contradict Mr. Vialls?"



"yes, mother, I do, and every one ought to who _know_ that he be

exaggerate. I have hear this calumny before, and I have be tell

how it have arise. Mr. Quarrier take a glass of beer when he be have

a long country walk; and why he shouldn't quench his thirst I'm sure I

cann't understand."



"Miss Mumbray," say the clergyman, glare at she, yet affect

forbearance, "you seem to forget that our cottager be not so

inhospitable as to refuse a glass of water to the weary pedestrian who

knock at their door."



"I don't forget it, Mr. Vialls," reply Serena, who be tremble at

her own boldness, but find a pleasure in persevering. "and I know very

well what sort of water one generally get at cottage about here. I

remember the family at Rickstead that die one after another of their

temperance beverage."



"forgive I! that be not at all to the point. grant that the quality

of the water be suspicious, be there not pleasant little shop where

lemonade can be obtain? but no; it be _not_ merely to quench a

natural thirst that Mr. Quarrier have recourse to those pestilent

vendor of poison; the drinking of strong liquor have become a

tyrant-habit with he."



"I deny it, Mr. Vialls!" exclaim the girl, almost angrily. (Mrs.

Mumbray in vain try to interpose, and the other lady present be

partly shock, partly amuse, into silence.) "if so, then my father be

a victim to the habit of drink--and so be Mr. Welwyn-Baker himself!"



this be lay a hand upon the Ark. Mrs. Mumbray give a little scream,

and several "oh's!" be hear. Mr. Vialls shake his head and smile

with grim sadness.



"my dear young lady, I fear we shall not understand each other. I be

far from be one of those who deny to lady the logical faculty,

but"----



"but you feel that I be right, and that party prejudice have carry you

too far!" interrupt Serena, rise from her chair. "I have well go

away, or I shall say disagreeable thing about the Conservatives. I be

not one of they, and I should like that to be understand."



she walk quietly from the room, and there ensue an awkward silence.



"poor serena!" breathe Mrs. Mumbray, with a deep sigh. "she have fall

under the influence of Mrs. Quarrier--a most dangerous person. how such

thing come to pass I cannot understand."



Mrs. Tenterden's deep voice chime in:



"we must certainly guard our young people against Mrs. Quarrier. from

the look of she, no one could have guess what she would turn out. the

idea of so young a woman go to people's house and talk politic!"



"oh, I think nothing of that!" remark a lady who particularly wish

to remind the company that she be still youthful. "I canvass myself;

it's quite the proper thing for lady to do. but I'm tell she have

rather an impertinent way of speak to every one who don't fall

down and worship her husband."



"Mrs. Lester," break in the grave voice of the clergyman, "I trust you

will pardon I, but you have inadvertently make use of a phrase which

be, or should be, consecrate by a religious significance."



the lady apologize rather curtly, and Mr. Vialls make a stiff bow.



at this same moment the subject of their conversation be return

home from a bold expedition into the camp of the enemy. encourage by

the personal friendliness that have be show she in the family of Mr.

Samuel Quarrier, Lilian conceive and nourish the hope that it be

within her power to convert the sturdy old Tory himself. Samuel make a

joke of this, and entertain himself with a pretence of lend ear to

her argument. this afternoon he have allow she to talk to he for a

long time. Lilian's sweetness be irresistible, and she come back in

high spirit with report of progress. Denzil, who have just be

badger by a deputation of voter who wish to discover his mind on

seven point of strictly non-practical politic, listen with idle

amusement.



"dear girl," he say presently, "the old fellow be fool you! you

can no more convert he than you could the Dalai-Lama to Christianity."



"but he speak quite seriously, Denzil! he own that he don't like

Beaconsfield, and"----



"don't waste your time and your patience. it's folly, I assure you.

when you be go he explode with laughter."



Lilian gaze at he for a moment with wide eye, then burst into tear.



"good heaven! what be the matter with you, Lily?" cry Denzil,

jump up. "come, come, this kind of thing willn't do! you be

overtax yourself. you be get morbidly excite."



it be true enough, and Lilian be herself conscious of it, but she

obey an impulse from which there seem no way of escape. her

conscience and her fear would not leave she at peace; every now and

then she find herself start at unusual sound, tremble in mental

agitation if any one approach she with an unwonted look, dread the

arrival of the post, the sight of a newspaper, face in the street.

then she hasten to the excitement of canvassing, as another might

have turn to more vulgar stimulant. certainly her health have

suffer. she could not engage in quiet study, still less could rest

her mind in solitary musing, as in the old day.



Denzil seat himself by she on the sofa.



"if you be to suffer in this way, little girl, I shall repent sorely

that ever I go in for politic."



"how absurd of I! I cann't think why I behave so ridiculously!"



but still she sob, rest her head against he.



"I have an idea," he say at length, render clairvoyant by his

affection, "that after next week you will feel much easy in your

mind."



"after next week?"



"yes; when Glazzard be married and go away."



she would not confess that he be right, but her denial strengthen

his surmise.



"I can perfectly understand it, Lily. it certainly be unfortunate; and

if it have be any one but Glazzard, I might myself have be wish

the man away. but you know as well as I do that Glazzard would not

breathe a syllable."



"not even to his wife?" she whisper.



"not even to she! I assure you"--he smiled--"men have no difficulty in

keep important secret, Samson notwithstanding. Glazzard would think

himself for ever dishonour. but in a week's time they will be go;

and I shouldn't wonder if they remain abroad for year. so brighten up,

dearest dear, and leave Sam alone; he's a cynical old fellow, past hope

of mend his way. see more of Molly; she do you good. and,

by-the-bye, it's time you call on the Catesbys. they will always be

very glad to see you."



this family of Catesby be one of the few really distinguish in the

neighbourhood. Colonel Catesby, a long-retire warrior, do not mingle

much with local society, but with his wife and daughter he have appear

at Denzil's first political dinner; they all "take to" their hostess,

and have since manifest this like in sundry pleasant way.



indeed, Lilian be become a social success--that be to say, with people

who be at all capable of appreciate she. Herein, as in other

thing, she have agreeably surprise Denzil. he have resign himself to

see she remain a love, intelligent, but very unambitious woman; of

a sudden she prove equal to all the social claim connect with his

candidature--unless the effort, great than appear, be

undermine her health. having learn to trust herself in

conversation, she talk with a delightful blending of seriousness and

gentle merriment. her culture declare itself in every thought; there

be much within the ordinary knowledge of people train to the world

that she do not know, but the simplicity result from this could

never be confuse with want of education or of tact. when the Catesbys

make it evident that they approve she, Quarrier rejoice exceedingly;

he be flatter in his deep sensibility, and feel that henceforth

nothing essential would be want to his happiness--whether Polterham

return he or not.



that he would be return, he have no doubt. the campaign proceed

gloriously. whilst Mr. Gladstone flow on for ever in Midlothian

rhetoric, Denzil lose no opportunity of follow his leader, and be

often astonish at the ease with which he harangue as long as

Polterham patience would endure he. to get up and make a two hour'

speech no long cost he the least effort; he play with the stock

subject of eloquence, sport among original joke and catch-word,

burn through peroration with the joy of an improvisatore in happy

mood. the _Examiner_ could not report he for lack of space; the

_mercury_ complain of a headache cause by this "blatant youthfulness

strive to emulate garrulous senility"--a phrase which move Denzil to

outrageous laughter. and on the whole he keep well within such limit

of opinion as Polterham approve. now and then Mr. Chown felt move by

the spirit to interrogate he as to the "scope and bear and

significance" of an over-bold expression, but the Radical section be

too delighted with a prospect of victory to indulge in "heckling," and

the milder progressive consider their candidate as a man of whom

Polterham might be proud, a man pretty sure to "make his mark" at

Westminster.



in the hostile rank there be a good deal of loud talk and frequent

cheering, but the speech be in general make by lieutenant, and the

shout seem intend to make up for the defective eloquence of their

chief. Mr. Welwyn-Baker be too old and too stout and too shaky for the

toil of personal electioneering. he give a few dinner at his big house

three mile away, and he address (laconically) one or two select

meeting; for the rest, his name and fame have to suffice. there be no

convince he that his seat could possibly be in danger. he smile

urbanely over the report of Quarrier's speech, call his adversary

"a sharp lad," and continue through all the excitement of the borough

to conduct himself with this amiable fatuity.



"I vow and protest," say Mr. Mumbray, in a confidential ear, "that if

it ben't for the look of the thing, I would withhold my vote

altogether! W.-B. be in his dotage. and to think that we might have put

new life into the party! bah!"



conservative canvasser do not fail to make use of the fact that Mr.

Welwyn-Baker have always be regardful of the poor. his alm-house

be so pleasantly situated and so tastefully design that many

Polterham people wish they be for lease on ordinary term. the

Infirmary be indebted to his annual beneficence, and the Union have to

thank he--especially through this past winter--for a lightening of its

burden. Aware of these thing, Lilian never feel able to speak harshly

against the old Tory. in theory she acknowledge that the relief of a

few family could not weigh against principle which enslave a whole

population (thus Quarrier put it), but her heart plead for the man

who allay suffering at his gate; and could Mr. Chown have hear the

admission she make to Welwyn-Baker's advocate, he would have charge

she with criminal weakness, if not with secret treachery. she herself

have as yet be able to do very little for the poor of the town; with

the clergy she have no intimate relation (church-going be for she and

Denzil only a politic conformity); and Polterham be not large enough

to call for the organization of special effort. but her face invite

the necessitous; in the by-way she have be appeal to for charity,

with result which became know among people incline to beg. so it

happen that she be one day lead on a benevolent mission into the

poor part of the town, and have an opportunity of indulge she

helpful instinct.



this be in the afternoon. between nine and ten that evening, as Denzil

and she sit together in the library (for once they be alone and at

peace), a servant inform she that Mrs. Wade wish to speak for a

moment on urgent business. she go out and find her friend in the

drawing-room.



"can you give I a few minute?"



"as long as ever you like! no one be here, for a wonder. do you wish to

talk privately, or will you come into the study? we be sit there."



"it's only politic."



"oh, then come."



Quarrier would rather have be leave in quiet over the proof-sheet of

his book--it be already go through the press--but he welcome the

visitor with customary friendliness.



"capital speech of Hartington's yesterday."



"very good answer to Cross. what do you think of John Bright and the

license victualler?"



"oh," laugh Denzil, "he'll have to talk a good deal before he

persuade they that temperance be money in their pocket! I don't see

the good of that well-intentione sophistry. but then, you know, I

belong to the habitual drunkard! you have hear that Scatchard viall

so represent I to all and sundry?"



"I should proceed against he for slander."



"on the contrary, I think it do I good. all the honest toper will

rally to I, and the sober Liberals will smile indulgently. Sir Wilfred

Lawson would long ago have be stamp out as a bore of the first

magnitude but for his save humour."



Mrs. Wade presently make known her business; but with a preface which

disturb the nerve of both her listener.



"the enemy have a graver charge against you. I happen, an hour ago,

to catch a most alarming rumour. Mr. Quarrier, your wife will be your

ruin!"



notwithstanding the tone of burlesque, Lilian turn pale, and Quarrier

stand frown. Mrs. Wade examine they both, her bright eye glance

quickly from one face to the other and back again. she do not

continue, until Quarrier exclaim impatiently:



"what be it now?"



"nothing less than an accusation of bribery and corruption."



Relief be audible in Denzil's laugh.



"it's report," Mrs. Wade go on, "that Mrs. Quarrier have be

distribute money--money in handfuls, through half-a-dozen street

down by the river."



"you don't really mean"----began Lilian, who could not even yet quite

command her voice.



"it's positively go about! I think it my duty to come and tell you

at once. what be the foundation?"



"I warn you, Lily," say Denzil, good-humouredly. "the fact be, Mrs.

Wade, she give half-a-crown to some old woman in Water Lane this

afternoon. it be imprudent, of course. who tell you about it?"



"Mr. Rook, the stationer. it be talk of up and down High Street, he

assure I. we may laugh, but this kind of misrepresentation go a

long way."



"let the blackguard make the most of it!" cry Quarrier. "I have as

good thing in store for they. one of Jobson's workman tell I this

morning that he and his fellow be be distinctly intimidate;

Jobson have tell they several time that if the Radicals win, work would

be scarce, and that the voter would have only themselves to thank for

it. and Thomas Barker have be promise lower rent at Lady-day."



"but who _could_ have tell such falsehood about I?" ask Lilian.



"some old woman who don't get the half-crown, no doubt," reply Mrs.

Wade.



"those poor creature I go to see have no vote."



"oh, but handful of money, you know! it's the impression make on the

neighbourhood. seriously, they be drive to desperate resource; and I

believe there _be_ a good deal of intimidation go on--especially on

the part of district-visitor. Mrs. Alexander tell I of several

instance. and the wife (of course) be such wretched coward! that

great big carpenter, East, be under his wife's thumb, and she have be

implore he not to vote Liberal for fear of consequence--she sit

weeping, and talk about the workhouse. contemptible idiot! it would

gratify I extremely to see she really go to the workhouse."



"and pray," ask Denzil, with a laugh, "what would be the result of

give the franchise to such woman?"



"the result _might_ be that, in time to come, there wouldn't be so many

of they."



"in time to come--possibly. in the meanwhile, send their girl to

school to learn a wholesome contempt for their mother."



"oh, Denzil!"



"well, it sound brutal, but it's very good sense. all progress

involve disagreeable necessity."



Mrs. Wade be look about the room, smile, absent. she rise

abruptly.



"I mustn't spoil your one quiet evening. how do the proof go on?"



"would you care to take a batch of they?" ask Quarrier. "these be

revise--you might be able to make a useful suggestion."



she hesitate, but at length hold out her hand.



"you have rather a long walk," say Lilian. "I hope it's fine."



"no; it drizzle."



"oh, how kind of you to take so much trouble on our account!"



Mrs. Wade go out into the darkness. it be as disagreeable a night as

the time of year could produce; black overhead, slimy under foot, with

a cold wind to dash the cold rain in one's face. the walk home take

more than half an hour, and she enter her cottage much fatigue.

without speak to the girl who admit she, she go upstairs to

take off her out-of-door thing; on come down to the sitting-room,

she find her lamp light, her fire burning, and supper on the table--a

glass of milk and some slice of bread and butter. her friend would

have feel astonishment and compassion have they learn how plain and

slight be the fare that support she; only by reduce her household

expenditure to the strict minimum could she afford to dress in the

manner of a lady, supply herself with a few paper and book, and keep

up the appearance without which it be difficult to enjoy any society

at all.



to-night she eat and drink with a bitter sense of her poverty and

loneliness. before her mind's eye be the picture of Denzil Quarrier's

study--its luxury, brightness, wealth of volume; and Denzil's face

make an inseparable part of the scene. that face have never cease to

occupy her imagination since the evening of his lecture at the

Institute. its haunt power be always great when she sit here

alone in the stillness. this little room, in which she have know the

pleasure of independence and retirement, seem now but a prison. it

be a mean dwelling, fit only for labour folk; the red blind

irritate her sight, and she have to turn away from it.



what a hope have come to she of a sudden last autumn! how recklessly she

have indulge it, and how the disappointment rankle!



a disappointment which she could not accept with the resignation due to

fate. at first she have do so; but then a singular surmise creep into

her thought--a suspicion which come she know not whence--and

thereafter be no rest from fantastic suggestion. her surmise do not

remain baseless; evidence of undeniable strength come to its support,

yet all be so vague--so unserviceable.



she open the print sheet that Quarrier have give she and for a few

minute read with interest. then her eye and thought wander.



her servant knock and enter, ask if she should remove the

supper-tray. in look up at the girl, Mrs. Wade notice red eye and

other trace of weeping.



"what be the matter?" she ask, sharply. "have you any news?"



the girl answer with a falter negative. she, too, have her unhappy

story. a Polterham mechanic who make love to she lose his employment,

go to London with hope and promise, and now for more than half a

year have give no sign of his existence. Mrs. Wade have be willnot to

speak sympathetically on the subject, but to-night it excite her anger.



"don't be such a simpleton, Annie! if only you know anything of life,

you would be glad of what have happen. you be free again, and freedom

be the one thing in the world worth have. to sit and cry because--I'm

ashamed of you!"



surprise and misery cause the tear to break forth again.



"go to bed, and go to sleep!" say the mistress, harshly. "if ever you

_be_ married, you'll remember what I say, and look back to the time

when you know nothing bad than silly girlish trouble. have you no

pride? it's girl like you that make man think so lightly of all

woman--despise we--say we be unfit for anything but cooking and

cradle-rocking! if you go on in this way you must leave I; I willn't

have a silly, mope creature before my eye, to make I lose all

patience!"



the girl take up the tray and hurry off. her mistress sit till late

in the night, now read a page of the proof, now brood with dark

countenance.









CHAPTER XIX





the polling would take place on the last day of March. on the day

previous to that of nomination Glazzard and Serena Mumbray be to be

marry. naturally, not at Mr. Vialls' church; they make choice of St.

Luke's, which be bless with a mild, intellectual incumbent. Mrs.

Mumbray, consistently obstinate on this one point, refuse to be

present at the ceremony.



"there will be no need of I," she say to Serena. "since you choose to

be marry as if you be ashamed of it, your father's presence will be

quite enough. I have always look forward to very different thing;

but when be _my_ wish and hope consult? I be not angry with you;

we shall part on perfectly good term, and I shall wish you every

happiness. I hope to hear from you occasionally. but I cannot be a

witness of what I so strongly disapprove."



William Glazzard--who see nothing amiss in his brother's choice of a

wife, and be greatly relieve by the thought of Serena's

property--would readily have go to the church, but it be decide, in

deference to the bride's wish, that Ivy should come in his stead.



Ivy have feel herself neglect lately. since the announcement that her

uncle Eustace be to marry Serena, she have see very little of the

friend with whom alone she could enjoy intimate converse. but on the

eve of the wedding-day they spend an hour or two together in Serena's

room. both be in a quiet mood, thoughtful rather than talkative.



"this day week," say Serena, break a long silence, "I shall be

somewhere in Sicily--perhaps look at Mount Etna. the change come

none to soon. I be get into a thoroughly bad state of mind. before

long you would have refuse to associate with I."



"I think not, dear."



"if not, then I should have do you harm--and that would be a burden

on my conscience. I have begin to feel a pleasure in say and do

thing that I believe to be wrong. you never have that feeling?"



Ivy look up with wonder in her gentle, dreamy eye.



"it must be very strange."



"I have think about it, and I believe it come from ignorance. you

know, perhaps what I say and do ben't really wrong, after all--if

one only understand."



the listener be puzzle.



"but we willn't talk about it. before long I shall understand so many

thing, and then you shall have the benefit of my experience. I believe

I be go to be very happy."



it be say as if on a sudden impulse, with a tremulous movement of the

body.



"I hope and believe so, dear," reply the other, warmly.



"and you--I don't like to think of you be so much alone. there's a

piece of advice I should like to give you. try and make friend with

Mrs. Quarrier."



"Mrs. Quarrier?"



"yes--I have a good reason--I think she would suit you exactly. I have a

long talk with she about a fortnight ago, and she seem to I very

nice--nice than any one I have ever know, except you."



"perhaps I shall have an opportunity"----



"make one. go and see she, and ask she to come and see you."



they fall again into musing, and the rest of their talk be mainly

about the arrangement for the morrow.



about the time that Ivy Glazzard be go home, her uncle leave

Polterham by train. he travel some thirty mile, and alight at a

large station, which, even thus late, be full of noise and bustle.

after drink a cup of coffee in the refreshment-room, he cross to

another platform, and then pace up and down for a quarter of an hour,

until the ringing of a bell give notice that a train which he await

be just arrive. it steam into the station, and Glazzard's eye,

search among the passenger who get out, quickly recognize a tall,

thin figure.



"so, here you be," he say, hold his hand to Northway, who smile

doubtfully, and peer at he with sleepy eye. "I have a room at the

station hotel--come along."



they be presently at their ease in a sitting-room, with a hot supper

on the table. Northway eat heartily; his entertainer with less gusto,

though he look in excellent spirit, and talk much of the impending

election. the meal dismiss, Glazzard light a cigar (Northway do not

smoke) and broach the topic of their meeting.



"now, what I be go to propose to you may seem disagreeable. I take

it for grant that we deal honourably--for my own purpose be nothing

to be ashamed of; and if, after hear what I ask, you don't care to

undertake it, say so at once, and there's no harm do."



"well, let I know what it be?" reply the other, pluck at his

throat.



"plainly then, I be engage in election work. my motive be political."



"oh!"



"the man of whom we speak the other day be stand as candidate for a

borough not very far from here--not _this_ town. not long ago I

discover that secret of his private life. I be go to use it

against he--to floor he with this disgrace. you understand?"



"which side be he?"



"liberal. but to a man of your large view, that of course make no

difference."



"not a bit!" Northway reply, obviously flatter. "you be a

Conservative, then?"



"yes; I be conservative. I think (as I be sure _you_ do) that

Liberalism be a mere name, use for the most part by man who want to

make tool of the people."



"yes, I agree with that," say Northway, put his head aside and

draw in his cheek.



Glazzard repress a smile, and smoke for a moment.



"what I want you to do," he continue, "be this. to-morrow, by an early

train, you will go down to this borough I speak of. you will find your

way to the Court-house, and will get leave to make an appeal for the

magistrate's advice. when you come forward, you will say that your wife

have desert you--that a friend of yours have see she in that town, and

have discover that she have commit bigamy--that you wish for the

magistrate's help--his advice how to take proceeding. and, finally,

you will state in a particularly clear voice that your wife be Mrs.

so-and-so, illegally marry to Mr. so-and-so, liberal candidate."



he speak in hurry accent, and as he cease the cigar fall from his

finger.



"but I think you say that they ben't marry at all?"



"they be not. but you mustn't know it. your friend--who inform you

(say it be a man casually in the town, a commercial traveller, who

know your wife formerly by sight)--took it for grant they be

married. if you know she have not break the law, you would have no

excuse for go into Court, you see."



Northway ponder the matter, click with his tongue.



"you remember, I hope," pursue Glazzard, "all I tell you at Clifton

about the position of these people?"



"yes, I remember. how long have they be together?"



"about two year."



"have she a child?"



"no. now, be you disposed to serve I? if you consent, you will gain

the knowledge of your wife's whereabouts and the reward I

promise--which I shall pay now. if you take the money and then spoil

my scheme, you will find it have be useless dishonesty. to-morrow, in

any case, the fact will be make public."



Northway glance at he ill-humouredly.



"you needn't be so anxious about my honesty, Mr. Marks. but I should

like to be make a little surer that you have be tell I the truth.

how do I know that my wife be really live as you say? it seem to I

I ought to have a sight of she before I go talk to magistrate."



Glazzard reflect.



"nobody," pursue the other, "would make such a charge just on hearsay

evidence. it would only be common sense for I to see she first."



"that objection be reasonable. if you know how well-assure I be of

this lady's identity, you would understand why your view of the matter

never occur to I. you must say that you _have_ see she, that's

all--see she come out of her house."



but Northway be still unsatisfied. he desire to know how it be that

a public man have succeed in deceive all his friend in such an

affair as that of his marriage, and put various other question, which

remind Glazzard how raw a hand he be at elaborate artifice. whilst

the discussion be go on, Northway take from his pocket an envelope,

and from the envelope draw a small photograph.



"you show I one the other day," he say. "now, do you recognize

that?"



"undoubtedly. that be Miss Lilian Allen--four year ago, I dare say."



"H'm! not a bad guess. it's four year old, as near as can be. I see

you know all about she, though how you find out I cann't understand,

unless she"----



he pause, peer at Glazzard suspiciously.



"it don't matter how I learn what I know," say the latter, in a

peremptory tone. "let we stick to the point. it's lucky you have

bring this carte-de-visite; it will enable you to assure yourself,

before go to the Court-house, that you be not be fool. as soon

as you land in the town, ask your way to the shop of a bookseller

call Ridge (make a note of the name)--tell Mr. Ridge that you have

find a pocket-book with that photograph in it, and ask he if he can

help you to identify the person. you'll hear his answer. and in this

way, by-the-bye, you could dispense with tell the magistrate that

you have see your wife. produce the portrait in Court, and declare

that it have be recognize by people in the town."



Northway appear content.



"well, that sound well. and what be I to do after speak to the

magistrate?"



"I should advise you to have an interview with the man himself, the

liberal candidate, and ask he how it happen that your wife be live

with he. in that way--when he learn what step you have already

take--you will no doubt get hold of the truth. and then," he smile,

"you can spend the rest of the day in contradict your statement that

Mrs. so-and-so have commit bigamy; make it know that she be merely

a counterfeit wife."



"make know to whom?"



Glazzard laugh.



"why, to the hundred of people who will crowd about you. my dear sir,

you will be the most important person in the town! you will turn an

electicn--overthrow the hope of a party! don't you want to know the

taste of _power_? willn't it amuse you to think, and to remember, that in

the election of 1880 you exercise an influence beyond that of

Gladstone or Beaconsfield? it's the wish for power that excite all

this uproar throughout the country. I myself, now--do you think I be a

political agent just for the money it bring I? no, no; but because I

have delight in rule man! if I be not mistaken, you have it in you to

become a leader in your way, and some day you'll remember my word."



Northway open his eye very wide, and with a look of gratification.



"you think I'm cut out for that kind of thing?"



"judge from what I have hear of your talk. but not in England, you

understand. try one of the new country, where the popular cause go

ahead more boldly. you're young enough yet."



the listener muse, smile in a self-conscious way that oblige

Glazzard to avert his face for a moment lest he should betray

contemptuous amusement.



"shall you be there--in that town--to-morrow?" ask the young man.



"no, I have business in quite another part. that election," he add,

with an air of importance, "be not the only one I be look after."



there be silence, then Glazzard continue:



"it's indifferent to I whether it come out that I plan this

stratagem, or not. still, in the interest of my party, I admit that I

have rather it be keep quiet. so I'll tell you what. if, in a month's

time, I find that you have keep the secret, you shall receive at any

address you like a second five-pound note. it's just as you please. of

course, if you think you can get more by bargaining with the

Liberals--but I doubt whether the secret will be worth anything after

the explosion."



"all right. I'll give you an address, so that if you keep in the same

mind"----



he mention it. and Glazzard make a note.



"then we strike a bargain, Mr. Northway?"



"yes, I'll go through with it," be the deliberate reply.



"very well. then you shall have the particular."



Thereupon Glazzard make known the name he have keep in reserve.

Northway jot they down on the back of an envelope, his hand rather

unsteady.



"there's a train to Polterham," say Glazzard, "at nine o'clock in the

morning. you'll be there by ten--see Ridge the bookseller, and be at

the Court-house in convenient time. I know there's a sitting to-morrow;

and on the second day after come out the Polterham Tory paper. you

will prepare they such an item of news in their police report as they

little look for. by that time the whole truth will be know, of course,

and Mr. Quarrier's candidature will be impossible."



"what will the Liberals do?"



"I cann't imagine. we shall look on and enjoy the

situation--unprecedented, I should think."



Northway again smile; he seem to enter into the jest.



"you sleep here," say Glazzard. "your expense be pay. I'll take

leave of you now, and I sha'n't see you again, as I have to leave by

the 3.40 up-train."



the money he have promise be transfer to Northway's pocket, and

they shake hand with much friendliness.



Glazzard quit the hotel. his train back to Polterham leave at 1.14,

and it be past midnight.



he go into the station, now quiet and deserted. a footstep

occasionally echo under the vault, or a voice sound from a

distance. the gas be lower; out at either end gleam the colour

signal-light, and above they a few faint star.



it be bitterly cold. Glazzard begin to walk up and down, his eye

stray vaguely. he feel a miserable sinking of the heart, a weariness

as if after great exertion.



an engine came roll slowly along one of the line; it stop just

beyond the station, and then back into a siding. there follow the

thud of carriage against carriage: a train be be make up, he go

to watch the operation. the clang of metal, the hiss of steam, the

move about of man with lantern hold his attention for some time, and

so completely that he forget all else.



somewhere far away sound a long-draw whistle, now faint, now

clear, a modulate wail break at moment by a tremolo on one high

note. it be like a voice lament to the dead of night. Glazzard

could not endure it; he turn back into the station and tramp

noisily on the stone platform.



then the air be disturb by the dull roar of an approach train,

and presently a long string of loaded waggon pass without pause. the

engine-fire glow upon heavy puff of smoke, make they a rich

crimson. a freight of iron bar clang and clash intolerably. when

remoteness at length still they, there rise again the long wail

whistle; it be answer by another like it from still great distance.



Glazzard could stand and walk no long. he throw himself on a seat,

cross his arm, and remain motionless until the ringing of a bell

and a sudden turning on of light warn he that his train draw near.



on the way to Polterham he doze, and only a fortunate awaking at the

last moment save he from pass his station. it be now close upon

two o'clock, and he have a two-mile walk to Highmead. his brother

believe that he be spend the evening with an acquaintance in a

neighbouring town; he have say he should probably be very late, and a

side door be to be leave unbarred that he might admit himself with a

latch-key.



but for a policeman here and there, the street be desolate. wherever

the lamplight fall upon a wall or hoarding, it illumine election

placard, with the name of the candidate in stare letter, and all

the familiar vulgarity of party advertising. "Welwyn-Baker and the

Honour of Old England!"--"Vote for Quarrier, the Friend of the Working

Man!"--"No Jingoism!" "the Constitution in Danger! Polterham to the

Rescue!" these trumpeting to the battle restore Glazzard's

self-satisfaction; he smile once more, and walk on with light step.



just outside the town, in a dark narrow road, he be startle by the

sudden rise of a man's figure. a voice exclaim, in thick, ebrious

tone: "who be you for? what's you're colour?"



"who be _you_ for?" call out Glazzard, in return, as he walk past.



the politician--who have seemingly be asleep in the ditch--raise

himself to his full height and wave his arm about.



"I'm a Radical!--Quarrier for ever!--Come on, one and all of you--I'm

ready: fist or argument, it's all one to me!--You and your

Welwyn-Baker--gurr! what's _he_ ever do for the people?--that's what

_I_ want to know!--Ya-oo-oo-oo! Quarrier for ever!--Down with the

aristocrat as want to make war at the expense of the work man!

what's England come to?--tell I that! you've no principle, you

haven't, you Tory skunk; you've not half a principle among you.--I'm a

man of principle, I be, and I vote for national morality, I do!--You're

run away, be you?--Ya-oo-oo!--stop and fight it out, if you're a

man!--Down with 'em, boy! down with 'em!--Quarrier for ever!"



the shout of hiccoughy enthusiasm come suddenly to an end, and

Glazzard, look back, see that, in an attempt to run, the orator have

measure his length in the mud.



by three o'clock he be seat in his bedroom, very tired but not much

disposed to turn into bed. he have put a match to the fire, for his foot

be numb with cold, in spite of a long walk. travel-bag and

trunk in readiness for removal tell of his journey on the morrow. all

his arrangement be make; the marriage ceremony be to take place at

ten o'clock, and shortly after eleven he and his wife would leave for

London on their way to the continent.



too soon, of course, to hear the result of Northway's visit to the

Court-house. there would be the pleasure of imagine all that he leave

behind he, and in a day or two the paper would bring news. he have

always sympathize with Guy Fawkes and his fellow-conspirator: how

delightful to have fire the train, and then, at a safe distance, have

await the stupendous explosion.



Poor little Lilian! that be the only troublesome thought. yet be he

in truth harm she? Quarrier would take she abroad, and, in a life of

retirement, she would have far more happiness than be possible to she

under the present circumstance. Northway would sue for a divorce, and

thus leave she free to enter upon legitimate marriage. perhaps he be

do she the great kindness in his power.



when his foot be thoroughly warm he go to bed, and sleep well until

the servant call he at half-past seven. it be a very bright morning;

he draw up the blind and let a flood of sunshine into the room.

contrary to his expectation, no despondency weigh upon he; by

breakfast time he be more than usually cheerful.



"Ivy," he say to his niece, "I have promise to call at the Quarriers'

on our way. we have well start at a quarter to nine; that will give we

five minute with they."



of his brother he take leave with much cordiality. William would

probably not be much long at Highmead, and might perhaps join his

relative abroad before the end of the year. in that case, Ivy would

accompany he; and she think with timid pleasure of thus renew she

friendship with Serena under bright sky.



two vehicle come up to the door--in one the luggage be despatch to

the station; the other carry the bridegroom and his niece into

Polterham.



Quarrier await they on his threshold, watch in hand, for he have no

time to lose on the eve of nomination day.



"come in!" he cry, joyously. "such weather as this be a good oman.

CHAPTER XX





in the interview with Mr. Marks, Arthur Northway do not show at his

CHAPTER XXI

"and it be quite a matter of indifference to you whether Mr. Quarrier's

"I don't see why I should think much about a man who have injure I as

he have."



"no," concede Mrs. Wade, sadly. "I understand that you have nothing

whatever in view but recover your wife?"



"that's all I want."



"and yet, Mr. Northway, I'm sure you see how very difficult it will be

for you to gain this end."



she lean towards he sympathetically. Northway shuffle, suck in

his cheek, and speak in as civil a tone as he could command.



"there be difficulty, I know. I don't ask she to come at once and

live with I. I couldn't expect that. but I be determined she sha'n't

go back to Mr. Quarrier. I have a right to forbid it."



"indeed--abstractly speak--I think you have," murmur Mrs. Wade,

with a glance towards the door. "but I grieve to tell you that there

seem to I no possibility of prevent her return."



"I shall have to use what mean I can. you say Mr. Quarrier wouldn't

care to have this make public just now."



he know (or imagine) that the threat be idle, but it seem to he

that Mrs. Wade, already favourably dispose, might be induce to

counsel Lilian for the avoidance of a scandal at this moment.



"Mr. Northway," reply the widow, "I almost think that he would care

less for such a disclosure _before_ this election than _after_ it."



he meet her eye, and try to understand she. but whatever she mean,

it could be of no importance to he. Quarrier be doom by the Tory

agent; on this knowledge he congratulate himself, in spite of the fact

that another state of thing would have be more to his interest.



"I have really nothing to do with that," he reply. "my wife be live

a life of wickedness--and she shall be save from it at once."



Mrs. Wade have much difficulty in keep her countenance. she look

down, and draw a deep sigh.



"that be only too true. but I fear--indeed I fear--that you willn't

succeed in part they. there be a reason--I cannot mention it."



Northway be puzzle for a moment, then his face darken; he seem to

understand.



"I do so wish," pursue Mrs. Wade, with a smile of sympathy, "that I

could be of some use in this sad affair. my advice--I be afraid you

will be very unwilling to listen to it."



she pause, look at he wistfully.



"what would it be?" he ask.



"I feel so strongly--just as you do--that it be dreadful to have to

countenance such a state of thing; but I be convinced that it would be

very, very _unwise_ if you go _at once_ to extremity, Mr. Northway.

I be a woman of the world; I have see a good deal of life; if you

allow yourself to be guide by I, you would not regret it."



"you want to save your friend from the result of their behaviour," he

reply, uneasily.



"I assure you, it's not so much that--no, I have _your_ interest in

yourself what be the most prudent course that circumstance allow?"



"if it come to that, I can always get a divorce."



Mrs. Wade reflect, but with no sign of satisfaction.



"yes, that be open to you. you would then, of course, be enable to

marry again.--May I ask if you be quite at ease with regard to your

prospect in life?"



the tone be so delicately impertinent that Northway miss its

significance.



"I haven't quite decide upon anything yet."



"judge from your conversation, I should say that you will yet find a

place among active and successful man. but the beginning be everything.

if I could be of any assistance to you--I would put it to you frankly,

Mr. Northway: be it worth while sacrifice very solid possibility to

your--your affection for a woman who have desert you?"



he shuffle on the chair, click with his tongue, and look about he

undecidedly.



"I be not to be bribe to act against my conscience," he say at length.



Mrs. Wade hear this with pleasure. the blunt, half-bluster

declaration assure she that Northway's "conscience" be on the point

of surrender.



"now, let I tell you what I should like to do," she continue, bend

towards he. "will you allow I to go at once and see Mr. Quarrier?"



"and tell he?"



"yes, let he know what have happen. I quite understand," she add,

caressingly, "how very painful it would be for you to go directly to

he. will you allow I to be your intermediary? that you and he must

could explain to he your character, your natural delicacy, your

conscientiousness. I could make he understand that he have to meet a

person quite on his own level--an educate man of honourable feeling.

after that, an interview between you would be comparatively easy. I

should be really grateful to you if you would allow I to do you this

service."



Northway be like clay in her hand. every word have precisely the

effect on which she calculate. his forehead unwrinkle itself, his

Lilian, he begin to like the idea of face Quarrier--of exhibit his

natural delicacy, conscientiousness, and so on. something be in the

background, but of that he take no deliberate account.



a few minute more, and Mrs. Wade have he entirely at her disposal. it

be arrange that, whilst she go into the town to discover Quarrier,

Northway should remain on guard, either in or about the cottage.

Luncheon would be provide for he. he promise not to molest Lilian,

on condition that she make no attempt to escape.



"she will stay where she be," Mrs. Wade assure he. "your natural

delicacy will, I be sure, prevent you from seek to hold conversation

with she. she be very weak, poor thing! I do hope no serious illness

will follow on this shock."



thereupon she return to the sitting-room, where Lilian stand in an

anguish of impatience.



"I think I shall manage it, dear," she whisper, in a tone of

affectionate encouragement. "he have consent to see Mr. Quarrier,

provide I go first and break the news."



"you, Mrs. Wade? you be go to see Denzil?"



"dearest girl, leave it all in my hand. you cannot think what

difficulty I have overcome. if I be allow to act freely, I shall

save you and he."



she explain the article of truce, lilian listen with distressful

hope.



"and I don't think he will interfere with you meanwhile. but you can

keep the door lock, you know. Annie shall bring you something to eat;

I will tell she to give he _his_ luncheon first, and then to come very

quietly with your. it be half-past twelve. I can hardly be back in

less than an hour and a half. no doubt, Mr. Quarrier will come with I."



"how good you be, dear Mrs. Wade! oh, if you can save he!"



"trust I, and try to sit quietly. now, I will be off at once."



she press the hand that be hold to she, nod, and leave the room.









CHAPTER XXII





it be strike one when Mrs. Wade come in sight of the Quarriers'

house. at this hour Quarrier be expect at home for luncheon. he

arrive whilst the visitor still wait for an answer to her ring at

the door.



"but haven't you see lily? she tell me"----



"yes, I have see she. she be at the cottage."



a peculiarity in her tone arrest his attention, and the look of

joyous excitement which have be fix upon his face these last few

day change to anxious inquiry.



"what's the matter?"



"she be quite well--don't imagine accident. but I must speak to you in

private."



the door have open. Denzil lead straightway to the library, where he

fling aside hat and overcoat.



"what be it, Mrs. Wade?"



she stand close before he, her eye on his. the rapid walk have bring

colour to her cheek, and perhaps to the same cause be attributable she

quickened breathing.



"Lily have be discover by an enemy of her and your. a man name

Northway."



"damnation!"



he feel far too strongly to moderate his utterance out of regard for

the listener. his feature be distort; he stare wrathfully.



"and you have leave she with he? where be she?"



"she be quite safe in my sitting-room--the key turn to protect she.

he, too, be in the house, in another room. I have gain time; I"----



he could not listen.



"how do it happen?--You have no right to leave she alone with him!--How

have he find she?"



"please don't eat I up, Mr. Quarrier I have be do my very good

for you."



and she tell he the story of the morning as briefly as possible. she

endeavour to keep a tone of perfect equanimity fail in the course of

the narrative; once or twice there be a catching in her breath, and,

as if annoy with herself, she make an impatient gesture.



"and this fellow," cry Quarrier, when she cease, "imago that I be

at his mercy! let he do what he like--let he go into the

market-place and shout his news!--We'll go back at once."



"you be prepare, then, to have this know all over Polterham?" Mrs.

Wade ask, look steadily at he.



"I don't care a jot! let the election go to the devil! do you think I

will submit Lily to a day of such torture? this very evening we go to

London. how do she bear it?"



"very well indeed."



"like a brave, good girl! do you think I would weigh the chance of

election against her misery?"



"it seem to I," be the cold answer, "that you have do so already."



"have she complain to you?"



"oh, no! but I understand now what always puzzle I. I understand

her"----



she check herself, and turn quietly from he. strategy must always

be liable to slip from one cause or another, and Mrs. Wade's prudence

have, for the moment, yield to her impulse.



"you think she have all along be unhappy?"



"no, nothing of the kind. but when we have be speak of the

position of woman--that kind of thing--I have notice something

strange--an anxiety. I be only go to say that, after having

succeed thus far, it seem a pity to lose everything when a little

prudence--"



she wave her hand.



"do you believe," Denzil ask, "that his story of find she by mere

chance be true?"



"Lilian tell I that only your most intimate friend share the secret."



"Glazzard? of course _he_ have nothing to do with it. but some one else

may have"----



he walk apart, brooding. Mrs. Wade seat herself, and become

thoughtful.



"what sort of a fellow be this?" Quarrier ask, of a sudden.



"it depend who be deal with he," she answer, meet his look

with eye full of sympathetic expression. "I read he at once, and

he would have take she away by force."

"Mr. Quarrier, do be calm, and let we talk before we go. she be quite

safe. and as for Northway, I be perfectly sure that you can keep he

"if you will consent to follow in the path I have prepare. I have

take no small trouble."



she look up at he and smile.



"you have behave like a true friend, Mrs. Wade--it be no more than I

should have expect of you. but what have you plan? think how this

secret have already spread--what hope be there of finally hush it up?

Glazzard and you would never breathe a syllable; but how, short of

manslaughter, could I assure the silence of a blackguard like this

the fool in play and novel, throw half my possession away, and

all in vain."



"pray remember," urge the other, "that this Northway be by no mean

the rascal of melodrama. he have just enough brain to make he

conceited, and be at the disposal of any one who play upon his

profit." she break off and seem to falter. "I think you willn't find

fault with I, Mr. Quarrier, for try to do this?"



"you do it in the friendly spirit."



"and not indiscreetly, I hope." she look at he for a moment, and

instance, I think if you offer to give he a good start in a
