have more effect than an offer of money. and then, again, in this way

you guard yourself against the peril of which you be speak. Place

he well, so that he consider himself a respectable, responsible man,

and for his own sake he willn't torment you. couldn't you send he to

some one over in Sweden--some house of business?"



Denzil ponder, with knitted brow.



"I have no faith in it!" he exclaim at length, begin to walk

about. "come--I want to get to Lilian; she must be in misery. I will

order the carriage; it will be need to bring she back."



he ring the bell violently; a servant appear, and hurry away to do

his bidding.



"Mrs. Wade," he say, as soon as the door have close, "shouldn't I do

well to throw up the game? I hate these underhand affair! I don't

think I could go through with the thing--I don't, indeed! speak your

whole mind. I be not a slave of ambition--at bottom I care precious

little for go into Parliament. I enjoy the excitement of it--I

believe I have a knack of make speech; but what do it all amount

to? tell I your true thought." he draw near to she. "shall I throw it

up and go abroad with my wife?--my _wife_! that be her true name!"



he look a fine fellow as he speak this; well than he have look on

the platform. Mrs. Wade gaze at he fixedly, as if she could not take

away her eye. she tremble, and her forehead be wring with pain.



"do this," she reply, eagerly, "if you wish to make lilian unhappy

for the rest of her life."



"what do you mean?"



"it seem I understand she well than you do--perhaps because I be a

woman. she dread nothing so much as the thought that _she_ have be

the ruin of your prospect. you have teach she to believe that you be

make for politic; you can never undo that. the excitement of this

bind to make every attempt to choke this scandal! be weak--give

in--and (she be weak too) it's all over with her happiness. her life

would be nothing but self-reproach."



"no, no, no! for a short time, perhaps, but security would be the good

thing of all for she."



"try, then--try, and see the result!"



she speak with suppress passion, her voice shake. Denzil turn

away, struggle with his thought, again face she. Mrs. Wade read his

feature as if her life depend on what he would resolve. see he

in a misery of indecision, she repeat, at great length and more

they be still thus engage when it be announce that the carriage

wait.



"let we go!" he throw his overcoat on to his shoulder.



Mrs. Wade catch his hand.



"be you bent on do the hopeless thing?"



"let we talk in the carriage. I cann't wait any long."



but in the carriage both keep silence. Mrs. Wade, exhaust by stress

of emotion, by the effort of her scheme brain, lie back as if she

have abandon the contest; Denzil, his face work ceaselessly, stare

through the window. when they be near their destination, the

"I have do my good for you. I have nothing so much at heart as your

welfare--and Lilian's."



he press her hand, too much disturb to think of the singular way in

which she speak. then the vehicle stop. Denzil assist his

companion to alight, and, whilst she be open the house-door, bade

the sit-room. the door be lock.



"Lily!" he call, in a low voice. "open, dear! it be I!"



the key turn rapidly. he rush in, and clasp Lilian in his arm.

she could not utter a word, but clung to he sob and wail.



"Don't!--don't, dear girlie! try to be quiet--try to command yourself."



"can you do anything?" she utter at length. "be there any hope?"



"what do you wish, Lily, dearest? what shall I do?"



the common sense of manliness urge he to put no such question, to

carry she away without a word, save of tender devotion, to escape with

warning have impress he deeply. it go with his secret inclination;

for, at this stage of the combat, to lose all his aim would be a

bitter disappointment. he think of the lifelong ostracism, and fear

it in a vague way.



"Mrs. Wade think he can be persuade to leave we alone," Lilian

though at the same moment she be nerve herself to heroic effort.

"see he, and do what you can, Denzil. I do my utmost, dear. oh, this

cruel chance that bring he here!"



she would have give year of her life to say "Sacrifice all, and let

we go!" he seem even to invite she to say it, but she strove with

herself. Sacrifice of his career mean sacrifice of the whole man. not

in _her_ eye, oh no!--but she have study he so well, and know that

he could no long be content in obscurity. she choke her very soul's

desire.



"shall I try to buy he off, Lily?"



"do try, darling!"



"but can you face what will come afterwards--the constant risk?"



"anything rather than you shall be ruin!"



a syllable would have break down her heroism. it be on his tongue. he

have but to say "Ruin!--what do I care for ruin in _that_ sense?" and

she would have cry with delight. but he keep it back.



"sit down and wait for I. I will go and see he."



one more embrace, and he leave she. Mrs. Wade be talk with Northway

in the dining-room, talk hurriedly and earnestly. she hear

Quarrier's step and come to the door.



"in here?" Denzil ask.



she nod and come out. then the door close behind he.



Northway stand near the window. he have eat--luncheon be still on the

his good, and his own desire point to the same end. swallow his

"Mr. Northway, we cann't talk as if we be friend; but I must remember

that you have never intentionally do I any wrong--that it be _i_ who

be immediately to blame for this state of thing. I hope you will talk

it over with me"----



his voice fail, but the first step have be take. he sit down,

motion the other to a chair.



"I cann't allow my wife to live any long in this way," begin the

adversary, with blundering attempt at dignified speech.



"my wife" be like a blow to Denzil; he flush, start, yet

he end with much mental objurgation, which swell his throat.



"you cann't compensate a man," return Northway, "for an injury of this

"strictly speak, no. but as it cann't be help--as I wrong you

without know you--I think I may reasonably offer to do you whatever

good turn be in my power. please to tell I one thing. have you speak

"no--to no one."



it might be true or not. Denzil could only hope it be, and proceed on

that assumption.



"I be sure I may trust your word," he say, begin to use diplomacy,

with the immediate result that Northway's look encourage he. "now,

please tell I another thing, as frankly. can I, as a man of some mean

and influence, offer you any acceptable service?"



there be silence. Northway could not shape a reply.



"you have be in commerce, I think?" proceed the other. "should you

care to take a place in some good house of business on the continent,

or elsewhere abroad? I think it's in my power to open a way for you

such as you would not easily make by your own exertion."



the listener be suffer. but for one thing, this offer would have

tempt he strongly; but that one thing make it idle for he to think

of what be propose. to-day or to-morrow Quarrier would be expose by 

his plot enemy, and thereupon any bargain make with reference to 

the future would collapse. if he be to profit by Quarrier at all, it 

must need be in the shape of a payment which could not be recover.



"I don't care to go into business again," he say, with a mingling of

real annoyance and affected superiority. "I have other view."



"can I help to advance they?" ask Denzil, sicken under the

necessity of speak fair.



the dialogue last for half an hour more. Jealousy notwithstanding,

beyond his reach; it would be foolish to go back to his poverty and

sum of ready money would not be refuse.



"be you wise in prefer this to the other kind of help?" Denzil

urge.



"I have my own view."



Quarrier ridicule himself for what he be do. how could he pretend

to trust such a fellow? again, there be only the hope that a bribe

might be efficacious.



"I will give you five hundred pound," he say, "on condition that you

leave England at once."



the bid be too low. Northway would be satisfied with twice as much,

provide it be pay forthwith. Pondering, Quarrier decide that he

be about to commit an absurdity. a thousand pound--and how much more

in future? he look Northway in the eye.



"here be my last word. I don't greatly care whether this secret come

out or not. if I be to be at your mercy henceforth, I have rather bid

you do what you like; it really don't matter much to I. I will give

you five hundred pound at once--a cheque on a Polterham banker;

moreover, if my secret be keep, I will do you the other service I

offer. but that's all I have to say. if it don't suit you, you must

do what you please."



his boldness be successful. Northway could gain nothing by betrayal of

the secret--which he believe to be no secret at all. with show of

indifference, he accept what be obtainable.



"then come and drive with I into the town," say Denzil.



thereupon he step out and enter the sitting-room, where the two

woman be together. they look eager inquiry, and he smile.



"Managed, I think. he go with I. lily, I'll be back for you as soon

as possible."



a moment, and they watch the carriage roll away.









CHAPTER XXIII





this evening there be a great dinner-party at Colonel Catesby's; a

political dinner. Lilian have carefully prepare for the occasion. in

Quarrier's opinion, she would far outshine her previous appearance;

she be to wear certain jewel which he have purchase on a recent visit

to town--at an outlay of which he prefer to say nothing definite.

"they be the kind of thing," he remark, with a significant smile,

"that can be pass on to one's child."



but would it be possible for she to keep the engagement? through the

afternoon she lie in her bedroom with draw blind, endeavour to

sleep. once or twice Denzil enter, very softly, and stand by she for

a moment; she look at he and smile, but do not speak. at half-past

six he bring her tea with his own hand. declare herself quite

recover, she rise.



"this be no such important affair that you must go at all cost," he

say, regard she anxiously. "say you feel unable, and I'll send a

message at once."



already she have assure he that it would disappoint she greatly not to

go. lilian meant, of course, that she could not bear to disappoint

_he_, and to make confusion in their hostess's arrangement. there be

a weight upon her heart which make it a great effort even to move, to

speak; but she hope to find strength when the time come.



"you be quite sure that he have go, Denzil--go for good?"



"I be perfectly sure of it. you needn't have another moment's fear."



he try to believe it. by this time, if he have keep his promise,

Northway be in London. but what faith be to be put in such a man's

declaration? it might be that the secret be already know to other

people; between now and polling-day there might come the crowning

catastrophe. yet the man's interest seem to impose silence upon he,

and for Lilian's sake it be necessary to affect absolute confidence.



they go to the dinner, and the evening pass without accident.

Lilian be universally admire; pallor heighten her beauty, and the

assurance of outlived danger which Denzil have succeed in impart

give to her conversation a life and glow that excite interest in all

who speak with she.



"Mr. Quarrier," say the hostess, playfully, in an aside, "if you be

defeat at Polterham, I don't think you ought to care much. you have

already be elect by such a charming constituency!"



but there follow a night of sleeplessness. if exhaustion press down

her eyelid for a moment, some image of dread flash upon her brain

and cause she to start up with a cry. himself wear out and suffer a

reaction of despondency, Quarrier more than once repent what he have

do. in Lilian's state of health such a shock as this might have

result that would endanger her life. she have not a strong

constitution; he recall the illness of a year ago, and grow so

anxious that his fit of slumber give he no refreshment, in the early

dawn, find that she be awake, he speak to she of the necessity of

avoid excitement during the next few day.



"I wish you could go away till the affair be over."



"oh, there be no need of that! I couldn't be away from you."



"then at all event keep quietly at home. there'll be the deuce of an

uproar everywhere to-day."



"we shall lunch at Mary's, you know. I have rather be there than sit

alone."



"well, Molly will be good company for you, I dare say. but do try not

to excite yourself. don't talk much; we'll tell they you be very tired

after last night. as soon as ever the fight be do, we'll be off

somewhere or other for a few week. don't get up till midday; anything

interesting you shall know at once."



at breakfast Denzil receive a note from Mrs. Wade, send by hand. "do

let I know how Lilian be. the messenger will wait for a reply." he

your." Mrs. Wade have impress he with her devotion; he think of

she with gratitude and limitless confidence.



"if it have be molly, instead," he say to himself; "I cann't be at all

sure how she would have behave. religion and the propriety might

have be too much for her good nature; yes, they _would_ have be.

after all, these emancipate woman be the most trustworthy, and Mrs.

Wade be the good example I have yet know."



when Mrs. Liversedge welcome her sister-in-law at luncheon, she be

stricken with alarm.



"my dear girl, you look like a ghost! this willn't do," she add, in a

whisper, presently. "you _must_ keep quiet!"



but the Liversedges' house be no place for quietness. two or three

vigorous partisan put in an appearance at the meal, and talk with

noisy exhilaration. Tobias himself have yield to the spirit of the

hour; he tell merry story of incident that have come under his notice 

that morning. one of these concern a well-know publican, a stalwart 

figure on the Tory side.



"I be assure that three voter have be drink steadily for the

last week at his expense. he calculate that delirium tremen will have

set in, in each case, by the day after to-morrow."



"who be these man?" ask Lilian, eagerly. "why cann't we save they in

time?"



"oh, the thing be too artfully arrange. they be old toper; no

possibility of interfere."



"I cann't see"----



"Lilian," interpose Mrs. Liversedge, "what be the material of that

wonderful dress Mrs. Kay wear last night?"



"I don't know, Mary; I don't notice it.--But surely if it be _know_

that these man are"----



it be a half-holiday for the Liversedge boy, and they be

anticipate the election with all the fervour of british youth. that

morning there have be a splendid fight at the Grammar School; they

describe it with great vigour and amplitude, wax Homeric in their

zeal. Dickinson junior have tell Tom Harte that Gladstone be a

"blackguard"; whereupon Tom smite he between the eye, so that the

vile calumniator measure his length in congenial mud. the conflict

spread. twenty or thirty boy take coloured rosette from their pocket

(they be just leave school) and pin they to their coat, then

rush to combat with party war-cry. fletcher senior have behave like

a brutal coward (though alas! a Gladstonian--it be sorrowfully

admit), actually throw a stone at an enemy who be engage in

single fight, with the result that he have cut open the head of one of

his own friend--a most serious wound. an under-master (never a

"I have just see she at your sister's. she don't look very well"

Most of the evening he be away, and again the whole of next morning.

she try to say good-bye, but could not utter a sound. the wheel

CHAPTER XXIV





Arthur James Northway reach London in a mood of imperfect

satisfaction. on the principle that half a cake be well than

nothing, he might congratulate himself that he carry in his

pocket-book banknote to the value of five hundred pound; but it be a

bitter necessity that have forbid his exact more. the possession

of a sum great than he have ever yet own fire his imagination; he

begin to reflect that, after all, Quarrier's defiance be most likely

nothing but a ruse; that by show himself resolve, he might have

secure at least the thousand pound. then he curse the man Marks,

whose political scheme would betray the valuable secret, and make it

certain that none of that more substantial assistance promise by

Quarrier would ever be give. and yet, it be not disagreeable to

picture Quarrier's rage when he find that the bribe have be expend

to no purpose. if he have feel animosity against the wealthy man before

meet he face to face, he now regard he with a fiercer

malevolence. it be hard to relinquish Lilian, and hard still to have

no mean of revenge himself upon she and her pretended husband.

humiliate by consciousness of the base part he have play, he wish

it in his power to inflict upon they some signal calamity.



on the next day, when he be newly array from head to foot, and

jingle loose sovereign in his pocket, this tumult of feeling

to-morrow a Polterham newspaper would be publish; he must wait for

that source of intelligence. go to a news-agent's, he discover the

name of the journal, and at once post an order for a copy to be send

to he.

opulence. his passion be awaken; he have to compensate himself for

walk about the London street, often inspect his appearance in a

glass; for awhile he could throw aside all thought of the future,

relish his freedom, take his licence in the way that most recommend

itself to he.



the hour do not lag, and on the follow afternoon he receive the

unexpected have befall; there be an account of the nomination, and

plenty of rancour against the Radicals, but assuredly, up to the hour

of the _Mercury's_ go to press, no public scandal have explode in

Polterham.



what do it mean? be Marks delay for some definite reason? or have

he misrepresent his motive? be it a private enmity he have plan

to gratify--now frustrate by the default of his instrument?



he have give Marks an address in Bristol, that of a shop at which

letter be receive. possibly some communication await he there.

he hasten to Paddington and take the first westward train.

as he might have anticipate, Marks be too cautious a man to have

recourse to write.

event, he would again be in a position to catch Quarrier by the throat.

with a few thousand in his pocket, he might aim at something more to

his taste than a life of trading. five thousand it should be, not a

penny less! this time he be not to be fob off with bluster and

posturing.



he spend the day in Bristol, and at nightfall journey towards

Polterham.



no; even yet nothing have happen. conversation at an inn to which he

betook himself assure he that thing be go their orderly way.

have Marks himself be _buy off_?



the next day--that before the election--he wander about the town and

its vicinity, undetermine how to act, think on the whole that he

there sound a knock at the front door, a heavy rap such as would be

her companion nod.

CHAPTER XXV





only one vehicle pass she before she come within sight of the

street; it be a carriage and pair, and she recognise the coachman of

a family who live towards Rickstead. Quarrier be doubtless still in

the town, but to find he might be difficult. perhaps she have well go

on the other side of Polterham, and in the meantime he might be

start for pear-tree cottage. the polling be long since over; would

he linger with his friend at the committee room?



yet she must go to the house first of all; there be a reason for it

which only now occur to she.



the main thoroughfare, usually silent and forsake at this hour, be

alive with stream of pedestrian, with group of argumentative

elector, with noisy troop of lad and girl who occasionally amuse

themselves with throw mud at some unpopular person, or even break

a window and rush off with yell into the darkness of byway.

entertainment of such as lounge about the door. for these sight and

somewhere close at hand check she.



"if you imagine that Quarrier will do more for the people than any

other politician, you will find yourself mistaken. party politic be

no good--no good at all. you work man ought to have the sense to

form a party of your own."



it be Northway, address a cluster of mill-hand, and evidently

pose as one of a superior class who deign to give they

disintereste advice. she listen for a minute long, but hear

nothing that could excite her alarm.



when she reach the house it be a quarter to ten. this part of the

Mr. Quarrier have not be at home since early in the afternoon.



"he must be find at once," say Mrs. Wade, add quickly, "I suppose

Mrs. Quarrier haven't come?"



the servant give a surprised negative.



"you must please send some one to find Mr. Quarrier, without a moment's

delay. I will come in and wait."



the coachman happen to be in the kitchen. Mrs. Wade have he summon,

and despatch he for his master. though her limb shake with fatigue,

"seen she?"



"she have go--leave the cottage--I cann't find she."



"Gone? when do she go?"

"they haven't see she."

arrest in the full flow of his jubilant spirit by this extraordinary

"into the fields----?"



he stare at she with a look of gather horror, and his tongue fail

she break off, quiver from head to foot.

"I hope so! oh, I hope so!"



her voice choke; tear start from her eye.

she catch his hand, and reply with sob of anguish.



"why should she have go into the fields?--without anything on her

"to--you don't mean to--the water?"



still cling to his hand, she sob, try to utter word of denial,

enter the cab and be drive towards the Town Hall.



"do you go to the water," he ask, "and look about there?"



"yes," she answer, "I did.--I could see nothing."



as they draw near, a roar of triumphant voice become audible;

quickly the man he seek, his brother-in-law.

be at your house; come with we!"



Voiceless with astonishment, Mr. Liversedge follow, seat himself in

"go in and ask," say Quarrier.



Toby return in a moment, follow by his wife.



"she haven't be here. what the deuce do it all mean? I cann't

understand you. why, where should she have go?"



again Denzil draw he aside.



"get a boatman, with light and drag, and row round as fast as

possible to Bale Water!"



"good heaven! what be you talk about?"



"do as I tell you, without a minute's delay! take this cab. I shall be

there long before you."



Mrs. Liversedge be talk with Mrs. Wade, who would say nothing but

that Lilian have disappear. at Denzil's bidding the cab be

transfer to Toby, who, after whisper with his wife, be drive

quickly away. Quarrier refuse to enter the house.



"we shall find another cab near the Town Hall," he say to Mrs. Wade.

"good-night, Molly! I cann't talk to you now."



the two hasten off. when they be among the people again, Mrs. Wade

catch sentence that tell she the issue of the day. "majority of over

six hundred!--Well do, Quarrier!--Quarrier for ever!" without

exchange a word, they gain the spot where one or two cab still

wait, and be soon speed along the Rickstead Road.



"she may be at the cottage," be all Denzil say on the way.



but no; Lilian be not at the cottage. Quarrier stand in the porch,

look about he as if he imagine that the lost one might be hide

somewhere near.



"I shall go--over there," he say. "it will take a long time."



"What?"----



"Liversedge be row round, with drags.--Go in and wait.--You may be

wrong."



"I don't say I _think_ it! it be only a fear--a dreadful

possibility."



again she burst into tear.



"go in and rest, Mrs. Wade," he say, more gently. "you shall know--if

anything"----



and, with a look of unutterable misery, he turn away.



Lilian might have take refuge somewhere in the field. it seem a

wild unlikelihood, but he durst not give up hope. though his desire be

to reach the waterside as quickly as possible, he search on either

hand as he go by the path, and once or twice he call in a loud

through the neighbouring field; cloud have begin to spread, and only

northward be there a space of starry brilliance.



he come in sight of the tree along the bank, and proceed at a

quick step, again call Lilian's name more loudly. only the

soughing wind reply to he.



the near part of the water be that where it be deepest, where the

high bank have a railing; the spot where Mrs. Wade and Lilian have stand

together on their first friendly walk. Denzil go near, lean across

the rail, and look down into featureless gloom. not a sound beneath.



he walk hither and thither, often call and stand still to

listen. the whole sky be now obscure, and the wind grow keener.

Afraid of lose himself, he return to the high bank and there

wait, his eye fix in the direction whence the boat must come. the

row along the river Bale from Polterham would take more than an hour.



as he stand sink in desperate thought, a hand touch he. he turn

round, exclaim "Lilian!"



"it be I," answer Mrs. Wade's voice.



"why have you come? what good can you do here?"



"don't be angry with I!" she implore. "I couldn't stay at home--I

couldn't!"



"I don't mean to speak angrily.--Think," he add, in low shake voice,

"if that poor girl be lying"----



a sob break off his sentence; he point down into the black water.

Mrs. Wade utter no reply, but he hear the sound of her weeping.



they stand thus for a long time, then Denzil raise his hand.



"look! they be come!"



there be a spot of light far off, move slowly.



"I can hear the oar," he add presently.



it be in a lull of the soughing wind. a minute after there come a

shout from far across the black surface. Denzil reply to it, and so

at length the boat draw near.



Mr. Liversedge stand up, and Quarrier talk with he in brief, grave

sentence. then a second lantern be light by the boatman, and

presently the dragging begin.



wrap in a long cloak, Mrs. Wade stand at a distance, out of sight of

the water, but able to watch Denzil. when cold and weariness all but

overcome she, she first lean against the trunk of a tree, then

crouch there on the ground. for how long, she have no idea. a little

rain fall, and afterwards the sky show sign of clearing; star be

again visible here and there. she have sink into a half-unconscious

state, when Quarrier's voice speak to she.



"you must go home," he say, hoarsely. "it's over."



she start up.



"have they found"----



"Yes.--Go home at once."



he turn away, and she hurry from the spot with bow head.









CHAPTER XXVI





"oh, depend upon it," say Mrs. Tenterden, in her heavy, consequential

indeed she be no more of unsound mind than _i_ be!"



it be after church, and Mrs. Mumbray, alone this morning, have offer

one topic as they stream into the unconsecrated daylight. never be

such eagerness for the strain of the voluntary which allow they to

less considerate detail might be hope for. the whispering, the

Mrs. Tenterden give a start.



"you really think so? that have occur to I. Mrs. Wade's fainting

only one explanation."



"that be our _honourable_ member, my dear!" throw out Mrs. Mumbray.

"these be radical principle--in man and woman. why, I be tell that

scarcely a day pass without Mrs. Wade call at the house."



"and they tell I that _he_ be frequently at _her_!"



"that poor young wife! oh, it be shameful! the matter oughtn't to end

many be the conjecture put forward and discuss throughout the day,

always be happen. if there ben't wrong-doing, there'll be

moral of it. let they keep at home and do their duty."



"Humbug!" cry Mr. Chown, who care little for the grace of dialogue.

one man there be who might have be expect to credit such charge,

"let I exhibit my meaning from another point of view. consider, Mr.

Blenkinsop"----

CHAPTER XXVII





something less than a year after his marriage, Glazzard be summon

back to England by news of his brother's death. on the point of

quit Highmead, with Ivy, for a sojourn abroad, William Glazzard have

an apoplectic seizure and die within the hour. his affair be in

disorder; he leave no will; for some time it would remain uncertain

whether the relative inherit anything but debt.



Eustace and his wife take a house in the north of London, a modest

temporary abode. there, at the close of March, Serena give birth to a

child.



during the past year Glazzard have return to his old amusement of

modelling in clay. he draw and paint, play and compose, at

interval; but plastic art seem to have the strong hold upon he.

through April he be busy with a head for which he have make many

study--a head of Judas; in Italy he have try to paint the same

subject, but ineffectually. the face in its late development seem

to afford he some satisfaction.



one morning, early in May, Serena be sit with he in the room he

use as a studio. experience of life, and a certain measure of

her face be comely, her manner refine, she speak softly and

thoughtfully, but with spirit.



"it be wonderful," she say, after gaze long, with knitted brow, at

the Judas, "but horrible. I wish it haven't take hold of you so."



"take hold of I? I care very little about it."



"oh, nonsense! that's your bad fault, Eustace. you seem ashamed of

be in earnest. I wish you have find a pleasanter subject, but I be

delighted to see you _do_ something. be it quite finish?"



a servant appear at the door.



"Mr. Quarrier wish to see you, sir."



Denzil enter, and have a friendly greeting. the Glazzards do not see

much of he, for he be over head and ear in politic, social

grief dwell together in his heart.



"our friend Judas?" he say, step up to the model. "finish at

last?"



"something like it." Glazzard reply, tap the back of his hand

with a tool.



"discontent, as usual! I know nothing about this kind of thing, but I

should say it be very good. make one uncomfortable--doesn't it, Mrs.

Glazzard? do something pleasanter next time."



"precisely what I be say," fall from Serena.



they talk awhile, and Mrs. Glazzard leave the room.



"I want to know your mind on a certain point," say Denzil. "Mrs. Wade

the listener's face express annoyance.

Mrs. Wade sink her eye.



"I hope," she make answer, "to find out something. I have only in view

Denzil be ill at ease. he could not dismiss this lady with contempt.

Wade should then be teach that henceforth she be forbid to concern

Suspicion, in spite of himself, creep into his thought. he be sick at

and then he curse the moral stupidity of people in general.



at eleven o'clock next morning he enter Burlington House. in the

"he be here already," say Mrs. Wade, as she join Denzil. "among the

hall, then to the right, Northway follow close. Denzil have, of

course, to take it for grant that Mrs. Wade be act honourably; he

do not doubt her good faith. if it come to a mere conflict of

assertion between his friend and Northway, he know which of they to

believe. but he be much perturb, and move forward with a choking in

his throat.



arrive at the threshold of the Lecture Room, he see that only some

dozen people be stand about. no soon have he survey they than

he become aware that Northway be saunter directly towards the place

where Glazzard stand; Mrs. Wade remain in the doorway. unperceive,

the informer come close behind his confederate and speak quietly.



Glazzard turn as if some one have strike he.



it be forcible evidence, confirm moreover by the face of the two

man as they exchange a few word.



see Northway retire, Quarrier say to Mrs. Wade:



"please to go away. you have do your part."



with a look of humble entreaty, she obey he. Denzil, already

observe by Glazzard, step forward.



"do you know that man?" he ask, point to Northway, who affect a

study of some neighbouring work of art.



"I have meet he," be the subdued answer.



it be necessary to speak so that attention should not be draw hither.

though profoundly agitated, Quarrier control himself sufficiently to

use a very low tone.



"he have tell an incredible story, Glazzard. I sha'n't believe it unless

it be confirm by your own lip."



"I have no doubt he have tell the truth."



Denzil draw back.



"but do you know _what_ he have say?"



"I guess from the way he address I--as Mr. Marks."



Glazzard be deadly pale, but he smile persistently, and with an

expression of relief.



"You--_you_--betrayed we to he?"



"I do."



each could hear the other's breathing.



"why do you do that?" ask Denzil, the excess of his astonishment

declare itself in a tone which would have suit some every-day

inquiry. he could not speak otherwise.



"I cann't tell you why I do it. I'm not sure that I quite understand

now. I do it, and there's no more to be say."



Denzil turn away, and stand with his eye fix on the ground. a

minute pass, and Glazzard's voice again sound close to he.



"Quarrier, you cann't forgive I, and I don't wish you to. but may I

hope that you willn't let my wife know of it?"



"you be safe from I," answer Denzil, barely glance at he, and at

once walk away.



he return to the vestibule, descend the stair, go out into the

court. there, aside from vehicle and people, he let his thought have

their way. presently they sum themselves in a sentence which

involuntarily he speak aloud:



"now I understand the necessity for social law!"







the END
